NORTHEAST BRAZIL
RIO GRANDE DO NORTE RURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
SUMMARY
(i)       Among the 30 million people in the Northeast of Brazil some 60%
live in mainly semi-arid areas of generally poor soils and subject to
periodic devastating droughts with accompanying high risks to farmers.
Land ownership is highly concentrated and many of the rural poor are
landless, often working in some form of sharecropping. The rural per
capita income in 1974 was less than US$225 as compared with a national
average per capita income of US$815. Government's earlier attempt to
improve incomes in the Northeast was largely through industrialization
(supported by fiscal and monetary incentives) and various social programs.
However, programs supporting small-scale agriculture have not been devel-
oped, but Government's focus is now shifting towards improving rural pro-
ductivity and incomes through a new program, POLONORDESTE, involving fed-
eral funding of US$700 million over the period 1975-79. The project would
be the first developed under the POLONORDESTE program.
(ii)      The project was prepared by technicians employed by the State of
Rio Grande do Norte (RN) with assistance from the Federal Government, the
Regional Development Agency (SUDENE) and the Bank. The five-year project,
located in an area of unreliable and marginal rainfall, seeks to improve the
agricultural production of some 15,000 smallholder famiilies. Existing insti-
tutional arrangements make it difficult for these farmers to obtain access
to credit, farming inputs and agricultural advice. The project would also
expand an existing community health program.  As the technical packages and
input delivery systems for improved agricultural production require further
refinement before being universally adopted, the project would be implemented
in two phases. The first phase, over about a three-year period, would largely
be devoted to experimentation and would involve only 20% of the project area.
The second phase, over about a two-year period, would be subject to satisfac-
tory progress in the first phase.
(iii)     The project would consist of:
(a) Extension service improvement, mainly by the addition of
agricultural advisers especially trained to focus on small
farmers' problems and the provision of logistic support;
and through increased numbers of social extension workers;
(b) Credit to small farmers for seasonal production purposes
and for on-farm investments and area expansion; alternative
delivery systems would be tested, and simplified credit proce-
dures developed to improve small farm families' access to
credit; credit insurance would be made available to small
farmers to decrease borrowers' risks;



- ii -
(c) Experimentation and farm trials to improve technical
packages for cotton and other crops focusing on the
practical needs of small farmers;
(d) Health program expansion and strengthening of supervision
and referral systems;
(e) Training of project staff and development of training
materials for farmers;
(f) Organizational support for project administration,
monitoring and evaluation, and a project special
fund; and
(g) Preparation of future rural development projects in
other areas of the State.
(iv)      The project would be implemented by existing State-level agencies
under annual plans approved by the Bank. A Project Unit located within the
State-level agricultural planning commission (CEPA/RN) would plan and coor-
dinate work of the participating agencies and control project finances. It
would have a small staff and would report periodically through a State agri-
cultural policy coordinating body to the Special Coordinating Group of POLO-
NORDESTE. A separate Monitoring Group would monitor and evaluate the proj-
ect.
(v)       A Bank loan of US$12 million would cover the foreign exchange compo-
nent of the project (US$3.8 million) and 31% of local costs, over a 5-year
disbursement period. This would represent 40% of total project cost of
US$30.0 million. Much of the project would involve staff costs and train-
ing, research and technical assistance, and only relatively small amounts
would be for construction or purchase of goods. Local health structures
(totalling about US$0.6 million) would be small, of varied design and
geographically dispersed, and would be constructed by a combination of
self-help, force account and local bidding. Purchases of equipment,
including small vehicles which are produced locally and readily avail-
able, would be bulked where practicable and would be made under local
competitive bidding procedures. Funds for agricultural credit would be
made available by Government to participating banks under agreements satis-
factory to the Bank and would be on-lent to farmers for seasonal production
up to a maximum of US$1,250 equivalent per farmer and for on-farm investment
up to a-maximum total indebtedness of US$2,500 per farmer. Government has
established interest rates to farmers of 7% for medium and long term credit,
and 13% for seasonal credit, excluding certain inputs (mainly pesticides)
which are made available interest free. These rates are negative in real
terms; however, most of the beneficiaries would be from the lowest income
groups and the strengthened extension service would help guard against mis-
use of funds.



- iii -
(vi)      As a result of the project, production of food crops and cotton
would be increased. Production of perennial cotton is expected to increase
by some 16,000 tons of seedcotton and would be marketed through existing
channels. Yields of food crops (maize, beans and sorghum) would be in-
creased through wider use of improved varieties and cultivation practices.
Net family income from cotton (representing at least 70% of total family
income) of farms under 50 ha would be expected to increase from US$210
before development to US$430 at full development (year 6); family net
income from cotton of farms from 50 ha to 200 ha would increase from
US$590 to US$1,200. The project would provide, through crop intensifi-
cation, improved employment opportunities which would amount to some 2,200
man-years annually.
(vii)     The estimated rate of return to the economy from the directly
quantifiable aspects of the project would be 15%. The project is sensitive
to yield changes, a 10% drop in yield reducing the rate of return by 6 per-
centage points. Additional to the quantifiable benefits from increased crop
production are those associated with improved health and other social serv-
ices and community and self-help project activities.
(viii)    The difficult physical environment of the project area and the
interrelated and not fully refined nature of actions under the project,
together with difficulties in estimating farmer response, give the project
a greater than usual degree of risk. Steps have been taken to reduce some
of the risks of low farmer response by improving credit delivery systems
and assuring that the PROAGRO voluntary credit insurance program is avail-
able to borrowers under the project. Progress regarding on-lending to
small farmers will be evaluated annually and additional measures intro-
duced if necessary. The project has the potential to reach some 75,000
people with its directly productive components and 220,000 with improved
health facilities. More importantly, it would provide a replicable approach
to small-farmer problems in other parts of the Northeast.
(ix)      The project would be suitable for a Bank loan of US$12 million
equivalent with a term of 25 years including 5 years' grace.






I. INTRODUCTION
1.01      The Government of Brazil is concerned about improving the incomes
and welfare of its poorest people, many of whom are concentrated in the rural
areas of the Northeast. The improvement is being sought through expanding
past programs and through launching new programs in the economic and social
fields, such as the recently-launched POLONORDESTE program, a special devel-
opment fund for the Northeast.
1.02      POLONORDESTE was created on October 30, 1974 as an effort to reduce
rural income disparities between the Northeast and the rest of Brazil, with
funds equivalent to over US$700 million for 1975-79. Government identified
28 sub-regions covering large portions of the Northeast on which POLONORDESTE
would focus initially. Consultants have been appointed to prepare projects
for some of these while others will be prepared by the states. The Government
intends to present some of these to the Bank and the Inter-American Devel-
opment Bank for possible financing. Projects will likely be implemented
by existing agencies coordinated by local (usually state-level) agencies
reporting to a regional POLONORDESTE coordinating group within the Federal
Development Agency for the Northeast (SUDENE) and to an interministerial
coordinating group at the Federal level.
1.03      The Government has requested IBRD assistance for a rural development
project in the State of Rio Grande do Norte (RN), the first of the projects
to be implemented under POLONORDESTE. A number of visits of Bank staff and
consultants assisted the formulation and preparation of this project. The
project was prepared by technicians at the State level and provides one way
for mobilizing State, Regional and Federal resources to prepare rural develop-
ment projects in a context where the traditional tendency had been for a high
degree of centralization at the Federal level. Other state-level teams are
now preparing rural development projects in other Northeast states and the
Bank is maintaining a close contact with these efforts. Appraisal was in
November/December 1974, with a follow-up mission in April 1975. A separate
rural education project in the state is now being prepared through studies
financed under the Bank's Second Education Loan (1067-BR).
1.04      The project is probably more risky than many rural development
projects. It must be viewed in the context of the difficult ecological,
institutional and social situation of the Northeast, as detailed in "Rural
Development Issues and Options in Northeast Brazil" (Report No. 665a-BR
dated June 23, 1975), and as a first slice of what could become a larger
effort at dealing with rural poverty in the Northeast.
1.05      Six IBRD loans totalling US$180 million have been previously
approved for agriculture and rural development in Brazil. Two loans (the
US$6.7 million Alto Turi settlement project and the US$23 million Lower
Sao Francisco Project) were in the Northeast. The start of execution of
the Alto Turi Land Settlement Project was delayed due to administrative
difficulties. Settlement, however, has begun and progress is now satis-
factory. The Lower Sao Francisco Project is not yet effective.



- 2 -
II.  THE NORTHEAST -/
General
2.01      The nine-state Northeast of Brazil, with a population of some 30
million is one of the principal poverty areas of South America. Some 60%
of the population lives in rural areas mainly engaged in low-productivity
agriculture. Apart from a relatively narrow humid coastal strip in which
sugarcane is the principal crop, much of the Northeast has a semi-arid
climate, with periodic devastating droughts (and resulting high risks to
farmers) and poor soils. Land ownership is highly skewed with a relatively
small number of large estates owning a large portion of total land (less
than 1% of establishments own 40% of the area). While varying considerably
within the region, in some areas up to 80% of the rural labor force owns no
land at all. Many of the landless work under some form of often tenuous
sharecropping arrangement. Despite many Government programs, the rural
per capita income of the Northeast, at less than US$225 in 1974, is still
less than one-third of national per capita income of US$815.
Government Policy
2.02      Government has long been concerned with the slow economic develop-
ment of the Northeast. Both the Federal development agency for the North-
east (SUDENE) and Bank of the Northeast (BNB) were created to stimulate
Northeast development. In recent years, there have also been substantial
fiscal and monetary incentives (the latter through subsidized credit under
PROTERRA, a federal program) for both industry and agriculture. Neverthe-
less, per capita incomes for the region as a whole remain at half the national
average.
2.03      Government's past policies to solve the poverty problems of the
rural Northeast were mainly oriented to (a) job-creation by the industrial
sector and establishment of agro-industries and (b) providing social serv-
ices. Also, efforts were made in some drought-prone areas to develop small
dams and ponds. Despite the region's plentiful labor and apparent limited
economies of scale in agriculture, programs supporting labor-intensive small-
scale agriculture have not so far been developed. A "big-farm mentality" has
tended to permeate the extension service, credit agencies and research endeav-
ors.
2.04      Government is now moving toward supporting smallholder agriculture,
supported by evidence that small-holders, if properly serviced, can be more
productive than large farmers. This new approach involves the need to re-
orient the thinking and focus of existing institutions. The permanent capac-
ity to create well-conceived credit and extension programs and technical pack-
ages for small producers in particular circumstances must be developed.
1/   Readers are directed to "Rural Development Issues and Options in North-
east Brazil" (Report No. 665a-BR dated June 23, 1975).



-3-
Land Tenure and Reform
2.05      Attempts have been made to change the region's highly uneven
structure of land ownership but little progress has been achieved. The
Government's land redistribution program financed under PROTERRA has had
minimal overall impact due to administrative shortcomings and inadequate
support from INCRA, the land reform agency. Actual cases of land reform
have generally been limited to highly specific areas (e.g., irrigation
development areas) of fairly limited overall size. A land purchase credit
program under PROTERRA and administered by Banco do Brasil has had some
success but the credit terms (requiring a 20% down payment and 12-year re-
payment) have limited its impact and access of existing landless such as
sharecroppers. The Government is now undertaking an intensive review of
its land reform policy and programs.
The State of Rio Grande do Norte (RN)
2.06      Rio Grande do Norte (RN) with a population of about 1.6 million
and an area of 51,000 km2 is one of the smallest of the Northeast states but
is nevertheless representative of much of the Northeast. Natal (population
350,000), capital and principal city, is in the densely-populated narrow
coastal belt. Moving west from Natal (map) both the annual rainfall and
population density decline quickly in passing through a transition Agreste
aone into the semi-arid Sertao. Both the Agreste and Sertao are vulnerable
to periodic drought. A variety of Federal and State programs have fostered
the establishment of new industries, mainly around Natal, but these have not
provided sufficient employment to absorb the increase of the State's labor
force.. There has been a gradual emigration from rural areas to the cities
of the Northeast and to the more industrialized Center-South. To help solve
its health problems, the State initiated with relevant Federal agencies a
pioneer program of low-cost village-level health "miniposts". It has also
been the only state in Brazil to officially support a family planning pro-
gram.
III. THE PROJECT AREA (Annex 1)
3.01      The project area of 21,000 km  corresponds approximately to the
State's perennial cotton-growing area (map) and about 40% of the total area
of the State. The area is generally semi-arid (average rainfall varying
from 400 mm to 700 mm) with erratic and unpredictable rainfall. The his-
tory of fieriodic drought has influenced greatly farmers' willingness to
Innovate and invest. Apart from valley bottoms, soils are generally poor.
The area is divided into 4 sub-areas and 53 municipios (similar to coun-
ties) and encompasses significant variatioi in climate, geology, crops,
infrastructure and farm size.  Population is 4159000 (1970 census) of
e^hich roughly 270,000 are rural. During the decade of the 60's the
urban population annual growth rate (2.6%) was twice that of the rural;
there has also been considerable emigration from the area. Only about



-4-
18% of the area is under crops, with the balance either natural (bush)
pasture or unusable. Cash income is mainly derived from high-quality
(but low-yielding) long staple cotton from perennial plants having a
five-year cycle and considerable drought resistance, supplemented by
cattle; maize and beans are the princ!pal foodcrops, g-nerally grown in
association with first-year cotton.
3.02      Rainfall records since 1930 indicate that whereas there are signi-
ficant risks of critical drought which reduce yields or in severe cases destroy
crops, adequate to good conditions for perenn4al cotton occurred in about 65%
of recorded years (Annex 1, para 3). Monthly rainfall in the project area
must exceed 100 mm in both March and April and 40 mm in May to ensure satis-
factory establishment of newly planted cotton and good yields of the mature
crop.
3.03      There are between 20,000 and 25,000 farms in the project area
(Annex 1). Of these, roughly three-quarters are less than 50 ha; however,
the remaining one-quarter of over 50 ha accounts for three-quarters of the
cultivated land. Because of the generally low percentage of area under cul-
tivation (due to exceptionally poor soils in the project area) and resulting
low value of output per ha, farms of less than 200 ha provide incomes below
one-third of the national average; about 90% of the farm families with fewer
than 200 ha fall within the target groups (Annex 1). Smaller farms cultivate
a higher percentage of their area than large farms do; farms of less than
10 ha cultivate 40 to 45% of their area while those over 500 ha cultivate
only 10-15% (which can only partly be attributed to the quality of the
land). The project area's rural population of some 52,000 families can be
subdivided into: temporary workers (35%); sharecroppers (20%); small owner-
operators (generally less than 200 ha) (35%); and larger owner-operators (10%).
The larger farmers generally operate using sharecoppers while smaller farmers;
particularly those owning farms of less than 10 ha, sometimes work for part
of the year outside their own farms. All but the largest landowners are
relatively poor by Brazilian standards.
3.04      A wide range of sharecropping systems exist on larger farms.  One
system is for the cash crop (cotton) to be split 50:50 between owner and
sharecropper, with the owner providing the original planting and sometimes
pesticides and the right to the sharecropper to plant subsistence crops in
certain areas. The sharing of the crop impedes investment; neither owner
nor sharecropper wishes to invest much where the other party takes one-half
of the incremental benefits. On-farm storage losses of subsistence crops
are high. The owner often also extends credit to the sharecropper and sells
him foodcrops during the off-season; the crop is almost always marketed
jointly. The implicit interest rates in these joint transactions are often
very high, to the sharecropper's disadvantage. Laws exist to protect share-
croppers' rights of tenancy but, given the large numbers involved and the
all-encompassing relationship between sharecropper and owner (involving pro-
duction, credit, food crops and social matters) protection is extremely dif-
ficult to ensure through legislation.



-5-
3.05      Most farmers sell their cotton to various private gins, either
directly or through a variety of buyer/transporters who sometimes also advance
money or buy a crop "on the bush" prior to harvest. Four cooperatives are
active in cotton marketing but only one is strong and together they account
for no more than 18% of crop sales.
Services in the Project Area
Extension Service (Annex 2)
3.06      ANCAR/RN 1/, part of Brazil's nationwide extension service network,
provides extension in the project area. The (Federal) Ministry of Agricul-
ture and SUDENE provide the bulk of the funding for ANCAR/RN; the (State)
Secretariat of Agriculture, Bank of Brazil and local authorities also con-
tribute.  ANCAR/RN serves 41 municipios in the project area through 22 field
offices staffed mainly by intermediate level (non-university graduate) agri-
cultural advisers; 12 municipios are not covered by ANCAR/RN. The intensity
of coverage varies greatly among sub-areas and, overall, funding has been
erratic.
3.07      A major part of ANCAR/RN's role is to provide technical support
to the agricultural lending activities of banks. The typical agricultural
adviser spends most of his time on administrative matters, mainly credit,
leaving little time for actual extension, Existing extensionists work
mainly with the (generally large) farmers who actually borrow from banks
and in aggregate the extension service probably reaches fewer than 10% of
the total number of farms. There has been little liaison with any activity
(e.g., research) devising technical packages financially attractive to farm-
ers.
Credit (Annex 3)
3.08      Bank of Brazil (BB) provides about 80% of the institutional credit
for seasonal production and investment purposes for agriculture in the proj-
ect area and Bank of the Northeast (BNB) most of the remainder. Institu-
tional credit is overwhelmingly for seasonal production purposes. Most of
the credit goes to larger farmers; nevertheless, the average loan (about
US$700 in 1973) is small. Over 80% of farms in the project area do not
borrow from institutional sources; some of these borrow from informal
sources, tying the borrower to a particular channel of crop sale at a
disadvantageous price to the borrower with resulting high implicit rates
of interest. In rare instances sharecroppers borrow directly from banks
but generally on the co-signature of the landowner.
1/   The Rio Grande do Norte section of the Northeast Association of Credit
and Rural Technical Assistance.



-6-
3.09      BB and BNB are both large, multi-purpose financial institutions
that have been used as credit channels in previous Bank loans. BB operates
four branches in the project area while BNB operates three; however, some
farmers travel up to 100 km to borrow from a bank branch. BNB, headquartered
in Fortaleza, is an official development institution oriented exclusively to
the Northeast. BB, Brazil's largest commercial bank, has experience with simpli-
fied lending procedures to individual farmers for small agricultural loans
of less than 50 times the reference value (i.e., a total of CR$ 18,800 or
US$2,500). 1/ BNB channels its small agricultural loans only through exist-
ing cooperatives. Existing lending procedures for both institutions are
cumbersome and the local staff, who generally perceive their career pros-
pects to be determined by loss experience, make lending decisions mainly
on borrowers' security coverage, even where greater discretionary power
is permitted under official procedures. Individual loan applications
require a major involvement by the local extensionist and occupy most
of his time.
Technical Development (Annex 4)
3010      EMBRAPA, 2/ a recently-created federal agency, is responsible for
Brazilian agricultural research and operates one minor research station for
cotton in RN at Cruzeta, in the project area. EMBRAPA is the successor of
earlier research arrangements. It is currently undergoing major reorganiza-
tion to meet its overall aim of making its work more relevant to farmers.
It has formulated a new research program for Northeast Brazil for which it
is requesting Bank financing. The Cruzeta station, founded in the 1930's,
has developed new strains with improved ginning characteristics. However,
in general, its work has not been well-integrated with farmers' economic
needs; e.g., there has been no work on food crops, of particular importance
to small farmers.
3.11      INFAOL 3/, a semi-public body located in the Northeast, has devel-
oped considerable experience in on-farm trials work in connection with seed
production for cotton and other crops. The Federal University of CEARA (UFC)
is carrying out agronomic and related economic analysis on perennial cotton
farms in both Rio Grande do Norte and the neighboring state of Ceara. The
agricultural economics department of UFC is doing some analysis of the econo-
mic impact on farmers of possible technical innovations.
1/   "Reference value"-is the monetary correction factor recently established
by the government replacing but roughly equivalent to the previous
monetary correction factor based on the minimum monthly wage.
2/   Empresa Brasileira de Pesquisa Agropecuaria.
3/   Instituto Nordestino Para 0 Fomento de Algodao e Oleaginosas (North-
east Institute for the Development of Cotton and Oilseeds).



-7-
3.12      A variety of innovations which are in varying degrees of readiness
and of potential benefit to farmers have been identified. However, to date
nobody has coordinated the various research results and technical opportuni-
ties in putting them into financially attractive technical packages based on
farm budgets. Research on chemical fertilizer use has been incomplete and
inconclusive. Little work has been done on crops grown in association, of
importance in the project area. Great variation in yields exist among farm-
ers, better farmers employing cultural practices such as plant protection, and
animal traction for rapid weeding (the low value of output per ha generally
makes tractors unattractive). Almost no technical development work has been
done on improved use of animal traction, alternative foodcrops (sorghum is
probably more suitable to the area than maize), organic fertilizer, use of
herbicides and other practices, some of which can be generally recommended
to farmers immediately or with little additional work and time.
Health (Annex 5)
3.13      A variety of communicable, water-borne or nutrition deficiency
related diseases are prevalent in the project area, reflected in higher
infant mortality and general incidence of sickness and lower life expec-
tancy than for Brazil as a whole. Most of the diseases are amenable to
simple, low-cost actions in preventive medicine and sanitation.
3.14      A number of State and Federal institutions are active in preven-
tive and curative health services in the project area, including the State
Department for Public Health (Health Department), Foundation for Public
Health Services (Foundation SESP), ANCAR/RN, and the Rural University
Center for Training and Community Development (CRUTAC). The Fund for
Assistance of Rural Workers (FUNRURAL) finances mainly curative services
through agreements with various treatment centers.
3015      ANCAR/RN, working closely with Foundation SESP, in 1968 started
a rural community health program centered around community-supported health
'miniposts". This program has proved highly successful in community accept-
ance and support and cost-effectiveness; it has also been taken up by other
states. The formation of a community health society which selects the mini-
post worker (for further training) and provides partial continuing finance
is a precondition for each minipost. Miniposts are small, located in simple
or renovated buildings with simple equipment. Principal emphasis is on
preventive maternal-child health (MCH), immunizations, nutrition, education,
training, health education and environmental sanitation. Referral is sup-
posed to be to larger health centers but these do not exist in sufficient
number or form to provide adequate referral at present. The recurrent cost
of medicines, vaccines and supplies is provided by the Federal and State
governments; personnel costs by a combination of State, local and community
sources. The salaries of minipost workers are sometimes delayed and local
officials are trying to develop a new formula to combine local government
(minicipio) financial support and prompt salary payment.  ANCAR/RN super-
vises the non-medical aspects of minipost operations through its social



A 8 _
extensionists located in the project area, who also promote nutrition-edu-
cation and other community activities. Under separate, State-related pro-
grams, many of the minipost workers have received training in family plan-
ning.
3.16      Despite their great success to date, the ex.1sting 28 miniposts in
the project area probably reach fewer than 10% of rural households, and they
are poorly served by supervision, backup and referral services; nevertheless,
the concept holds great promise for improving, at relatively low cost, the
health status and, hence, the potential productivity of the population in
the project area.
Marketing, Infrastructure, Communications (Annex 6)
3.17      The road network in the project area is generally not a bottleneck
especially in that major movements of harvested crops and inputs are generally
during the dry season. Private trucking at competitive rates is widely avail-
able. Cotton gins are mainly privately-owned and in aggregate have consider-
able excess capacity. The gins buy on a delivered to gin basis at posted
prices or from intermediaries who buy from farmers (and often extend credit),
often in advance of harvest, and transport to the gins. Also, Banco do Brazil
administers a minimum price system linked to its production loans, though the
system is virtually inoperative because the minimum price has been set in the
past at a very low level (60%) and only the poorest quality cotton benefits
from it. The State Secretariat of Agriculture provides Government grading
services at gins but this is not well supervised and the grading performance
varies considerably among gins. Improved seeds are distributed by a few pri-
vate gins and by CIDA, a State-owned input-supply and development company.
CIDA is a new agency that is currently reorganizing its activities to improve
the often inadequate performance of its predecessor agencies. CIDA and a var-'
iety of private agencies distribute pesticides and simple tools.
3.18      Radio Rural, a semi-private body transmitting from three sites
in RN, provides farm and rural news to the large number of radio receivers
in the project area. The Brazilian Space Agency (INPE) provides under
"Project SACI" a variety of television services to 2,000 television sets
that it has supDlied and maintains (very effectively) in schools and other
community centers through RN. These provide various educational programs but
so far little progress has been made in developing programs relevant to the
project area or to link the viewing to particular projects or community
programs.



- 9 -
IV. THE PROJECT
A. Objectives and General Approach
4.01      A Bank mission to the Northeast in 1972 identified RN as a state
having the commitment and ability to prepare and implement rural development
projects. The State then hired consultants who prepared a project (mainly
settlement) which in late 1973 was reviewed by Bank staff and found exces-
sively costly per beneficiary. The State therefore undertook to prepare an
alternative project intended to improve the incomes of large numbers of people
at relatively low cost. The State-level agricultural planning agency, CEPA/
RN, 1/ was made responsible for preparing the project. Other State and Fed-
eral agencies seconded staff to CEPA/RN and in total over 20 State-level tech-
nicians participated in the preparation, several of whom would be heavily in-
volved in implementation (para 5.02). Several Bank missions have visited the
State for discussions during preparation. The local team, mindful of the
structural and technical problems involved, recognized that the first proj-
ect could not tackle all of the problems at once. Thus, in this project the
largest group of beneficiaries would be expected to be the large number of
poor farm families owning and operating small farms in the project area, as
the group of rural poor most able to respond to project actions. Sharecrop-
pers and other landless would participate only marginally in the credit com-
ponent but would participate to a greater degree through the extension com-
ponent and fully through the health component. Also some may purchase land
through a parallel program of easier credit for land-purchase (para 4.13).
Subsequent rural development projects in RN or neighboring states hopefully
would find ways to expand project coverage to other groups of beneficiaries,
but may also require more direct land reform components. A major focus on
this project would be on identifying suitable technical packages and devel-
oping the delivery mechanisms (involving considerable institutional reori-
entation) to reach small farmers, gaining their participation and demon-
strating that small farmers can be productive.
4.02      The project's principal objective would be to develop the insti-
tutional capability and focus to conceive, plan and implement in a coor-
dinated manner actions to increase the incomes and welfare of the target
group of farm families, at cost levels that would permit their replicabil-
ity. During the disbursement period (covering phases I and II) the project
would aim to reach some 15,000 farm families by its directly productive com-
ponents, generating a marketable surplus of food crops and roughly doubling
cotton production of participants. This would enable an increase in incomes
of fully-participating farm families of same 50% within 5 years of their par-
ticipation. The project would also aim to provide complete community health
coverage, including a system of referral, to about 80% of the project area's
total rural population.
1/   ComissaoEstadual de Planejamento Agricola, formed as a convenio between
the Federal Ministry of Agriculture, SUDENE and the State Government.



- 10 -
4.03      The physical environment of the project area (Annex 1) is difficult
and the project's actions are not fully proven. To reduce the risks, the
project would be phased. Phase I would focus on 11 representative munici-
pios covering about 20% of the cultivated land in the project area. In
addition to its productive impact, it would refine and disseminate tech-
nical packages financially attractive to small farmers and methods of deliv-
ering extension end credit and health services to the target group. It would
also aim to strengthen existing institutions involved in rural development in
the project area. Phase II, expected to begin in year 3, would expand the
actions developed and tested during Phase I to part or all of the project
area only if the Bank is satisfied with progress under Phase I. The deci-
sion to proceed with Phase II would be subject to a comprehensive review of
Phase I by the Government, in consultation with Bank staff. During negotia-
tions assurances were obtained to this effect.
B. Brief Description
4.04      The project would consist of:
(a)  Extension service improvement, mainly by the addition of
agricultural advisers especially trained to focus on small
farmers' problpms and the provision of logistical support;
and through increased numbers of social extension workers;
(b)  Credit to small farmers for seasonal production purposes and
for on-farm investments and area expansion; alternative deliv-
ery systems would be tested and simplified, and credit proce-
dures developed to improve small farmers' access to credit;
credit insurance would be made available to small farmers to
decrease borrowers' risks;
(c)  Experimentation and farm trials focusing on the practical
needs of small farmers, to improve technical packages for
cotton and other crops;
(d)  Health program expansion and strengthening of supervision
and referral systems;
(e)  Training of project staff and development of training
materials for farmers;
(f)  Organizational support for project administration,
monitoring and evaluation, and a project special
fund; and
(g)  Preparation of future rural development projects in
other areas of the State.



- 11 -
4.05      During Phase I, the various methods and delivery systems of the
project would be monitored and modified as necessary prior to Phase II.
Throughout the project, flexibility to change the project's actions accord-
ing to actual project needs and results would be important.
4.06      The responsibility for administering the project would be placed
in a Project Unit located in CEPA/RN (para 5.01), The Project Unit would
conceive and stimulate actions under the project, coordinate the works of
the various participating agencies, and control project finances. The
Project Unit has been established. During negotiations assurances were
obtained from Government that it would adequately support CEPA/RN during
the disbursement period to permit it to effectively house the Project
Unit.
C. Detailed Features
Extension (Annex 2)
4.07      The extension service (ANCAR/RN) would be expanded to provide a
more intensive coverage of small farmers. This intensified effort would be
expanded in the second phase to cover the total project area. Staff would
be recruited and trained to deal with small farm systems and problems (Annex 7).
The work of agricultural advisers would be rationalized and their involvement
in preparing loan applications restricted to technical recommendations. (These
recommendations and the participating banks' subsequent evaluation of the pro-
posals would be based on matrices of the likely viability of the technical
packages in different farm sizes and subregions). The coverage of agricul-
tural advisers would gradually be built up to a ratio of one agricultural
adviser per 120 farm families. The project would finance the incremental
costs of increased extension service including, mainly, vehicles, equipment
and incremental salaries during the five-year project period (Annex 2, para
32). Specific attempts would be made (closely with the credit component--
paras 4.09 thru 4.14) to permit the agricultural adviser to work through
small groups of farmers, using different types of local extension inter-
mediaries, thereby increasing his reach and reducing the extension cost
per farmer.
4.08      New agricultural advisers hired would largely be intermediate level
rather than university graduate extensionists. They would be supervised by
regional coordinators (about 1 for each 17 agricultural advisers) and given
periodic training to link the agricultural adviser's work with the technical
recommendations developed under the practical experimentation and field test
component (paras 4.15 and 4.16). The nuwber of social extensionists of ANCAIR/
RN would also be increased (by about 16 extensionists during Phase I) to work
with the minipost health program (para 40X7) and work with expanded community
and development programs (e.g. nutrition, household gardens). This would in-
clude a small pilot credit (in kind) component to familiarize households with
the production of small animals.



12 -
Credit (Annex 3)
4.09      The project would include incremental credit to farmers for sea-
sonal (crop production) and investment purDoses, simplified lending proce-
dures and innovative delivery  vystems.  Seasonal crop rroduction loans would
cover normal crop production c0stS included in the techniques recommended
under the project (e.g. use of pesticides) and costs of hired labor for
picking, weeding, etc.  Sub-,Yoans for investmeent purposes wsould mainly be

finance purchases of some modern inputs, for which the interest rate u-jould
be 0%. Loans for investment purposes would typically be for 5 years for
"semi-fixed" investment (e.g., work animals) and 12 years for "fixed"
(e.g., small buildings, fences). The unindexed credit, with low nominal
rates of interest, results in negative real interest rates to the farmer.
However, the beneficiaries are from the lowest income groups, (to be en-
forced through the above-mentioned size limits on credits) and the consid-
erable strengthening of extension would help guard against misuse of the
funds.
4.11      Simplified lending procedures would include lending predetermined
amounts per hectare on an unsecured (signature only) basis for seasonal loans
and, for investment loans, employing simplified procedures focusing on an
applicant's projected debt service capacity based on average yields obtained
and area cultivated. The extension worker's role in this would be limited
to technical matters. Under mobile banking, BB would bring its activities
closer to farmers by providing to municipios not currently covered by bank
branches, a banking outlet (in rented quarters or mobile unit) to be open,
say, one day each two weeks, year-round; this appears to be financially
feasible for the participating bank (Annex 3). Lending to small groups
of farmers (say 5 to 12) for seasonal production loans and in certain
cases for investment purposes could reduce the administrative costs of



-13 -
providing loans to large numbers of small farmers. The farmer groups could
also perform other useful roles, e.g., spreading extension more economically.
As a condition of effectiveness, Government and the Project Unit would enter
into formal agreements with BNB and BB to ensure application of the above-
described actions and policies.
4.12      The Government would review annually the progress made in expand-
ing banks' lending to small farmers, including the effectiveness of existing
terms, conditions and promotional efforts in removing potential biases of
participating banks' staff against making small agricultural loans. The
Government has agreed to submit to the Bank a report of the annual progress
and to introduce additional measures when the Government and the Bank agree
they would be necessary.
4.13      In support of project objectives, Government would revise through
POLONORDESTE the terms for credit for individuals to purchase small parcels
of land and become owner-operator farmers. Such a revision in terms for
land purchase credit, which is already available under the PROTERRA program,
could become a significant means for rural landless to acquire land. Credit
terms would be 7% interest rate; a 20 year maturity including 5 years of grace
and loan amounts would cover up to 100% of the purchase price of land. These
terms are more favorable than the existing land purchase program under PROTERRA
(see para 2.06). During negotiations an assurance was obtained from Govern-
ment that these terms for land purchase credits under POLONORDESTE would apply
to the project area.
4.14      During negotiations, the Government gave assurances that it would
promote the use of PROAGRO (a voluntary crop-credit insurance scheme newly
initiated by the Government) for subloans under the project to the maximum
possible extent. Sub-borrowers who use PROAGRO would pay a standard annual
1% on average loan balances to cover their loan repayment obligations for
specific risks (e.g. drought). The need for compulsory credit insurance
would be analyzed as part of the annual review of progress in lending to
small farmers.
Technical Proposals and Farm Trials (Annex 4)
4.15      The initial technical recommendations would include animal traction
(for rapid weeding and expanding cultivated area), improved plant protection
(e.g., use of pesticides), improved seeds, and on-farm storage of food crops,
for the target group of small farmers. These techniques are currently used
by better farmers to good effect but are not universally used because of
limited access to credit and extension. These techniques would be further
refined during years 1 and 2 (improved selection and use of pesticides; use
of animal-drawn equipment, permitting wfider use but requiring testing in
local conditions), for rapid transmittal to farmers. The technical recom-
mendations would be in conjunction with modified credit procedures, the pro-
motion of credit insurance and more effective extension; the combination of
all of these can be considered to be the technical package.



- 14 -
4.16      A number of other improved techniques have been identified (e.g.,
use of herbicides, organic and inorganic fertilizers, new varieties of food
crops, soil conservation, early planting) which are in varying degrees of
readiness for diffusion to farmers, ranging from immediate readiness for
broad diffusion to farmers, through a necessity for refinement by on-farm
trials, to additional work at the research station. The project would
finance on-farm trials to refine these techniques to permit rapid general
diffusion to farmers.  Initial emphasis would be on new implements to im-
prove effectiveness of animal traction and on improved plant protection
through pesticide selection and application. The project would also work
closely bi_-h ::elated wzrk at the research station level.  Working through
the technicEl committae (para 5.02) the project would focus the trials and
research work towaryo developing technical packages financially attractive
to small farmers. The techlnical activities would liaise closely with the
extension component in order ts prnvids a rapid diffusion of results.
Health (Annex 5)

to nine miniposts). For purposes of cost-effectiveness, buildings where possi-

w^ould be mobilized. The project would provide training for the locally-
recruited minipost workers and health center auxilliary nurses. The health
centers would take over more of the supervision of miniposts, freeing the
ANCAR/RN social extensionists to do more community health and nutrition
work, Under Phase II, based on the evaluation of Phase I, and employing
lessons learned, actions under Phase I would be extended to cover about
80% of the project area's population. The project would finance the full
five-year costs (including salaries) of the (largely developmental) activ-
ities in the Phase I municipios but only the training, construction and
equipment costs of the Phase II municipios.
Training (Annex 7)
4.18      Training would be provided for extension personnel, community
health workers, field trials technicians, bank staff, community workers
and project unit and monitoring group personnel. Training would be both
formal and informal and would be within and among different agencies
working on the same component. Training would be executed by the par-
ticipating agencies assisted by the Project Unit and consultants (para
4.20).



- 15 -
4.19      The project would attempt, in close liaison with the extension
component, to reach farmers through media and printed materials. The proj-
ect would support the production of television films on project-related
subjects (e.g., animal traction, credit procedures) especially for the
project area in support of Project SACI (para 3.18).
Project Administration, Monitoring and Evaluation, Technical Assistance,
Project Special Fund and Preparation of Further Rural Development Projects
4.20      The project would be implemented by existing institutions (Annex
12). A Project Unit would be set up (para 5.01) within CEPA/RN to be mainly
responsible for coordinating the planning, implementation and control of the
finances under the project; a separate unit would monitor and evaluate the
project (Annex 8). Technical assistance (including some 12 man-years of
consulting services) would be provided in planning and implementing: spec-
ific training programs (para 4.18), the planning, analysis and interpreta-
tion of actions to refine improved agricultural techniques and delivery
systems (para 4.15 and 4.16) and the monitoring and evaluation of the var-
ious project components. The project would finance a project special fund
for use on the authorization of the coordinator of the Project Unit mainly
for special technical assistance and training. The project would also
finance the project preparation of another rural development project in
RN, probably in the Agreste_area between the project area and the coast
(Annex 9). During negotiations assurances were obtained that prior Bank
approval would be secured for the project preparation work program and
consultants' selection and terms of reference.
D. Cost Estimates
4.21      The project comprises a time-slice in a program of actions includ-
ing agricultural credit, expansion of agricultural extension services, on-
farm trials and other items for which the calculation of physical contin-
gencies would not be appropriate. Price contingencies used (1975, 11%;
1976, 9%; 1977, 8%; 1978, 8%; 1979, 8%; 1980, 7%) reflect Bank guidelines.
Cost estimates are detailed in Annex 10 and summarized as follows:

E. Financing
4.22      Financing of Project costs would be shared as follows:

The proposed Bank loan of US$12.0 million would be to the Federative Republic
of Brazil and would be for 25 years including 5 years grace. It would finance
the relatively small foreign exchange costs (US$3.8 million) plus 31% of local
costs (totalling US$8.2 million equivalent) or 40% of total costs. The financ-
ing of part of the local cost component would be warranted given the size of
Brazil's total external capital needs greatly exceeding its need for imported
capital goods and by the highly desirable project focus on small farmers.  Funds
for agricultural credit would be made available by Government to the partici-
pating banks (para 4.11).  The closing date of the loan would be June 30, 1981.
An estimated schedule of Bank disbursements is attached as Annex 11.  During
negotiations an assurance was obtained from Government that sufficient local
funds would be provided promptly as needed to implement the project.
F. Procurement
4.23      Much of the project would finance training, build-up of staffs,
research and technical assistance, activities involving only minor con-
struction or purchase of equipment. Mini-health posts and health centers
(totalling about US$0.6 million) would be small, of varied design and geo-
graphically dispersed and would be constructed by a combination of self-
help, force account and local bidding. Purchased farm inputs would be



- 18 
procured by farmers in small amounts through local trade channels. Purchases
of equipment, including small vehicles which are produced and readily avail-
able locally would be under local bidding procedures which are satisfactory.
G. Disbursements
4.24      Disbursements for extension would be at 31% of total expenditures
resulting in the Bank financing an equivalent of about 40% of incremental
expenditures; all other components and disbursements would be at the rate
of 40% of total expenditures or sub-loans. Disbursements would be against
statements of expenditures initiated by the various implementing agencies
under the project and certified by the Project Unit within CEPA/RN, under
annual plans approved by the Bank (para 5.04).  The documentation in sup-
port of expenditures under the Project would not be submitted for review
but retained by the Borrower and made available for inspection by the Bank
during the course of project supervision missions.  Undisbursed funds remain-
ing after project completion could, with Bank approval, be used for other
rural development activity in RN.
H. Accounts and Audit
4.25      Each of the participating agencies would maintain accounts of ex-
penditures under the project which would be audited annually by Government
auditors according to standard practice. The Project Unit would maintain
accounts of its expenditures under the project together with statements of
project expenditures by participating agencies. Copies of the audited
accounts of project expenditures of the various agencies would be forwarded
to the Bank within four months of the end of each fiscal year; the Project
Unit would supply the necessary coordination to effect this. Both Banco
do Brasil and Banco do Nordeste are audited by satisfactory independent
auditors. Both would provide within four months of the end of their fiscal
years audited statements of their lending under the project. During nego-
tiations assurances were obtained that these procedures would be followed.
V. ORGANIZATION AND MANAGEMENT
5.01      The project's detailed actions would be implemented by existing
agencies (e.g., ANCAR/RN, the participating banks, INFAOL, Secretariat of
Health). A Project Unit to be concerned solely with the project, would
be established within CEPA/RN to stimulate and conceive actions under the
project, coordinate work of the various participating agencies and control
project finances. The Project Unit would report periodically through a
State-level agricultural policy coordinating body (Conselho Diretor) to



- 19 -
the Special Coordinating and Monitoring Group of POLONORDESTE. The overall
approach is consistent with Brazil's "convenio system" whereby a number of
agencies work together jointly under a written agreement or "convenio" to
carry out a particular program.
5.02      The Project Unit would be expected to have a small staff (about
seven professionals). In view of the complexity of the Project Unit's
task, the staff would need to be experienced and imaginative. Fortunately,
a number of persons who would probably be assigned to the Project Unit in-
cluding the team leader and a number from the participating agencies have
worked in preparing the project. The organization of the Project Unit
staff would need to be flexible, to respond to the results encountered by
initial project actions. Suggested terms of reference for the Project Unit
are in Annex 12, para 21-27. The precise roles of each would need to be
interpreted flexibly, depending on the evolving project task and particular
experiences of people recruited. In addition to a Project Leader/Coordi-
nator, the Project Unit staff would consist of advisers for technical activ-
ities, extension, credit, human resources and cooperatives and marketing.
Each would work closely with the relevant participating agencies and with
the other members of the Project Unit in conceiving, stimulating, planning
and implementing actions under the project. Each would chair a committee
of representatives of the relevant participating agencies to meet at least
quarterly to coordinate work on the particular component. A financial con-
troller would coordinate the project financial plans of participating agen-
cies and review and approve disbursement requests of participating agencies.
5.03      A separate monitoring group, staffed by at least two full-time
professionals experienced in research in social or economic fields, would
be established within the Project Unit. The monitoring group would work
closely with the project unit but would not have specific implementing
responsibility. It would be assisted by consultants in work design,
data-processing, analysis and interpretation.
5.04      Each participating agency would prepare jointly with the Project
Unit an annual plan covering actions under the project for the year. The
annual plans would be reviewed by the Conselho Diretor and the Special
Group of POLONORDESTE and would be subject to Bank approval. An assurance
was obtained during negotiations that these procedures would be followed.
5.05      The state Government of RN and its various agencies are active in
various fields of importance to the project, in particular input supply and
cotton grading (para 3.17). During negotiations an assurance was obtained
from the State Government that it would generally support the project by,
in particular, taking all steps necessary to support the maintenance of
the Project Unit within CEPA/RN, adequately enforcing its cotton grading
regulations within the project area and supporting CIDA in its efforts to
ensure adequate supplies of improved seeds and other inputs in the project
area.



- 20 -
VI. PRODUCTION, MARKETS, AND FARMERS' BENEFITS
Production
6.01      Major producer benefits are expected to arise from:  (a) increased
cotton production through increased yields and an expansion of cultivated
area; and (b) increases in production of subsistence crops (corn, beans,
sorghum) through improved varieties and cultivation practices to be further
tested and refined during the first years of implementation. Cotton yield
increases from existing low levels would be facilitated by improved farmer
training, and technical assistance from an expanded extension service net-
work; intensive trials and demonstration on farmers plots; and improved
credit and input delivery systems. Projected yield improvements would be
an increase from a five-year cycle annual average of 175 kg/ha at present
to an average of 300 kg/ha by the sixth year. After nine years substantial
results from research and experimentation could be expected to increase this
average to a minimum of 350 kg/ha (Annex 4).
6.02      Under these assumptions, at full development, total area of partici-
pating farmers under cotton cultivation would increase from 88,000 ha to about
100,000 ha and annual production would more than double. Total area under
food crops is estimated to increase from about 18,000 ha to 20,000 ha. Of
the expected cotton production increase, 25% would be attributed to increase
area and 75% to increased yields. For the aggregate of small farms of 0 to
50 ha and medium of 50 to 200 ha, 1/ the results would be as follows:
1/   Because of the exceptionally poor soils, farms in the project area
of less than 200 ha generally produce per capita incomes below one-
third of the national average (para 3.03).

6.03      Further benefits from increased cotton production of larger farms
(more than 200 ha), would also be expected from the results of research and
improvements in extension. Benefits from increased production of food crops,
although expected to be large, are not readily quantifiable, as they are
dependent on results of further research and experimentation (Annex 4).
Marketing and Prices
6.04      The overall increase in cotton production under the project, amount-
ing to approximately 18% of current long-staple perennial cotton production
of the State or less than 3% of the Northeast, would have no impact on over-,
all price relationships. All of the increase would be absorbed by the local
ginning industry which currently experiences more than 40% unused capacity.
The marketing and transportation of the incremental production would be done
according to the traditional systems of: (a) collection by the gins; (b)
intermediaries; and (c) cooperatives. Improvements in the delivery systems
and the pricing mechanisms would be expected from: (a) the anticipated ex-
pansion of cooperative coverage; (b) the strengthened bargaining power of
farmers having access to institutional credit and less dependence on money
lenders; and (c) possible formation of farmer marketing groups.
6.05      The price of long-staple cotton, benefiting from a premium over
regular cotton, would nevertheless be subject to the same price fluctuations,
mainly related to international cotton price movements. Long-run projections
by the Bank's Commodities Division indicate that the price would remain about
constant in real terms through the project development period.
6.06      The expected marketable surplus of corn and beans would not basic-
ally modify the existing price structure of these crops. However, improved
on-farm conservation, especially for beans, would allow farmers to delay
their sales to take advantage of more favorable seasonal price circum-
stances.



- 22 -
Producer Income and Benefits
6.07      The general average cash income from cotton operations would be
expected to have doubled by year 12. Net cash income per family from in-
creased cotton production would be as follows: 1/

---                 us-------------US$-
Small farms                   210                 430            220
Medium farms                  590               1,200            610
6.08      Large variations could be expected among the various groups of each
farm class, depending upon the availability of credit, location in the proj-
ect area and other factors (e.g. tenure arrangements). In addition, substan-
tial increases in producers' incomes through increased on-farm consumption
and some marketable surpluses can be expected from increases in the produc-
tion of subsistence crops. Other important but non-quantifiable benefits
could be expected from improved health and other social services and com-
munity and self-help project activities (para 4.17).
6.09      The project would provide a modest increase in employment in the
project area and would reduce underemployment. On the smaller farms much of
the increased labor requirement for project development is met largely from
family labor rather than hired labor. Nevertheless, at full development, the
incremental man-power requirements would be approximately 2,200 man-years per
year, equivalent to about 4% of the agricultural labor force of the project
area. This amount, while important, would not be expected to significantly
affect the existing labor supply and wages.
VII.  ECONOMIC ANALYSIS
7.01      The project's combination of institutional reform, social compo-
nents and overall developmental nature in a difficult and varied project
area, make the estimation of economic costs and benefits particularly dif-
ficult. The economic analysis is in Annex 13.
7.02      The 15,000 farmers projected to benefit directly from the project
(para. 6.07) have been subdivided according to farm size, previous use of
1/   Output from crops (mainly cotton) represents from 75 to 85% of total
value of output before development (Annex 13). Net cash income from
non-cotton output at full development would not be expected to exceed
30%.



- 23 -
formal credit and extension and level of expected participation in these
services under the project. Data for the numbers and classification of farmers
have been drawn heavily from the preliminary results from SUDENE/IBRD/DRC sur-
vey. 1/ About 9% of small farms and 17% of medium sized farms currently re-
ceive credit. Production credit under the project would be expected to reach
50% of those not currently receiving credit, half of which would also borrow
to increase cultivated area.   All of the farms would be reached, in varying
degrees, by expanded agricultural extension technical advice. While foodcrop
development would be important under the project the economic analysis focuses
on perennial cotton, the principal cash crop.
7.03      Because of wide differences among farms the analysis covers groups
of farms on an aggregate basis. For farmers participating fully under the
project average yields for the five-year plant cycle 2/ would be expected to
increase over about 9 years from existing extremely low levels of 150 kg to
200 kg of seedcotton per ha to 350 kg per ha.
7.04      The baseline situation involves little or no use of animal traction,
rudimentary manual weeding, traditional seeds and little or no use of in-
secticides. A 300 kg/ha average yield would involve full use of existing
technology including animal traction, frequent weeding, proper spraying,
improved seeds and corresponds to yields of existing better farmers; the
350 kg/ha yield allows for some results from the trials and research pro-
gram (e.g., improved tools for animal traction). The various groups of
target farmers are at different baseline stages and would advance at dif-
ferent rates under the project.
7.05      Cash costs have been valued at prevailing market rates.  Family
labor has been valued at the minimum wage which reflects the market rate
on a year-round average basis. No shadow rate for foreign exchange was
used. The price projected for perennial cotton is according to Bank
projections for international cotton, adjusted to the project area for
grade and location.
7.06      Benefits for part of project investment would extend beyond the
project area. Accordingly, 70% of research and experimentation and 40% of
project administration, monitoring and evaluation were not charged to the
benefit cost stream. The overall economic rate of return for the directly
productive parts of the project is 15% without taking account of benefits
from increased production of foodcrops.
1/   A farge survey of farms in the Northeast was carried out in 1973
jointly by SUDENE and the Bank's Development Research Center.
2/   The average baseline yield of seedcotton of 150 kg would be an
average of: 1 year - 50 kg, year 2 - 225 kg, year 3 - 225 kg,
year 4 - 150 kg, and year 5 - 100 kg; these yields also are aver-
ages of good and bad rainfall years, and represent expected values
including the drought probability coefficient.



- 24 -
7.07      The sensitivity of several of the major project risks was tested.
One major risk is that yield increases would not materialize as projected.
A 10% shortfall in projected yields would result in a 9% rate of return and
a 10% greater yield than projected would result in a 20% rate of return.
In the event of lower than projected yields, financial rates of return to
farmers would be reduced and fewer farms would participate. This would be
discerned by the project Monitoring Group (para 5.03) so that some reduction
in investment (e.g., through adjusting the rate of increase in the extension
component) could be effected. Another risk is that farmer participation in
the project would develop more slowly than projected. Lagging the projected
farmer participation (and hence the benefit stream) by two years reduces the
rate of return to 9%. Increasing total project costs in real terms by 10%
results in a 9% rate of return, increasing farm costs in real terms by 10%
gives a 12% rate of return.
7.08      The project would involve an investment cost per benefiting family
of US$867 for the directly-productive components and US$38 for the social
components. It would give rise to additional annual recurrent costs to
Government following the disbursement period of some US$2.3 million, partially
offset by additional tax revenues of US$0.7 million from the ICM (value added)
tax on incremental cotton production. The agricultural extension service
would constitute some two-thirds of the extra recurrent costs. The per family
costs are hi-gh, mainly due to the experimental nature of the project.  One
objective of the project would be to identify, test and spread more cost effec-
tive delivery systems for technical assistance and credit, to increase coverage
and decrease costs per participating family, thereby increasing the potential
for replicability throughout large parts of the Northeast.
7.09      The project has greater than usual risks stemming from the diffi-
cult physical environment of the project area and the interrelated and not
fully refined actions under the project, requiring procedural changes and
considerable coordination for their success. Success also depends on
responses of farmers and communities that involve subjective matters such
as attitudes to risk that are difficult to predict in advance. A major
drought during the project's first two or three years could get the
project off to a bad start. On the other hand, a number of the State-
level technicians who prepared the project would be heavily involved in
implementation, greatly improving the chances of proper coordination and
overall effectiveness. Also, steps have been taken to reduce the risks
of low farmer response by encouraging participation in the PROAGRO program
of crop-credit insurance and special monitoring of the credit supply. More-
over, the project has the potential of reaching some 75,000 people with its
directly productive component and 220,000 with its health component. Poten-
tially more important, it would provide experience with institutional ap-
proaches to small farmer problems that may be replicated elsewhere in the
Northeast.



- 25 -
VIII. RECOMIENDATIONS
8.01      During negotiations, assurances were obtained from the Government
that:
(a) It would proceed with Phase II only after a complete
review of Phase I, with progress satisfactory to the
Bank (para 4.03);
(b)  It would adequately support CEPA/RN to allow it to
effectively house the Project Unit (para 4.06);
(c) It would make annual reviews of the progress made in
expanding lending to small farmers in the project
area, present the report on such reviews and recom-
mendations for further action to the Bank, and carry
out the actions that may be agreed;
(d) PROAGRO voluntary crop-credit insurance would be
promoted for sub-loans under the project (para 4.14);
*e) The terms for the land purchase credits under POLONORDESTE
would apply to the project area (para 4.13);
(f) Bank approval would be secured for the project prepara-
tion work program and consultants' selection and terms
of reference (para 4.20);
(g) Sufficient local funds would be provided promptly as
needed to implement the project (para 4.22);
(h) Appropriate audit procedures would be followed by the
Project Unit and participating agencies (para 4.25); and
(i) The annual project work plan would be subject to the Bank
approval (para 5.04).
8.02      During negotiations assurances were obtained from the Government
of RN that it would support the project as outlined in para 5.05.
8.03      A condition of effectiveness would be that the Government and the
Project Unit had entered into formal agreements satisfactory to the Bank with
the participating banks (para 4.11).
8.04      Subject to the above assurances and conditions the project would be
suitable for a Bank loan of US$12.0 million equivalent with a term of 25 years
including a 5-year grace period.

NORTHEAST BRAZIL
RIO GRANDE DO NORTE RURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Project Area
General
1.        The project area covers 53 municipios, grouped in 4 disparate
sub-areas, representing a total area of about 2.1 million ha with a total
population of 415,000 (1970 Census). Most of the soils found in the area
are of Pre-cambrian origin and are usually poor. Other soil types can be
found on a small scale (especially the Jandaira limestone and Acu sandstone).
The relief of the area is variable with peneplains limited by high elevations.
In general the relief is characterized by flat or round-shaped hills with
short slopes having an average declivity between 3 and 10%. Altitudes range
from 50 to 300 meters. The project area is heterogeneous from several view-
points, as further explained below.
Climate
2.        Because of the Atlantic Equatorial air masses, the area has semi-
arid ,characteristics. Temperatures are high with an annual average around
27.4'C (maximum average about 36°C in December; minimum average about 24°C
in July). Average annual rainfall varies from 400 mm in the central part
to 700 mm in the western part of the project area. Annual rainfall is
extremely irregular, as evidenced by the value of the variation coefficient
(standard deviation t mean) which consistently exceeds .50 in the central
part of the area (between .40 and .50 in the western part). 1/ Rainfall is
often deficient for several months, causing severe droughts which may
persist over several crop seasons. The potential evapotranspiration,
calculated by Turc's formula, is rather uniform throughout the area, at about
1,500 mm per year.
3.        Analyzing the risk of drought is critical since insufficient rain-
fall results in reduced plant populations and yields for cotton and food
crops. Annual rainfall data are available for years since 1930 from ex-
perimerital stations in Cruzeta and Sao Miguel, located within the perennial
cotton region of the project area. With the assistance of INFAOL, this
information has been interpreted to show how annual variation in rainfall
affects the production of cotton. In the project area, rainfall must
exceed 100 mm in both March and April and 40 mm in May to ensure good yields
of mature cotton and permit good establishment of the newly planted crop.
As indicated below, risks of severe or critical drought are significant,
creating continual uncertainty regarding cotton production. However,
1/   Results of rainfall recording in several state stations for 35 to 55
years.

judging from conditions since 1930, adequate to good years for perennial
cotton have about a 65% probability of occurrence.
