Land Use
4.        Total cropland area is small (about 370,000 ha), less than 18% of
total land area (Table 1). Land for temporary crops (mainly ainual crops in
pure stand) averages only 3.7% of the total project area. Almost half of
the area is under natural pasture; the proportion of improved pasture is
negligible (less than 5,000 ha). Total fallow land is not precisely known,
but would not be more than 10% of the project area (210,000 ha) which would
be equivalent to almost 60% of crop land and more than 70% of land under
permanent crops. This fallow land would also probably represent an absolute
maximum of land which could be brought to cultivation. Considering the



ANNEX 1
Page 3
necessity of keeping a substantial amount of cultivable land for fallow
(between cycles of perennial cotton to avoid soil exhaustion), a maximum
possible increase in perennial cotton area would be in the order of 50,000 to
75,000 ha.  This is further substantiated by the pattern of land use according
to farm size (para 7 and Table 3).
5.        The land use pattern varies considerably between the four sub-areas.
Sub-area 4 (Santa Cruz) shows the largest proportion of cropland to total
land area (32%) while Sub-area 2 (Serido) has the lowest ratio (13%). The
proportion of temporary crops to total cropland area varies from 9% (Sub-area
1, Pedro Avelino) to 33% (Serido). The latter is probably due to a denser
system of food-crop cultivation in the riverbeds and around the numerous small
dams during the rainy season. This would obviously influence the degree of
emphasis on cotton cultivation, according to each farm's economic structure,
especially in small farms where there would be more dependence on subsistence
farming.
Land Distribution
6.        The three sources of data currently available are based on varying
definitions, rendering interpretation difficult. 1/ Table 2 summarizes the
respective farm distribution totals for the whole project area. There are
about 34,000 "establishments" (Census) or 25,000 "holdings" (Cadastral
1/   1970 Census:  defines establishments as units of one or more parcels,
contiguous and under a single administration, where agricultural activities
are carried out. Contiguous parcels under the same administration,
occupied under different legal conditions (owned, rented or squatted on)
are considered as one establishment; i.e., sharecroppers or owner-opera-.
tors on a unit of contiguous parcels are not differentiated. Conversely,
areas under the same administration, but not contiguous, are considered
as distinct establishments; e.g., a sharecropper on a plot not contiguous
to the owner's unit would constitute a separate establishment.
Cadastral Survey (INCRA-IBGE): defines rural holdings as units of one
or more parcels, contiguous, belonging to the same owner, irrespective
of whether or not they are under agricultural use.
SUDENE-IBRD (DRC) Survey: defines farms as a parcel or a collection of
close-lying parcels under the same entrepreneurship.
The census data generally represent a disaggregation of farms or rural
holdings, especially in the small-size category. The number of farms
may differ from the number of holdings due to rental arrangements, or
because the farm is comprised of more than one parcel, or again because
parcels of the same farm are not contiguous.



ANNEX 1
Page 4
Survey), or 18,000 "farms" (IBRD-SUDENE Survey). Because the differences in
number of "units" are most acute for the smaller areas, unit totals under
the 50 ha and above category can be expected to be almost the same for each
definition. Thus, there are approximately 6,500 to 7,000 units of more than
50 ha in the project area, or more than 85% of the total area under culti-
vation in the project area. Conversely, there would be about 12,000 "farms",
17,500 "holdings" and 28,000 "establishments" of less than 50 ha. The number
of "establishments" clearly overstates the actual number of units defined as
decision-making centers having direct impact on physical production at the
farm level, because these are actually parcelled units. On the other hand,
the "farm" definition may under-estimate the number of independent decision-
making units (e.g., tenants or sharecroppers). Hence, the number of 50 ha-
units or less intended for direct intervention may fall between 15,000 and
20,000.
7.        Most of the farms of more than 50 ha have at least 10 ha under
cultivation. They account for approximately 75% of the total cultivated
land in the area. A tentative estimate of land use according to farm size
a proportion in the project area indicates higher of land under cultivation
on small farms (reaching 45% in some instances; see Table 3), resulting in
less potential for the expansion of cropland.
8,        There are variations in farm size and proportion of small farms
between the four sub-areas of significance in designing systems of credit
and extension. Sub-areas 4 and 3 (Santa Cruz and Umarizal) have more small
farms and smaller-sized farms than the other sub-areas. Sub-area 1 (Pedro
Avelino) has fewer small farms, and larger-sized farms.
Population
9.        The total population of the project area was 415,000 in the 1970
Census, representing 26% of the State's total population. About 267,000,
equivalent to about 49,500 families and 64% of the total project area, were
defined as rural population. At the average annual growth rate of 1.2%, this
rural population would be about 280,000 for 1974, representing about 52,000
families (Table 4). Some interesting population features of the project area
are: (i) urban growth rate is more than double that of the rural population;
and (ii) there is a wide variation in growth rate among the four sub-areas'
rural populations (Table 4). There are also wide sub-regional differences
in the ratio of rural to total population, as well as in the density of
population per square km.
Agricultural Labor
10.       The main source of information on the project area is the 1973
SUDENE-IBRD (DRC) Survey. The total agricultural labor force of the project
area is estimated at about 115,000 men-equivalent, about 40% of which would
be family workers on owner-operator units (Table 5). A striking feature of
the project area is the large number of sharecroppers and temporary workers.
Under the tentative assumption of 2.2 men-equivalent per family, there would



ANNEX 1
Page 5
be 52,100 rural families in the project area, consistent with the census
data (para 9), and over 20,000 owner-operator families and 10,000 share-
cropper families, and about 19,000 temporary worker families. These figures
should be matched against the respective number of "farms": Census, 34,000;
Cadastral Survey, 25,000; and the SUDENE-IBRD Survey, 18,000.
11.       Large proportional differences exist among the four sub-areas'
worker classes. Whereas Sub-areas 3 and 4 (Umarizal and Santa Cruz) evidence
a large ratio of family workers (43%) to sharecroppers, Sub-area 1 (Pedro
Evelino) has the largest percentage of sharecroppers and temporary workers, and
the least family workers (30%). This again is consistent with the land
distribution pattern described in para 8.
Performance-Income Levels
12.       Based on the SUDENE-IBRD/DRC Survey, gross receipts originate
mainly from crops (from 65 to 80%), the balance originating from livestock.
Variations exist between farm classes, but variations between sub-areas are not
known. Receipts from non-farm operations are almost negligible. Purchased
inputs (not including hired labor) are few and represent from 10 to 30% of
total costs. Performance indices indicate a markedly larger output per ha
on smaller farms, while labor productivity is substantially lower on small
farms than on larger ones. Wide variations in all performance indices appear
to result from (non-) availability of institutional credit. Table 6 gives
a summary of selected performance indices by farm size for 1973.
13.       The target group would correspond to farm  family income levels
below.about US$1,010 in 1973 (and US$1,145 in 1974). 1/ Over 90% of the
potential beneficiaries of the project fall within this group. Per family
incomes of farm families on less than 200 hectares averaged US$515, less
than one-fifth the national average of US$3,060. About 95% families with
0 to 50 hectares fall within the target group, the average family income
of this group being $375. About 70% of the farm families on 50 to 200
hectares have incomes below the poverty line; low incomes on these farms
is caused by the small percentage of cultivated area, drought conditions
and disincentives to invest with existing sharecropping relationships, and

Sector Policy Paper on Rural Development, February 1975).

low level of penetration of institutional credit.  The vast majority of the
landless workers, especially temporeary workers, would also fall within the
target group. 1/
Target Population
14.       Some characteristics of the project area have important implica-
tions in the identification of target groups:
(a) About 35% of the area's rural population are temporary
workers, defined as landless people, probably without
permanent residence. There is little hope at reaching
these people directly through the kind of intervention
envisaged under the project, although some indirect
benefits can be anticipated through increased employment
opportunities (e.g., hired labor for expanded agricul-
tural production), improved land purchase credit terms
and improved health services. Immediate direct action
for this eligible target group would be extremely
difficult in the absence of structural reforms, for which
satisfactory executing capacity does not yet exist.
(b) Sharecroppers, representing almost 20% of the total rural
population, will also be difficult to reach directly
by the project. However, some impact may be envisaged
through selected activities especially extension. The
other main input of the project--the direct delivery of
credit services which acts as a strong incentive for the
adoption of improved technology--would probably be
negligible in view of the existing tenure problems;
(c) Therefore, 55% of the "eligible" target population is
likely to be only partially or indirectly affected by
project intervention; and
(d) Of the 45% remaining, the large proportion of larger
farmers, about 5,00U families (10%) of the rural popula-
tion) are considered a secondary target group.
15.       Therefore, the directly "reachable" target population would be about
15,000 families representing less than 30% of the total rural population and
less than 20% of the total project area population. These families would
be mostly small owner-operators. Under the project, it would be unrealistic
to assume a 100% rate of penetration; the project credit cormponent would
probably reach only from 9,000 to 12,000 families.
1/   Several farms below 200 ha will certainly evidence much higher average
income levels. This, however, is consistent with the Bank policy which
acknowledges that Rural Development projects will characteristically
include non-target group farmers.



ANNEX 1
Page 7
Implications for Project Implementation Strategy
16.       The preceding observations have some bearing on project implemen-
tation strategies:
(a) The "primary" target population is relatively small compared
to the total project area population;
(b) Little is known about several population groups which
could be eligible for direct intervention, especially
the temporary workers and sharecroppers;
(c) The prospects for an overall expansion of the area under
cultivation for perennial cotton are limited, probably
to a maximum of 75,000 ha, or a 35% increase over the
present level of cultivation. Moreover, labor require-
ments for clearing of these new areas appear to be large,
about 4 million man-days, or 15% of the agricultural
manpower available annually;
(d) Prospects for area expansion for cotton appear to be more
limited among small farmers, due to high proportion of
land already under cultivation. For the small farmer to
expand he would probably have to rent land or use sharecropping;
(e) Finally, and possibly most importantly, there are wide
differences between the 4 sub-areas of the project area
in physical characteristics (size, geology, climate) and
in population characteristics, land distribution, land
use and cropping intensity. This is further compounded
by infrastructural differences, and large variations in
the nature, intensity and quality of the delivery systems
available to farmers in these sub-areas (extension coverage,
bank and credit coverage and marketing facilities; see
Annex 6). This suggests the need for adapting project
interventions to the specific conditions of each sub-area.

NORTHEAST BRAZIL
RIO GRANDE do NORTE RURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
A. Agricultural Extension
I. Existing Situation
Background: Agricultural Extension in the Northeast
1.        The situation of agricultural extension in the State and the
project area reflects the general situation of extension services in the
Northeast. 1/ A relatively large number of separate institutions are involved
in technical assistance to agriculture: universities, agricultural research
centers, state secretariats of agriculture and private institutions such as
cooperatives and large agro-industries. The largest input of technical assist-
ance, however, has come from the Brazilian system of rural extension, composed
of a central federal institution, ABCAR (Brazilian Association of Credit and
Rural Assistance), and 23 non-profit, technically and administratively autono-
mous entities in the various states, known as the ANCARes in the states of
the Northeast (ANCAR/RN for Rio Grande do Norte). The effectiveness of the
extension system has been limited by lack of coordination of its work with
other extension activities, lack of long-term objectives and planning, and
financial instability of the executing agencies at the State level.
2.        The instability and short term nature of their sources of financing
has been a major obstacle to the formulation and implementation of long-term
work programs objectives and by the ANCARes of the Northeast.  The ANCARes
obtain their financing mainly trom-the Federal Government, supplemented by
service contracts with local institutions, banks, states and municipalities.
The Federal Government has contributed 50% to 60% of the financing of tech-
nical assistance in the past, channelled through the Ministry of Agriculture
and various other Federal institutions, such as ABCAR, INCRA, SUDENE, Banco
do Brasil and Banco do Nordeste. State and Municipal governments establish
contracts directly with the ANCARes and contribute to their support according
to their financial strength. (For ANCAR/RN, the State government provides approx
approximately 20% of financing, while municipal contribution is about 10%)
In addition, the ANCARes obtain resources from the banking system through
their assistance in programs of supervised agricultural credit; (2% of the
outstanding interest is set aside for this purpose).
3.        The extension agencies in the Northeast, as in the rest of Brazil,
were originally conceived as services oriented to small and medium-sized
farmers, providing technical assistance and supervised rural credit. Farmers
1/   See "Rural Development Issues and Options in Northeast Brazil," IBRD
Report No. 665a-BR, June 23, 1975.



ANNEX 2
Page 2
were assisted individually and, due to the large number of small farmers and
the size of their holdings, the number reached and the impact on production
was negligible. As a result, the extension service gradually shifted into
providing technical assistance to larger producers and ceased to give credit
directly, supporting instead the credit programs administered by the banking
system. At present, the bulk of technical assistance for agricultural pro-
duction is being directed to larger farmers. In addition, to the extent that
the effectiveness of agricultural extension depends fundamentally on the
availability of technological packages appropriate to the specific conditions
of various types of farmers, the virtual non-existence of a research effort
directed at small farmers' problems contributes to a further neglect of small
farmers. Besides an indisputable need for additional staff, the training of
existing staff has lacked the content and breadth to effectively disseminate
agricultural technology. Major deficiencies have been insufficient knowledge
of recent technological innovations and lack of knowledge of practical aspects
of application of technology, (such as farm budgeting and assessment of profit-
ability of technological recommendations under local input/output price rela-
tionships.
Recent Developments
4.        The Government has already taken steps to strengthen the extension
services, through the creation of EMBRATER (Brazilian Enterprise for Technical
Assistance and Rural Extension) in June 1974, thereby complementing its
efforts on the research side with the creation of EMBRAPA (Brazilian Agri-
cultural Research Enterprise) in 1973. EMBRATER is an autonomous entity of
the Ministry of Agriculture, whose main purpose is to formulate, stimulate
and coordinate programs for technical assistance, in close harmony with
the research activities of EMBRAPA, to achieve the Government's production
objectives in the agricultural sector. 1/ EMBRATER will exercise its control-
ling and coordinating power over the extension services operating in the
various states by centralizing all Federal budgetary allocations for technical
assistance.
5.        Simultaneously, the Government has created a National Commission
for Agricultural Research and Technical Assistance (COMPATER) with the role
of reviewing and coordinating the programs of both EMBRAPA and EMBRATER.
This reflects the Government's determination to make research and extension
more effective and production-oriented.
6.        At the State level, the new organizational structure will mainly
ensure a more coordinated and regular supply of Federal funding. The State
ANCAR will remain entirely responsible for the execution of the extension
function.
1/   EMBRATER differs from EMBRAPA in that it coordinates and funds, at the
Federal level, but does not execute the extension functions, which remain
the State's responsibility.



ANNEX 2
Page 3
RN Extension Facilities
7.        The State level extension service (ANCAR/RN) operates 52 field units
providing services for a total 100 of the 150 municipios in the State. For
the whole State, there are about 150 extension workers of higher (82) or
intermediate (69) levels. More than 60% of the higher level extensionists
are either in the State office in Natal (36) or in the 4 regional offices (16),
leaving only 30 in the local operating units. Most of the intermediate level
extensionists (64) are located in the operating units (Table 1).
The Project Area
8.        Presently there are 22 operating units (agencies) in the project
area, providing a largely insufficient administrative coverage of the area. 1/
At the field level, there are 12 extensionists of a higher level and 26 inter-
mediate level extensionists. However, 8 of the latter are "social" extensionists
who are mostly involved in administering a community health program. The agri-
cultural extension work force in the project area, therefore, amounts to 30
agricultural advisers.1/ The actual geographical distribution of these
advisers indicates that 29 municipios (of the 41 serviced) actually receive
extension services.
9.        On the basis of the number of farms located in these municipios,
probably no more than 2,500 farms (most of them being farms with more than
50 ha) actually receive assistance. 3/ Hence present coverage and the needs
for the project area can tentatively be summarized as follows:
1/   The 22 operating units are responsible for the 22 municipios where
they are located, and for 19 additional municipios. Ten municipios
have their own agency, without any dependent neighboring municipio,
while the 12 other agencies assist 19 municipios, 4 of which are located
outside of the Project area. Four municipios depend on an ANCAR agency
located outside of the Project area. Twelve municipios are not covered
by the existing extension network.
2/ In this Annex, agricultural extension workers are referred to as
Agricultural Advisers, divided into Agricultural Extensionists (an
adviser of relatively high level, typically an "Engenheiro Agronomo")
and Technical Advisers (or extension worker of an intermediate level,
typically a graduate from a technical agricultural school).
3/ Tables 2 and 3 show the distribution of the actual extension coverage
by sub-area of the project area. In the 29 municipios, (representing
60% of the project area) there are approximately 4,200 farms of more
than 50 ha. The technique most widely diffused by ANCAR/RN, the use
of improved seeds of cotton, was diffused to only 3,100 farmers in the
whole state in 1973.



ANNEX 2
Page 4
Number of farms    Number of farms
Number of                of more             of less
Municipios              than 50 ha         than 50 ha
Total                    53                     7,000            15-20,000
Effectively Assisted    29                      2,500            Negl.
Not Assisted             24                     4,500            15-20,000
10.       There are wide differences in the degree of extension coverage in
each sub-area (80% to 50%). However, in the 29 municipios actually covered,
Sub-areas 3 and 4 (Umarizal and Santa Cruz) appear relatively better served,
especially when the number of agricultural advisers is related to the number
of holdings of more than 50 ha.
Present Organization of Extension Work
11.       On average, an agricultural adviser spends 80% of his time on
administrative duties (50% in assisting farmers and banks with loan applica-
tion and administration, 30% in registering data for several institutions).
This leaves him 20% of his time for actual agricultural extension. Because
of the institutional nature of the official extension service (para 3),
extension work is often considered a secondary task and, in fact, the agri-
cultural advisor is free to organize it as he wants, using the teaching
methods, and general (State-level conceived) materials as he sees fit. In
this respect, he gets little assistance from ANCAR/RN. The periodic training
sessions and guidelines are mainly related to administrative duties. Tech-
nical knowledge is very general, is not adapted to the particular farmer's
problems, nor is it upgraded in training.
II. Extension Arrangements under the Project
Introduction: Aproach
12.       In view of the general situation of agricultural extension, a sub-
stantial reorientation of extension activities is required if agricultural
technology is to be delivered effectively to large numbers of small farmers.
Four guiding principles provide the basis for the actions which the project
would propose to undertake with respect to agricultural extension:
a.   Given the complex nature of the area and the target popula-
tion to be reached by the extei.sion service, there does not
exist a unique answer as to the most appropriate functional
system of extension to be used in the project area. Different
kinds of recipient groupings, extensionists' backgrounds,
technical packages, and delivery systems should be tested.



ANNEX 2
Page 5
b.   The extension service should focus more of its efforts
on transmitting available progressive agricultural practices
and input packages to small farmers, moving from the past
nearly complete concentration on credit assistance for
larger farmers. At the outset, attention could center
around specific problems felt by small farmers concerning
economically important crops, focussing upon aspects where
the potential for early, visible results would be greatest;
c.   There should be very close coordination between the
extension service and the other agencies having an
impact on farmers' production because of their responsi-
bility in: (i) development of viable technical packages
(EMBRAPA, INFAOL); (ii) supply of technical inputs at
reasonable costs (CIDA); (iii) provision of agricultural
credit (BB, BNB); and (iv) access to marketing channels
(gins, cooperatives).
-d.   The extension service must attempt to work with groups
rather than with individuals to increase potential coverage,
lower administrative costs per family and encourage group
formation for other activities.
13.       The project includes a first phase extending over three years, which
would be mainly utilized for testing various alternatives of extension delivery
systems in a number of selected municipios of the project area. The testing
of alternative approaches to reach larger number of small farmers through
groups rather than individuals would receive special emphasis. The main ob-
jective of the project would be to re-orient extension services towards the
selected target groups through modifications of the agricultural advisers'
attitude and work routines, complemented by a major effort in training.
14.       The alternatives to be tested would be selected on the basis of the
local circumstances (a) creating a full coverage in places where nothing pre-
sently exists; (b) modifying part or all of what currently exists; or (c) ex-
panding part of the existing network where appropriate.
15.       The target groups which the extension system would be requested
to reach include: (a) large farmers, a part of whom are currently receiving
almost the whole of the existing extension service; (b) small holders, almost
totally ignored under the present extension system; and (c) sharecroppers,
also entirely left out under the present system, except via the larger land-
owners on whose farms they work.
16.       Several types of individuals could be responsible for actually
carrying out the bulk of the extension work, irrespective of their belong-
ing to the formal institutional system: (a) The Agricultural Extensionist



ANNEX 2
Page 6
(a "engenhiero agronomo," with a BSc. from an agricultural college), the
individual currently carrying out most of the task of extension, mainly
directed at large farmers; (b) the Technical Adviser, "tecnico agricola,"
a graduate from an agricultural high school; (c) a Community Development
Officer, who would have responsibilities extending largely beyond pure
extension work, and whose activities would have a welfare impact; (d) a
Village Agricultural Assistant, a locally selected agricultural agent, who
could be a part time agent; and (e) a Farmers' Representative who would serve
as an intermediary between a technical adviser and a group of no more than
12 farmers, with responsibilities in the technical credit and marketing
fields. The bulk of agricultural extension work would be expected to fall
on the Technical Advisers.
Coverage
17.       Since backgrounds of agricultural extensionists and technical advisers
differ, some specialization in their responsibilities to target farm families
would probably be advisable. Various organizational alternatives would be
tested to combine the skills and experiences of different categories of exten-
sionists. Decisions made in various Phase I experimental areas regarding the
organization of extensionists and the definition of their responsibilities
will affect the nature of coverage and the number reached by extension. In
designing each extension unit, decisions would include: (a) whether different
classes of extensionists would be responsible for different geographical areas,
different functions within an extension team, or different farm sizes; (b)
whether formal or informal teams of extensionists would be established with
more experienced, highly skilled agricultural extensionists acting as specialist
resources for two to four technical advisers working in the same subarea;
(c) how the scope of the extensionist's work will be defined, and what will be
the priorities in time allocation among technical assistance for various crops
and activities, group training, credit promotion, data collection, marketing,
administration, and travel time; if the proportion of time to be spent on
technical assistance or group formation is to increase, credit procedures must
be simplified and bank staffs must assume a more active role in loan process-
ing; (d) with how many farmers the extensionist or extension team would work;
to what extent farmers will be grouped for some of the extension sessions and
to what extent farmer leaders and demonstrators will be used to expand the
potential coverage, lower costs per family and increase possibilities for
effective impact of extension efforts; (e) what the pace of introducing tech-
nical changes should be; (f) and whether extensionists would focus on one con-
crete practice geared to the specific needs in the crop cycle or introduce
several complementary suggestions at once.
18.       Because little is known about the potential rate of group formation
among farmers and the prospects for development of "base-line" extension
echelons (Development Officers, Village Assistants and Farmers' Representa-
tives), initial actions of extension service might be directed toward in-
dividual farmers. In these cases, each adviser would be assigned a specific
number of farm families. For instance, agricultural extensionists might be



ANNEX 2
Page 7
expected to assist up to 120 larger farmers, with specifications that 70%
would fall in the 50 to 200 ha range. Technical advisers might be assigned
120 small farmers each. Several alternative combinations of agricultural
advisers working with village assistants, community officers or farmers
representatives would be tested concurrently to determine how overall coverage
by the extension retwork can best be increased. Once organizational alterna-
tives have been selected, extensionists and regional coordinators could de-
velop specific job descriptions for different classes of extensionists de-
scribing the desired qualifications in terms of experience, knowledge and
attitudes. A description of the responsibilities of each extension worker
could indicate: (a) the number of farmers and other extension workers that
the extensionist is to assist; (b) suggested allocation of time between tech-
nical, credit, organizational and administrative functions; (c) number and
purposes of farm visits; (d) overall objectives sought in terms of farm incomes
and production levels; (e) meetings, training sessions and demonstrations to
be organized; (f) liaison and communication functions; .(g) specialist advice
to specific groups. Such description would require frequent updating as ex-
perience is gained.
Implementation and Organization
19.       The project would be implemented in two phases:  (a) Phase I would
concern a limited area (11 municipios) providing the testing grounds for
the alternatives selected, over a period of 3 years; and (b) Phase II would
concern the whole project area and would be initiated after a large scale
evaluation of phase I. ANCAR/RN would be responsible for the extension
functions under the project. ANCAR/RN would create a special unit within
its organization to plan and implement the extension actions to be under-
taken in the project area, in close liaison with the Project Unit Extension
Adviser (Annex 12). Jointly with the Extension Adviser, ANCAR/RN would
define the precise alternatives to be tested, depending upon the specific
circumstances of the selected municipios. 1/
20.       The project would increase the total number of agricultural advisers
to 136 over 5 years in the whole project area, from the present level of 38
(from 12 to 36 agricultural extensionists and from 26 to 100 technical advisers).
Requirements for Phase I
21.       For the 11 selected municipios, the requirements for the first
operational phase would be 7 agricultural extensionists and 26 technical
advisers. The administrative support staff requirement would be 18. Taking
into account the personnel currently employed in the agencies existing in the
11 municipios, the incremental requirements would be: 5 agricultural
extensionists, 22 technical advisers and 13 administrative support staff
1/   Several alternative systems of logistical support for extension workers
would also have to be tested, especially with respect to the use of
vehicles (to be shared with social extensionists).



ANNEX 2
Page 8
(Table 4). The overall number of farms expected to receive extension would
be roughly 3,900 units, 25% of which (about 1,000) would be farms of more than
50 ha. This probably would correspond to a coverage of 70% of all farms
(as defined by the Cadastral Survey). Since the number of small farms to
be reached by technical advisers is considered rather conservative (120 per
T.A.) using the assumption of individual coverage, the actual number of small
farms assisted could be much larger depending upon the degree of success of
group formation.
22.       The relatively small number of additional personnel required suggests
that phasing would be unnecessary and all the agricultural advisers could
start their functions at the same time. Over and above the field staff there
would be the need for at least two Regional Coordinators (one for Serido
and one for Umarizal). The Regional Coordinators would be agricultural
extensionists with considerable experience. Optimum coverage by the field
extensionists would be expected to take place after two. years. During the
first year, this coverage would be about 50%.
Requirements for Phase II
23.       The expansion to the rest of the project area would require an
estimated 6 regional coordinators, 19 agricultural extensionists, 42 tech-
nical advisers, and 47 administrative support staff. New coordinating and
field units would be created to provide for an adequate geographical coverage.
The incremental requirement in units and personnel are estimated as follows:
Present
Situation     Required      Increment
Field Units                            18            27              9
Coordinating Units                      -             2              2
Regional Coordinators                   -             6              6
Agricultural Extensionists             10            29             19
Technical Advisers                     22            74             42
Administrative Personnel               15            62             47
24.       Part of the staff trained under Phase I would transfer to the Phase
II area.  The remaining experienced staff would train new staff within the
Phase I municipios and would continue to participate in refining actions
under the project. Part of the new management style would be an attempt to
have a transfer and promotion policy based on the extent to which extension
field staff actually succeed in improving the livelihood of target farmers.
Performance would not be rated on "thousands of Cruzeiros of credit extended",
nor on mere numbers of farmers contacted, but on the actual content and
effectiveness of a person's work. The human management approach would be
facilitated through constant contact between the regional supervisors and
the field units and by frequent group meetings of extension workers to
evaluate and plan actions.



ANNEX 2
Page 9
Work Organization
25.       The work of the agricultural advisor would be scheduled according
to:  (a) the timely transmission of technical informat'.on, which should
precede the farmer's decisions concerning the following crop cycle; (b) the
adequate application of the techniques taught to the farmers, implying
close linkages with the farmer's work.  W4hile the major emphasis would be
expected to be on technical assistance to the farmer, the agricultural advisor
would devote a substantial amount of time to: (i) liaising closely with the
research and experimentation personnel responsible for the elaboration of
technical packages, especially the INFAOL staff responsible for on-farm trials
(Annex 4); (ii) participating in the organization of input delivery and
marketing; (iii) collecting limited relevant information for monitoring
and evaluation purposes; and (iv) his own training (para 27). 1/ The actual
organization of the adviser's workload would be defined by the Regional
Coordinator jointly with the Project Unit Extension Adviser. It would be
reviewed periodically and subject to adjustments. During Phase I, the exten-
sion service would identify successful existing practices and extend these
practical innovations to less progressive farmers. The resulting ad hoc
technical packages would help orient Phase I research efforts and farm trials
to developing detailed technical packages appropriate to specific sub-areas.
Also, farmers would be motivated to participate if extensionists focus their
attention on problems critical to the farmers and if farmers can see some
visible production and income benefits. During Phase I, the extensionists
would build up their coverage gradually. If teams of extensionists were
formed, they might dissiminate one or two key farming practices to participa-
ting farm families throughout a homegeneous sub-region, with extension team
members exchanging experiences. In areas where social cohesion or peer in-
fluence is strong, extensionists might try to get small or medium scale farm
families to participate as local demonstrators of the benefits of credit and
improved technical practices, influencing neighboring farmers to adopt the
new methods (e.g. plant dates, input utilization, intensity of planting,
weeding). During Phase II, the orientation and extension process would be
enriched using technical, didactic and organizational findings attained during
Phase I. Technical packages would have been refined for different areas and
adapted to individual and group communication methods. The scope of exten-
sionists work may be expanded to increase emphasis on diversified rural econ-
omic activities, marketing and farm budget assistance; however, ANCAR and the
Project Unit may decide that it remains more critical that agricultural ex-
tensionists concentrate on seeing that new techniques are used correctly.
1/   The agricultural adUiser's work should be largely based on direct con-
tact with farmers through field visits. Permanent recording of farmer's
performance and progress should be made through the establishment of in-
dividual files. The training of farmers should include field demonstra-
tion sessions. Audio-visual aids for the training of groups should be
used (Annex 7).



ANNEX 2
Page 10
Recruitment of Agricultural Advisors
26.       Agricultural extensionists generally would be graduates from four-
year agricultural colleges such as Mossoro (RN), Areia (Paraiba) or Ceara.
Current salary levels of ANCAR/RN appear sufficiently attractive that the incre-
mental requirements for agricultural extensionists under the project could be
met with graduates from these colleges. The technical advisers would typically
be graduates from the agriculturai highschools of the state. In 1973, 220
students graduated from these schools and 500 graduates are predicted for
1980. Additional staff requirements under the project, both for agricultural
extension (ANCAR-RN) and farm-trials (INFAOL, see Annex 4) could therefore,
be satisfied. Recruitment of Community Development Officers and Village
Agricultural Assistants should be the subject of investigations by the Project
Unit Extension Coordinator and Human Resources Coordinator (Annex 12). Pre-
ference would be given to technical advisers and agricultural extensionists
having field experience with local crops and conditions, specifically in tech-
nical assistance to small or medium scale farmers. Also, recruitment should
seek extensionists motivated to work with small farmers, try new approaches
and use good judgement and vision.
Training
27.       In view of the projects' objective to re-orient existing extension
services towards greater coverage of small farmers combined with the need for
considerable additional staff, the project would place heavy emphasis on the
training of extension personnel. Training would be subject to various experi-
ments especially during Phase I of the project. Training would be: (a)
established on a permanent basis; (b) performed from each echelon down; and
(c) subject to continuous evaluation. Training would consist of an initial
phase (pre-service) in which the agricultural advisor would become familiar-
ized with the project and his new operating environment, and a regular phase
(in-service) in which the advisor is upgraded on a permanent basis. An annual
training program, subject to periodical review, would be established jointly
by the Project Unit Extension Adviser, the Human Resources Adviser, and Senior
staff of ANCAR/RN.
28.       At the beginning of the project, ANCAR/RN would schedule a two month
training period for the extensionists, beginning with one month of formal
training during which the new objectives and operational approaches of the
agricultural extension effort within the project would be emphasized. Training
would focus on the needs of small and medium scale farmers, the attitude
changes among extensionists and innovative, result-oriented approaches to
reach small-scale farmers. At this time, extensionists or extension groups
receive the list of farm families whom they would assist. The next three
weeks could be spent in contacting the farmers, explaining the general objec-
tives of the program, getting a sense of their needs and conditions, deriving
a set of existing, improved practices (e.g. animal traction, spacing, sales)
and impediments to adoption of those practices; extensionists could also



ANNEX 2
Page 11
attempt to assess the influence patterns among farmers to analyze possibili-
ties for organizing groups and involving progressive farmers in field trials
and demonstrators. At the end of the month of field contacts, extensionists
could receive one more week of training. During the wrap-up sessions, ANCAR/RN
could suggest guidelines for the extensionists' programs and allocation of
their time among technical, credit, administrative, coordinating and informa-
tion - gathering functions. Extensionists would be encouraged to develop
alternative schemes for fulfilling these responsibilities, testing farmers'
responses to different suggestions and paces of introducing change. At the
wrap-up session and in subsequent bi-weekly or monthly meetings, extensionists
could discuss progress and present specific action plans. Training would
emphasize specific technical practices, group organization, and use of appro-
priate communication tools (e.g. extension aids, use of demonstrations, spe-
cific technical packages, use of media, small group and individual farmer
training); and discussion of attitudes and delivery systems effective in
reaching and motivating the target population.
Monitoring and Evaluation
29.       The overall impact of the agricultural extension service should
be measured in terms of the ultimate project objectives, i.e., increased
production and increased income for farmers. Monthly meetings and reports
would yield sensitive measurements of the impact at the field level. Farmers'
reactions and solutions to problems encountered would give valuable clues as to
the necessary adjustments to the project actions. The build-up of a team
spirit between the extension network and the Monitoring Group would be essential
for the Monitoring Group to have access to, and to discuss data and problems
with, the extension staff at all levels without being seen as a controlling body.
Self evaluation and adjustment would be built into the system through a series
of regular meetings, performance reviews action plans and progress reports
combined with encouragement of local initiative to make changes as needed.
30.       The various extension methods would be observed and analyzed by
Regional Coordinators, the Project Unit Extension Adviser, the Human Resources
Adviser, and Senior ANICAR/RN staff. Changes and adjustments would be sug-
gested when appropriate.
31.       Sources for Community Development Officers, Village Assistants and/or
Farmer's Representatives, as well as eventual remuneration levels, supervision
systems; community reactions and the possible costs and benefits would be re-
fined during Phase I. The Monitoring Group would follow the progress of these
actions and recommend whether and precisely how to proceed with Phase II.
CU sts
32.       Total costs of agricultural extension over the five year develop-
rent period are estimated at Cr$30.8 million (US$4.3 million). Salaries
(including fringe benefits and social charges) would represent more than



ANNEX 2
Page 12
60% of total costs (Cr$18.9 million). Equipment (including 150 vehicles)
would amount to Cr$10.2 million, and the remaining Cr$1.7 million (US$238,000)
would be for training.
33.       Project costs for the extension component are estimated at Cr$23.9
million (US$3.3 million) of which close to 60% (Cr$14.0 million) would
correspond to the Phase I area (Annex 10, Table 2). Because the Phase I
area (11 municipios) would provide the testing and training grounds for
project actions during the whole project, with a corresponding human invest-
ment which would not otherwise take place and with a large modification in
the quality of the (small) staff currently located in the Phase I area, all
costs for this area are included as project costs. For the Phase II area,
however, only incremental costs (including salaries) are included in total
project costs.
34.       The annual recurrent costs at the end of the five-year development
period would amount to approximately Cr$10.8 million (US$1.5 million) about
Cr$8.0 million over the estimated current level of annual expenditure for the
project area.

B. Community Action - Social and Welfare Assistance
Background
35.       The agricultural extension actions under the project recognize that
assistance to farmers' groups would be more cost-effective than individual
coverage and would reach a larger number of small farmers. This assumes
that some degree of productively-oriented farmers' organization can be achieved.
36.       However, small farmer organizations, in the Northeast in general,
and in the project area, are either weak or non-existent. There is a tradi-
tion of farmer individualism and limited community development, and organiza-
tion of farmers is complicated by the diversity of land tenure arrangements.
In addition, small farmers in the project area are numerous and geographically
dispersed.
37. To date, the only attempts towards some form of rural organization
have been directed at the development of community actions for social and
welfare purposes. The successes in social mobilization have been due largely
to the quality of the staff involved, in spite of their limited number and
resources. A body of highly motivated and dedicated women (ANCAR-RN, FSESP
and others) has been responsible for promoting the development of health so-
cieties (miniposts) and other social services (Annex 5).
38.       A prerequisite for the establishment of farmers' organizations rele-
vant to farmers in the technical and possibly credit fields, is the promotion
and development of community involvement for productive purposes. While
agricultural advisers would undertake to promote the formation of farmers
groups, attempts would be made to develop and strengthen community involve-
ment and social mobilization for educational and productive purposes. To
that effect, the existing structures of the social welfare institutions would
be largely used, and the existing high quality personnel would provide the
necessary initial impetus. Close working relationships between agricultural
and social extension personnel of ANCAR/RN would be expected to develop at
the field unit level.
Present Situation
39.       As one of the major institutions involved in social welfare,
through its social extension unit, the extension service (ANCAR/RN) largely
develops and operates the health minipost network.  Social extension workers
(30 in the state, 8 in the project area) are active at the minipost level,
but also at the primary school level (state and municipal). Programs and
activities of ANCAR/RN social extension unit are thus mainly concentrated
on nutrition, health and education.



ANNEX 2
Page 18
40.       Activities at the minipost level include non-medical operational
support and supervision of community health workers (Annex 5). At the
school level, social extension workers assist in health education to
teachers, parents and students: water purification, water supply, vac-
cination, nutrition, etc. Participation in nutrition-oriented activities
include improvement of school lunches, establishment of kitchen gardens
and orchards, training of parents and children in vegetable/fruit growing,
etc. Education related activities include participation in school con-
struction and repairs, assistance to PTA's, 4H clubs and health clubs,
short training in handicrafts, etc.
41.       Most of these activities are carried out within the context of
"Projeto Integrado de Educacao para Saude" (Integrated Health Education
Project), a community education program created in 1973 by the State Secretariat
of Education and administered by ANCAR/RN through contract. The main objectives
of this program are: (a) integration of the school in the community; (b) devel-
opment of educational programs in health and nutrition; (c) increased partici-
pation of local leadership, groups and existing services; and (d) provision of
training in agricultural and health activities to older school children (14
years and older).
42.       Under this program, rural school teachers are trained to carry
out the health and nutrition education component together with social
extension workers and community health workers. In addition, training
includes courses and demonstration in vegetable production and small animal
raising.
43.       In 1974, the overall program involved 250 schools in the State
(with an estimated 18,000 children). Approximately 1,300 families started
a small kitchen garden (800 families were estimated to have increased
their consumption of vegetables) and 150 persons had been trained in
vegetable production.
The Project
44.       The project would intensify the community education and participation
efforts in the project area through increased involvement of social extension
workers and school teachers. Reduced medical supervision of community health
workers (which would increasingly be the responsibility of auxiliary nurses,
Annex 5) would permit greater participation of social extension workers
in community development activities. 1/ The integrated community education
project would be supported and expanded during Phase I of the Project, by
the addition of 5 social extension workers and the training of 100 school
teachers in nutrition and health education, and community action.
1/   In the social extension worker's schedule, health related activities
would not be necessarily separated from school/community development
related actions. Therefore, no division of tasks would be necessary
within ANCAR/RN.



ANNEX 2
Page 19
Demonstration Units
45. To translate concretely the education efforts undertaken at the
community level, demonstration units for vegetable/fruit growing and
raising of small animals would be established. Such units would be placed
at a central point in the community, preferably close to the school or
the minipost. One caretaker per unit would be trained during a week (costs
are Cr$ 750/caretaker) and training would be held in the agricultural
schools or at already established units. All social extension workers
would also receive training in working with the demonstration units for
3 days (Cost:  Cr$ 900/participant for 16 extensionists).
46.       A total of 50 demonstration units would be established in the
11 Phase I municipios. Phasing would take place as follows:
Number of Demonstration Units in Phase I Municipios
Year            1         2          3         Total
Goat units                3          4          3          10
Rabbit units              4          6         5           15

Vegetable plots           5          5         5           15
47.       The establishment cost per unit would be expected to vary from
Cr$ 500 (vegetable garden) to Cr$ 1,500 (goats, sheep), as detailed in Table 1.
48.       Credit Fund.  A rotating credit fund (Cr$ 550,000) would be established,
to provide credit in kind (e.g. seeds or young animals) to families interested
in complementing their own home production. Repayments would also be made
in kind. This system is currently operating very satisfactorily in some
communities for cattle and bean seeds under youth clubs programs.
49.       After a demonstration unit has been established in a community,
an estimated 15 families would be expected to participate during the first
year and ask for credit. In subsequent years this amount could rise to 20,
then 30 families. At the end of the first phase about 1,880 families could
have participated in the program.
50.       Monitoring and Evaluation.  The Monitoring Group would follow the
response of participants to this type of program. Major items of enquiry
would be: (a) the effects of the program on the family in terms of income,
labor, debt repayment, and nutrition; (b) the organization of the program
(extension worker, teacher, community health worker, CDO, teleclub) and
its efficiency.



ANNEX 2
Page 20
51.       Once the experiment has been developed into a replicable model,
this program could be extended to other areas.
52.       Costs.  The project would cover the costs over 3 years, estimated
at Cr$ 757,000 (US$105,000), covering the training of social extension
workers, school teachers and caretakers, the construction of the demon-
stration units and the rotating credit fund.
