ANNEX 2
Appendix 1
The role of the technical adviser with respect to credit
1.        One of the objectives of the proposed modifications to the existing
credit delivery system is to simplify procedures. Under the new procedures,
the technical advisor would still be a key element in the system, but the
time required of him would be reduced. His primary resronsibility with
respect to credit would be to: (a) provide investment advice; (b) promote
credit use; (c) help farmers prepare their applications; (d) provide
technical advice to the banks.                                      i
2.        In practical terms, the technical adviser's involvement would be
as follows:
(a) Keep a file on each individual farm under his assistance.
Each file would have a reference number which would be
used also for the loan applications. (A photograph may be
included);
(b) Provide the participating bank with the identification
check necessary for the loan application, including
name, location and reference number of the farmers;
(c) Provide the bank with the farm classification criteria
including: farm area (broken down by use including
distribution and ages of cotton trees), average yields
obtained and farming techniques used;
(d) When and if warranted, make negative technical recommendations;
(e) Keep a record of borrowers (notified by the participating
Bank after credit has been extended);
(f) Provide technical supervision to farmers having received
credit.
All files would be organized along the same system to ensure continuity and
standardization of information.



ANNEX 3
Page 1
NORTHEAST BRAZIL
RIO GRANDE DO NORTE RURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Rural Credit
Present Coverage
1.        Current coverage of the institutional (commercial banking) credit
system of farms, producing perennial cotton, is relatively low. Based on
the results of the SUDENE/IBRD/DRC sample survey taken in 1973, about 13%
or 2,300 of the estimated 18,000 (Annex 1) owner-operated farms in the
project area received credit directly from institutional sources. This
estimate is probably reasonable; the 4 Banco do Brasil (BB) branches 1/
most active in the project area registered about 2,700 "custeio" contracts in
1973 for all crops. The SUDENE/IBRD/DRC survey also indicates that those
individual farmers who borrow from banks, either for custeio (seasonal
credit) or investimento (medium and long-term credit) purposes do not do
so every year. Roughly 30% of respondents in the project area who borrowed
from institutional sources during 1971-73 did not actually borrow in 1973;
the 13% credit coverage figure for 1973 therefore understates somewhat the
number of farmers who may previously have had access to institutional credit.
2.        In 1973 institutional lending for perennial cotton operations was
overwhelmingly for seasonal crop production (seasonal labor and cash inputs);
according to BB data 86% of the number of contracts and 71% of the volume
of agricultural lending in RN was for custeio credit. Many custeio credits
were small, averaging Cr $ 4425 (about US$700) per contract in 1973 terms.
Investimento credits finance "semi fixo" items such as acquisition of work
animals and small equipment (with life times generally less than five years)
and "fixo" items such as perennial cotton first year establishment (largely
labor intensive), fences, etc.
3.        Banco do Brasil (BB) has about 80% of the institutional credit
market. Apart from direct lending by banks and lending through coopera-
tives, important suppliers of custeio credit to individual farmers are
marketing/financial intermediaries. While little data exists to indicate
the scale of such activities these intermediaries typically charge higher
annual interest rates (at 30-40%) than the banks (0-18%) and also generally
lend only on the condition that the crop is sold to them (sometimes at less
than market prices). The 1970 Census results suggest that informal lending is
predominantly for custeio purposes and is more significant for owner operators
with less than 100 ha.
1/   Caico, Currais Novos, Santa Cruz and Acu.



ANNW     3
Yage 2
4.        Despite the small average size of institutional credits most cur-
rent institutional rural credit is to larger farmers; the SUDENE/IBRD/DRC
survey indicates that almost 80% of the volume of credits (and 40% of the
number of borrowers) corresponds to farm sizes greater than 100 ha, while
slightly more than 20% (and 60% of the number of'borrowers) corresponds
to farm sizes less than 100 ha. This bias is compounded by the activities
of the rural credit and extension service, ANCAR, which is compensated for
its credit processing and administration activities, on the basis of 2% of
the total debt outstanding under the terms of its convenio with BB (Annex 2).
The low coverage of extension agents results at least partly from their
spending at least 50% of their time on credit processing, mostly for
larger loans with correspondingly higher fees, and the fact that the
extension services have not had the benefit of assured funding from other
sources.
Obstacles to Expanding Credit Coverage
5.        The vast majority of direct rural credit operations by banks are
to landowners.  Sharecroppers must obtain a "carta d'anuencia" and usually
the co-signature of their landlord as well in order to receive credit
directly from banks. Farmers owning both their crops and land can borrow
using their land and/or crops as security. As outlined below, expanding
credit coverage to landowners presents significant difficulties; accordingly,
increasing lending to landless farmers would not be an initial project
objective.
6.        Rural credit operations by banks in the project area are charac-
terized by risk-averse lending practices and lengthy credit procedures.
Lending practices are primarily determined by centrally established credit
norms which limit credit amounts relative to the value of crop or fixed asset
security and to the debt servicing capacity of- the potential borrower. 1/
In an environment where drought risks are substantial and bank managersT per-
formances are judged by their (high) profit performance and (low) level
of defaults branch managers understandably practice conservative lending
practices. Thus, even in lending operations below 50 times the highest
reference value (approximately US$2,600 in 1974), where the lending need
not be formally secured and simplified procedures give managers considerable
credit discretion, managers typically lend only to those who can already
show impressive security and good crop yields. Branch managers sometimes
also withhold disbursements of seasonal credits until the likely rainfall
can be more reliably estimated, thus limiting early plantings which have
potentially better yields. In brief, the minimum risk lending practices
1/   A maximum of 60% of the crop valued at the minimum price for custejo
credits, and a limit of 70% of net cash flow - with crop revenues
valued at the minimum price -- available for term debt service.
Crops are generally valued at the minimum price which can be as
much as 40% below the prevailing market price.



ANNEX 3
Page 3
tends to favor larger and/or better-off farmers. 1/ To expand credit
coverage, particularly to smaller farmers, changes in minimum risk lending
practices and norms are required. Bringing credit outlets closer to farmers
(farmers currently travel up to 100 km to the nearest bank branch) is also
necessary.
Credit Actions under Project
7.        The basic goal of the project credit component would be to expand
rapidly the number of farmers borrowing for productive purposes from insti-
tutional sources for seasonal, fixed investment and land purchase credits.
New credit delivery systems to achieve this goal especially for small
credits would be developed and tested by the project to meet the key cri-
teria of credit expansion, overall cost effectiveness, close physical
accessibility to farmers and ease of replicability elsewhere.
8.        To promote the development of such systems new mechanisms may be
needed to encourage participating banks to increase lending to small farmers.
A guarantee fund was considered as means of diminishing possible biases and
fears by banks in expanding lending to small farmers, by allowing participa-
ting banks to write off a percentage of possible bad debts against the fund.
However, the Government felt that the creation of such a fund would demand
extensive changes in the legal framework, affecting the entire credit system;
the Government also felt that the problem with small farmer credit may have
been due more to the failure of small farmers to request loans than to
bankers' reluctance to lend. Another possible mechanism to motivate
lending to small farmers considered was the use of a differential interest
spread for loans under the project, to compensate for possible increased
administrative costs in making many small loans. It was decided that progress
in lending should be reviewed in one year to assess more thoroughly the need
for such additional incentives.
1/   According to the SUDENE IBRD/DRC survey some of the differences
between farms receiving and not receiving institutional credit in
1973 in the 10-49.9 ha stratum (for example were as follows:
Receiving     Not Receiving       Difference    (R-NR) (
R
Farm Size          29 ha            27 ha              2 ha        7
Land in
crops            11 ha             6                 5 ha       54
Total
Capital     38,200 crs.       25,300 crs.       12,900 crs.    34
Net
Income       6,700 crs.        3,500 crs.        3,200 crs.    48



ANNEX 3
Page 4
9.        Demand for credit by small farmers would be increased by improving
promotional efforts and by decreasing risks to farmers. The promotional
role will be shared by bank agents and extensionists. Use of PROAGRO crop
credit insurance would be encouraged to reduce borrowers' risks associated
with crop failure or droughts. While the basic definidion of the PROAGRO
scheme is voluntary, the Government has given assurances that it would use
its best efforts to cause PROAGRO to be applied to all subloans. The
effectiveness of the voluntary crop credit insurance scheme will be reviewed
annually by the Government and the Bank.
10.       The effectiveness of improvements in credit promotion, processing,
and delivery systems in reaching large numbers of small farmers would also be
reviewed annually. The Government has agreed to report findings of this
progress review to the Bank, discuss possible additional mechanisms for
motivating lenders and borrowers (e.g. differential interest rates, guarantee
fund, compulsory crop credit insurance), and to carry out actions to improve
small farm credit effectiveness, as agreed by the Government and the Bank.
11.       Land purchase credits apart, custeio credit extended would be
limited to 25 times the (highest) minimum monthly salary in the country
(totalling about US$1,250) per season per individual borrower while the
sum of total outstanding seasonal and investment credits would be limited
to 50 times the reference value (totalling about US$2,500). All lending
undertaken would be unsecured, on personal signatures ("aval") only.
12.       Custeio credit (including both purchased inputs and labor) would
be extended on a fixed amount per hectare cultivated (excluding first-year
cotton), to be determined on a sub-area wide basis. This would not be less
(in constant terms) than the value of the present standard for second year
cotton.
13.       To simplify credit analysis, investment credit conditions would
be standardized for all borrowers on the basis of even streams of repay-
ments and fixed repayment and grace periods. Individual credit limits
for fixed and semi-fixed investments would then be established on the
basis of average yields obtained and cotton area cultivated, as deter-
mined by the local extensionist. These limits would be prepared in the
form of a matrix or table which would be simple to use by low level field
personnel.
14.       Wlile it is anticipated that borrowers under the project would
be largely cotton farm owner-operators with holdings of less than 200 ha,
owner operators of all size farms would be able to borrow under the terms
of the project. Sharecroppers having their landlords' approval or able to
purchase farming land would also be able to borrow under the project. In
all cases a condition for credit would be specific prior-registration of
the farner with the local extensionist. This specific condition for all



ANNEX 3
Page 5
borrowers would only be relaxed in the case of group (collective) borrow-
ing in which case group registration would be necessary.
15.       Initial credit actions would be tested to determine viable credit
delivery mechanisms meetiig project objectives. Therefore, in Phase I the
project would implement t1ree alternatives in pilot municipios. These
delivery actions (or sets cf credit procedures) would then be developed
under the guidance of the C02 dit Committee (Annex 12) utilizing the results
of close monitoring and evalLation of credit impact.
16.       Two initial credit actions under the project are described below.
For each action the following 8 iv'y steps in the credit delivery process
are identified to provide a uniform framework for analysis both of starting
procedures and possible subsequent modifications:
(a) Positive Identification;
(b) Character Reference;
(c)  Farm Classification;
(d) Credit Request (Proposta);
(e) Credit Decision;
(f) Signing (of Nota de Credito Rural) and Credit Disbursement;
(g) Supervision;
(h) Collection.
Procedures under the two actions differ for certain steps. Each action
represents a different trade-off among credit expansion, cost effective-
ness, physical accessibility to farmers and ease of replicability. Each
also represents a different level of lending risk. Table 1 attached
shows the 8 steps in the credit delivery process together with brief
descriptions of each step under the two actions.
Action 1: Direct Lending to Individuals
17.       Action 1 would include direct lending to individuals, a mobile
banking service for perhaps one day every two weeks to selected municipios,
and the availability of cash credit within two weeks of a bank's receiving
a credit proposal. Screening of credit applicants would be minimized. As
a result, the default rate may be higher than with traditional approaches.
This would be closely monitored and, if warranted, alternative mechanisms
would be recommended to reduce lending risks. The government PROAGRO
voluntary credit insurance scheme would be expected to reduce risks related
to drought and crop failures.



ANNEX 3
Page   6
18.       Previous or new borrowers of formal credit would be treated uni-
formly for credit delivery purposes, but for administrative simplicity bor-
rowing through the mobile unit would preclude borrowing from other formal
sources. The maximum for borrowing through the mobile unit would be 25
times the reference value for custeio and 50 times for total borrowing
(investimento and custeio combined).
19.       Step 1, Positive Identification, would aim towards the minimum
information required to identify the applicant as a potential borrower
under the new procedures. Many small farmers are currently deterred from
applying for formal credit by their unfamiliarity with the complex pro-
cedures involved. In order to avoid the simplified procedures under the
project being perceived as special "government" money with resulting high
defaults, the credit must be fully identified with the participating banks,
in whose names advertising and promotional schemes should be undertaken.
20.       Step 2, the Character Reference, is important because if sought,
implicitly or explicitly, it can be a major procedural factor, or lever,
in mitigating against the risk of high defaults. In this action no
character reference as such is sought.
21.       Step 3, Farm Classification, serves as a reference basis for the
credit proposal ("Proposta"), Step 4.  Throughout the steps the incremental
burden on the extensionist of credit activities as opposed to agricultural
advisory activities would be minimized. In the farm classification step,
the extensionist would not be required to value the farmer's assets in order
to arrive at a net worth assessment. The lending criteria emphasis would
be on' the farmer's productivity and cash generation capacity rather than
on collateral. This means that average yields obtained by the farmer would
have to be estimated by the extensionist and in doing so he would also be
making an implicit credit decision in terms of the investment credit limits
applicable to that particular farmer. Extensionists are already currently
expected to make such yield estimations. 1/ Although he could not do so on
character grounds, the extensionist would be expected to indicate a negative
recommendation on the proposta on technical grounds alone, if appropriate.
22.       Steps 5 and 6 would be performed largely by the mobile banking
unit. The essence of this operation would be speed and simplicity: a
commitment to deliver cash within two weeks of a bona fide proposta.
Blanket convenios either do or would exist with local land registry
authorities and other formal credit channels for exchange of the neces-
sary lists of borrowers (and defaulters). Each mobile unit would have to
comprise at least one relatively senior bank staff member (e.g., an assis-
tant manager), a cashier, a guard/driver and a van together with some small
portable office equipment (cash boxes, etc.). On the basis of one visit
1/   Information gathering on farms under his supervision would be conducted
on a permanent basis by the extensionist (see Annex 2).



ANNEX 3
Page 7
per municipio of one day every two weeks, each mobile unit could probably
service four municipios according to a weekly schedule of two days at
the municipios, two days at the bank branch and one day travelling time
with alternate pairs of municipios each week. Most of the credit demand
can be expected to be for custeio credit in the early months of the
season. However, the mobile unit should be open year-round, especially
near harvest when some non-borrowers run out of cash and are vulnerable to
"informal" credit or disadvantageous crop advances. The operating costs
of the mobile unit can therefore be regarded as largely fixed in nature
and would probably be not more than about (1974) CR$ 150,000 per annum. 1/
23.       Despite the simplified credit processing implied by steps 5 and 6,
the branch manager's formal authorization for credit disbursement must be
obtained (most probably on a weekly "block" basis) by the assistant man-
ager in charge of the mobile unit. Table 1 suggests that at least part of
custeio credit disbursement be made by voucher (or local purchase order) to
encourage acquisition of insecticides at local suppliers. Although this
represents a small proportion of the total credit, it would reinforce the
activities of the extensionist in associating the custeio credit with
productivity gains. While a phased disbursement procedure for custeio
credit is not necessarily anticipated, a modified system - which does not
depend on the specific approval of the extensionist - could be arranged,
such as three equal disbursements over the cultivation season.
24.       Assuming that collection costs and defaults and arrears are fully
recovered either through renegotiation, foreclosure, or the guarantee fund,
preliminary calculations indicate that a single mobile unit would have to
capture more than 20% of the credit "market" in a four municipio market to
break-even on the basis of the existing 5% lending spread. This level of
lending should be achievable within two years of a unit's start of operations.
25.       The actual break-even situation will vary considerably from
municipio to municipio, and if custeio borrowers do not borrow each year,
in spite of the availability of credit insurance, the overall penetration
rates would have to be correspondingly higher. However, other business
such as land purchase credits and other banking business (including
deposits) would probably be generated which would further improve the
viability of the mobile banking units.
Action 2: Credit Lending to Groups
26.       Under action 2 the banking procedures would be sinilar to action 1,
but credit would be extended to groups of between 5 and 12 farmers. These
1/   Assistant Manager (55,000) + Cashier (35,000) + Guard/Driver (25,000)
+ van (15,000) + supplies (5,000) + overhead (15,000) = 150,000
(1974 cars).



ANNEX 3
Page 8
farmers would have collective responsibility for their custeio debt and
would jointly sign a single proposta. Disbursements would be either to
group representatives or to group members individually (but simultaneously)
at the option of the group. Given the local presence of the mobile banking
unit, which would be largely responsible for cash management of credit,
this system would eventually lead to lower administrative credit delivery
costs, depending on the utilization of the capacity of the mobile unit (whose
costs are largely fixed relative to time spent in each municipio).
27.       One major advantage of this lending system would be the probable
reduction in the level of delinquencies due to peer pressure on group members
to repay on time. Another advantage would be the formation of groups which
could be used for other purposes, e.g., extension, input supply, transport,
etc. The availability of formal credit may be a more effective incentive
for group formation than such other purposes, especially in the early proj-
ect stages.
28.       One way of helping to develop the social and economic strength
of groups might be to stipulate a low minimum size group at the start of
the project, but then to raise gradually this minimum. For example, a
minimum size of three could be established for the first year of the
group's life, expanding to four in the second year and five in the third
year. Presumably group founding members would scrutinize additional
members closely. Also the initial low minimum group size could help
to maximize initial credit penetration by limiting starting group cash
management responsibilities and thus also limiting the social management
implications of the group's formation. At the same time a 12-member upper
group limit could allow both the formation of larger groups and mergers
between groups. In the special case of group member replacement (from
one year to the next) the escalating group size condition could be relaxed.
29.       As indicated in Table 1 the extensionist could take advantage
of formed groups through sampling procedures within groups for credit (and
extension) activities. Thus at the time of group "registration" and farm
classification (steps 1 and 3) he need not be familiar with each individual
group member's farm situation - but should be so with say, at least one up
to group sizes of 4, 2 for group sizes between 5 and 8, and 3 for group
sizes between 9 and 12. The same sampling ratios could be used for the
proposta step in order to speed credit delivery. This does not however
relieve the extensionist from doing an effective extension job which
includes reaching all target farmers in his area, whether on a person
to person, person to "credit" groups or person to ad hoc groups (e.g.,
media forums) basis. Coordination between credit and extension would be
critical to project success but the involvement of the extensionist in
credit processing paper work would be minimized.
30.       While group borrowing for custeio credit would be relatively
straight forward, group borrowing for investment credit would in many
cases be complicated by varying individual farmer investment needs. In
this case it would be useful to extend credit for investment purposes on
an individual basis. However, this raises a series of possible problems



ANNEX 3
Page 9
in the event of either group and/or individual credit defaults. Recog-
nizing the need for simplicity, a possible initial set of guidelines
would be the following:
(a) Individual farmers would have to be members of groups
for custeio purposes -- but need not be members of
groups for investment purposes;
(b)  A group member desiring investment credit would have
to obtain the pricr consent of other members of the
group although he alone would be solely responsible
for his investment debt on a separate personal signature;
(c) Group penalties would continue to apply for group custeio
default; and
(d) In the event of individual investment default, action
could be taken against him without compromising the
creditworthiness of the group. This set of guidelines
would place considerable reliance on the overall effec-
tiveness of farmers groups for credit purposes, the basic
hypothesis under action 2.
31.       Actions 1 and 2 should probably be initially introduced separately
in different municipios in order to learn more about their respective impacts.
Action 3: Revised Funding Through Cooperatives
32.       At least two steps could be taken:  (a) attempts to streamline
participating bank/cooperative procedures to reduce the time required for
an individual cooperative member to actually receive cash; and (b) intro-
duction by cooperatives of branch offices or "entrepostos". The entreposto
would be a combination of credit office, input supply and crop buying point.
On credit matters the entreposto would act as a channel; actual lending
decisions would remain centralized at the cooperative's headquarters. The
feasibility of entrepostos requires further analysis prior to testing on a
limited scale.
33.       The National Cooperative Bank, BNCC, presently lends only for
cooperative infrastructure, an activity not covered by the project; also,
BNCC does not yet operate in the project area. However, the Government hopes
to strengthen BNCC, expand its operations and widen its scope. The Govern-
ment plans to direct BNCC to review cooperative investment requirements in
the project area and would provide BNCC with the funds to finance these
investments as a complements to the project.

NORTHEAST BRAZIL
RIO GRANDE DO NORTE RURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Agricultural Production; Technical Aspects and Experimentation
I. Existing Situation
Project Area
1.        As described in Annex 1, the project area corresponds roughly to
the area of perennial cotton, covering about 40% of the State. The climate
is semi-arid with annual average rainfall varying from 400 to 700 mm, and
an unusually large variation coefficient (more than .50); the monthly dis-
tribution of rainfall is also unpredictable. Rainfall variability brings
about periodic major drought, the possibility of several successive years of
below average rainfall, and occasional years of excessive rainfall, all
causing damage to cotton plants and crops. Soils are generally poor. The
project area is made up of several micro-regions differentiated according to
rainfall, soils, size of holdings, and infrastructure. Cropping patterns,
form of tenancy, and number of cattle also vary greatly among farms through-
out the project area.
Farmihg Systems
2.        Only 18% of the project area is under permanent or temporary crops
(higher percentage on small farms than on large) with much of the balance
under bush used for cattle grazing. There are wide variations between farms,
but cattle sales on average account for 20 to 30% of farm cash revenues.
Cattle graze on the leaves of perennial cotton "trees" following harvest.
This provides much of the cattle's nutrition between harvest (August -
October) and the next rainy season (January - March). Perennial cotton
is the principal cash crop. Cotton quality is high (long-staple; mainly
3b-38 mm), but yields are low (annual average over the 5-year life of the
cotton tree is about 150 kg of seed cotton per ha). Cash outlays per ha for
production are low and mainly for labor; cash inputs are few and consist
mainly of pesticides (for the 50% of farms that use it at all). Recommended
cultivation practices are not widely used (para 12). Low yields, coupled
with the pervasive risk of devastating drought make farming in the project
area a low input/low output activity accompanied by marked risk-aversion
(para 7). Statistics on area under cotton are somewhat contradictory, but
suggest that there has been a gradual inc7ease in the area under cotton
(perhaps by 40%) over the last 25 years. Yields declined somewhat over the
same period. Food crops, especially corn and beans, are an essential part
of the farming system. Generally interplanted with first year cotton, these
crops largely contribute to the subsistence of farm families. Yields are
very low (200 to 400 kg per ha for both crops) and poor on-farm storage
systems further reduce total supply of food crops.



ANNEX 4
Page 2
Research and Technical Assistance
3.        There has to date been an almost total absence of relevant agricul-
tural research and trials yielding results which could be transferred to
small scale producers. Most crop research has been conducted without concur-
rent economic analysis. As a result, economically sound, well defined tech-
nological packages, combining traditional and non-traditional practices
adapted to specific micro-climates, are generally not available. Although
interplanting of corn, beans, and.cotton is a basic characteristic of small
scale tarming in the project area and the Northeast, there is a particular
lack of data and technical recommendations pertinent to crops cultivated in
association. Furthermore, agricultural research has generally ignored the
influence of risk and uncertainty in the adoption of new technology, a key
factor in Northeast agriculture (para 8).
4.        Another obstacle to generating and delivering profitable techno-
logical packages is that the technical assistance agencies are not accus-
tomed to making their recommendations on the basis of farm budgets. Their
work programs are crop oriented, primarily in terms of technical factors.
Strictly technical recommendations are necessary but do not show the profit-
ability of the recommended practices nor their effect on new farm income from
all operations. Due to the current nature of the training of extension workers
(Annex 7) and institutional problems, most existing technical recommendations
are formulated for the large scale farming conditions.
5.        In the project area, research on perennial cotton is mainly the
responsibility of: (a) the EMBRAPA research station at Cruzeta (with limited
impact); (b) the privately owned Fazenda San Miguel; and (c) a semi-public
concern, INFAOL (connected to private industry). Most of the traditional
research has been geared to improving varieties for better fiber quality. 1/
Research on food crops, especially as a component of a typical farming system,
is virtually non-existent in the State.
6.        In recent months, however, a growing awareness of the specific
problems of small farmers has taken place in the Northeast. Although
embryonic in their practical implications, some results have been made
available. INFAOL, the University of Ceara, the IPA of Pernambuco and
others have demonstrated in several instances that research and demonstra-
tion efforts could lead to substantial benefits to small farmers. INFAOL
has been instrumental especially in the larger diffusion of improved cotton
seeds from its several trial and demonstration plots scattered in the Northeast.
Also, in collaboration with relevant state agencies, EMBRAPA is redefining
its program at Cruzeta to complement new rural development efforts.
1/   To date, perennial cotton evidences a markedly negative correlation
between-yields and fiber quality. For obvious reasons, the industry
has been more inclined to promote quality related research. Neverthe-
less, on-going research is now very much productivity minded.



ANNEX 4
Page 3
Farmers' Response
7.        Agriculture in the drought-prone project area involves a consider-
able degree of uncertainty. This uncertainty leads farmers, particularly
small ones, to prefer production strategies which minimize the risks from
poor weather, rejecting other combinations of techniques and crops which
would yield greater benefits in the short run, albeit with greater risk
of loss from drought. This is further compounded by the problem of tenancy
whereby sharecropping arrangements generally act as a disincentive with
respect to technological change insofar as technological progress would
imply higher cash outlays under an uncertain weather and land use situation,
in which the other party would often reap 50% of any incremental benefits
without necessarily having contributed 50% of the incremental cost. Obstacles
to the adoption of change on a wider scale also include a general lack of
information, especially among small scale farmers, not so much about the
existence of the technology, but rather on its applicability in a specific
context. This is explained by the lack of emphasis which technical assistance
agencies place on small farmers' problems, further complicated by the farmers'
lack of confidence in the technical adviser.
Uncertainty, Productivity and Adoption of Technology
8.        Cotton is by far the most important cash crop of the project area.
The current technical knowledge concerning perennial cotton is probably
more empirical than a result of comprehensive, systematic research. The
available evidence from several empirical studies and limited research data
indicates that technical improvements, in the current state of the art, are
more likely to result from a combination of several improved techniques
at various stages of the cotton cultivation cycle, rather than from an as
yet undeveloped technical breakthrough for one particular aspect of cotton
production. Although unsatisfactory, an advantage of this situation is that
the cost of technical improvement is spread over several techniques, each
contributing to the general productivity increase. In the adverse climatic
context of the Northeast, this is probably more acceptable than a high-cost,
high-risk single technique which, in the case of crop failure, may prove
disastrous to farmers (this appears to be the case with chemical fertilizers,
see para 31). In essence, therefore, improvements based on labor intensive
techniques should be favored over those which are capital intensive or
increase cash operating costs.
9.        The importance of food crops in the risk-uncertainty problem
should be emphasized.  The security of farmers to be able to provide
for their families' basic subsistence needs probably receives higher
priority than cash receipts from cotton sales. When these needs are ful-
filled, farmers are willing to make more risky decisions concerning cash
crops involving the adoption of new technology. Conversely, any new
technology geared exclusively at cash crops production could have detri-
mental effects if it were introduced at the expense of food crops produc-
tion. This problem is unfortunately not well documented. In essence, it



ANNEX 4
Page 4
is the complex issue of what farming systems are best adapted to specific
conditions of the area.
10.       From the preceding, it is clear that interventions directed at
improving productivity and total production of small scale farmers should
explicitly take into account the following:
(a) The main emphasis should be placed on farming systems
rather than on one specific activity. In the project
area, farming systems necessarily evolve around the
cotton-cattle-food crops combination;
(b) The risk factor should be duly accounted for both in
the estimates of expected returns and in the expected
farmers response to technological change; and
(c) Small scale farmers experience with specific problems
must be recognized by the agricultural research and
technical assistance agencies. In particular, the
credibility of these agencies vis-a-vis small farmers
should be clearly established.
Perennial Cotton Production
11.       Current technical recommendations concerning the cultivation of
perennial cotton by small scale farmers include the following: 1/
Clearing and soil preparation - Recommended operations include:
land clearing, burning, stump removal, clearing and soil preparation. Animal
traction is recommended especially for stump removal and soil preparation.
Under specific circumstances, contour lining is essential, to be done with
animal traction. The use of a reversible plow is recommended.
Planting - The generally recommended practice is to plant
immediately following the first rain (between January and March). In
specific cases where the probability coefficient of rainfall is accu-
rately known, early planting may be recommended (para 26).
Spacing and Density - Recommended spacing norms are:  2.U(m X u.5Um,
or 3.00m X 0.50m depending on soil type, with 5 to 6 seeds per hole for manual
planting; and 2.00m X 1.OOm for planting done with an animal-drawn seed drill.
Sowing should be made at a depth of about 5 cm.
1/   These recommendations generally represent the latest set of technical
recommendations prepared at the request of EMBRAPA for the specific
context of Rio Grande do Norte, by a team of experts and farmers from
Northeast Brazil and RN in particular, in November 1974.



ANNEX 4
Page 5
Organic fertilization - When and if available, corral manure
should be used (at least 10 tons per ha, para 32).
Crop association - Corn and/or beans or both can be inter-
planted with first year cotton, between the cotton rows (para 33).
Cultivation practices:
(a) Thinning should be done manually, 20 to 30 days after
plant emergence, leaving 2 to 3 plants per hole;
(b) Weeding should be performed with an animal-drawn
cultivator at least three times during the first
year.  Hoe finishing should complement this, as
required.  Herbicides may be warranted in specific
cases (para 28);
(c)  Plant protection, pest control, should be performed
with insecticide, with doses of 1 to 2 litres per
ha according to the year of the cotton cycle (para 27);
(d) Trimming of cotton trees should be done after harvest,
starting the second year.
Harvestin& - This operation should be started when the capsules
are open and in the absence of humidity. Storage should be in a dry area.
12.       Although not necessarily representing the result of systematic
research, these recommended practices are sound and have been empirically
proved acceptable in several parts of the project area. Nevertheless,
several of them, if not all, require further investigation at the experi-
mentation station and/or at the farm level in real-life farming conditions.
These recommendations are already partially or totally followed by the
more progressive farmers of the area. The main reasons for non-acceptance
by other farmers have been presented in para 7. Failure to use recommended
practices occurs especially in soil preparation, use of improved seeds and
cultivation practices, particularly plant protection (weeding and pest
control), together with non-use of animal traction.
II.  Technical Actions under the Project
General
13.       The project would recommend that an initial technical package of
improved plant protection practices, improved seeds and animal traction be
disseminated immediately to farmers. These improved practices would be made
available in close association with intensified extension and modified credit



ANNEX 4
Page 6
procedures and related policies. These are practices currently employed by
better farmers in the project area. Simultaneously, the project would under-
take a program of mainly on-farm trials to improve the initial recommendations
and to refine other highly promising practices to the stage where they can be
extended to farmers on a broad scale.
A.   Initial Technical Recommendations
14.       The initial technically related interventions would constitute
a "package" consisting of:
(a) wider dissemination of existing practices (animal traction
and improved plant protection and seeds);
(b) increased area under cotton cultivation;
(c) simplified credit procedures;
(d) modified credit delivery mechanisms;
(e) voluntary crop credit insurance; and
(f) expanded and reoriented extension coverage.
This annex concerns only the technical aspects (extension is discussed in
Annex 2 and credit in Annex 3); however, credit and extension actions are
integral parts of the project's "technical package".
1.   Animal Traction
15.       The project would recommend animal traction to the roughly 50%
of farmers not currently using it. The SUDENE-IBRD/DRC Survey and other
studies 1/ show that farmers using animal traction have significantly
higher incomes than those without animal traction. (In 1973/74 farms using
animal traction had 12 ha under cultivation and gross revenues of Cr $ 7,100);
those without cultivated 5.4 ha and had gross revenues of Cr $ 2,400).
Animal traction has a positive effect on yields (better plant protection
from fast weeding) and on labor productivity (tentative results indicate
a doubling of labor productivity with animal traction, for normal years).
The initial approaches of the project would aim to increase incomes by
raising yields with more rapid weeding and by gradually increasing a farm-
1/   Especially "Selecting and Evaluating New Techniques for Small Farmers
in the Central Sertao of Ceara" by John Sanders and D.M. de Albuquerque
Lima, 1974.



ANNEX 4
Page 7
family's area under cultivation. Animal traction, by reducing labor require-
ments at peak periods, would enable the farm family to handle a larger area
under cotton, thus raising its income.
16.       The increase in the use of animal traction would be largely
independent of tenure arrangements. Initial investment costs are limited
and there is ample evidence that, on the average, most farms already have
work animals, although often unused for animal traction. The cost esti-
mates and credit projections were built up on the following estimates of
farmer acceptance of animal traction and increased area during the proj-
ect:
Animal Traction        New Area under Cotton
(No. of Animals)               (Ha)
Years 1-3                 1,100                      3,800
Years 4 and 5             1,650                      6,300
Total                   2,750                     10,100-
/1   Some 1,600 ha additional would be placed under cultivation
in year 6 of the project, raising the total incremental
area to 11,700 ha.
These are small increases relative to the existing approximately 50,000 work
animals and 220,000 (to 250,000) ha under perennial cotton in the project
area and should not create major supply problems. Animal traction is
presently used essentially for weeding and this would continue to be the
main focus of'the initial intervention under the project. However, the
trials program would seek to develop ways of extending, within three years,
the uses and impact of animal traction (soil preparation, plowing, spraying,
sowing, transport, etc.). Special attention would be given to the problem
of animal-drawn equipment adapted to the characteristics of the project
area (para 25).
2.   Improved Plant Protection
17.       The project would recommend the use of pesticides to control the
buildup of damaging insects. This is particularly important in perennial
cotton where a disease not treated in one year can affect the yield of
subsequent years in the same cycle. As with animal traction, the SUDENE/
IBRD/DRC survey and other studies indicate that farmers using pesticides
achieve 40% to 70% higher yields than those not doing so. The trials program
(para 27) would attempt to further refine this recommended practice by exam-
ining types of pesticl-e and frequency, manner and amount of application.



ANNEX 4
Page 8
18.       Specific attempts would also be made under the project to develop
an insect information and early warning system (para 27).
3.   Improved Seed
19.       Two varieties of improved seeds (SL9193 from Cruzeta, MF-3 or
MF-4 from Sao Miguel) have been developed and are generally, but by no
means universally, used. The improved seeds provide greater farm income
largely through their improved cotton quality (for which gins pay a better
price), although some yield improvement of the improved varieties is also
possible. The project would recommend use of these seeds and would work
with relevant supply agencies to improve availability of these seeds
(Annex 6). INFAOL would be largely called upon to intensify its program
of seed multiplication and diffusion through contracting with individual
farmers.
B.   Trials and Research
20.       The project area (Annex 1) is heterogeneous and its differences
must be taken into account in developing recommendations to farmers; no
single uniform "technical package" is possible. The project is mainly
directed to a target group of small farmers, initially to those owning
some land. Technical recommendations must be oriented to improving the
target group's total welfare covering a variety of crops and activities,
not simply cotton, the main cash crop in the area. Because they have
been largely neglected in the past, food crops should receive relatively
high emphasis.
21.       A number (over 20) of techniques exist which are actually or poten-
tially useful to the target group and which are in varying degrees of readi-
ness for widespread diffusion to farmers. These have been examined in relation
to farmers' reactions (where the idea has already been accessible to farmers),
the cash exposure involved and the labor required. The various techniques
have then been divided into categories according to their readiness for dis-
tribution to farmers:
(a) Requiring further work at experimental station level:
in this class, the techniques are not sufficiently
defined and the expected gain to the farmer, although
potentially attractive, is not yet sufficiently tested
to decrease the risk to the farmer.
(b) Requiring further work at the farm level: here, the
techniques are practically ready for diffusion but
further insights are needed into the discrepancy between
experimental results and farm field results, together
with economic aspects of the proposed technique. This



ANNEX 4
Page 9
level presupposes a very simple model of experimentation
with adequate security and perhaps a substantial incen-
tive (e.g., guaranteed return) for the farmers who agree
to carry out the experiments.
(c) Limited diffusion. In this class, the techniques are
essentially ready but require testing on a limited
number of farms within specific micro-climatic condi-
tions for their last refinement prior to general diffu-
sion within that particular area. Simultaneously the
materials required for general diffusion (e.g.,
demonstration, teaching aids, media, etc.) would be
prepared.
(d) General diffusion. At this point, the techniques are
recommended by extensionists to all farmers or to a
specific target group in a particular micro-region.
22.       The focus of the trials and research program would be-on those
techniques that would be seen by farmers as concretely improving their incomes
and welfare and that can quickly be brought to the stage where they can be
generally diffused to farmers. The methods would involve close coordination
between the trials and extension actions under the project and coordination
with similar activities underway on similar crops and conditions elsewhere
in the Northeast. Results of the trials activity would be reviewed at least
annually to see that the trials still focus on techniques that can be developed
quickly for profitable use by farmers, especially small ones. The extension
component would work closely with the trials component to provide a rapid
two-way flow of information.
23.       The following items to be examined by the technical investiga-
tions component are discussed in rough order of likely speed of reaching
the level of general diffusion. Each is followed by a letter(s) in paren-
thesis denoting the level or levels at which further investigations should
be initiated: research station (a), on-farm trials (b) and limited diffu-
sion (c). The items are not exhaustive; other items would be expected to
be added during the project. Also, the levels of investigation are not
exclusive. For example, new equipment could be tested simultaneously at
both the research station (a) and farm (b) levels. Some items (e.g.,
effect of types of tillage) could only be evaluated with considerable
confidence after many years of testing, to allow for the wide range in
year-to-year rainfall. Other items (e.g., different types of animal-
drawn equipment) could be evaluated with considerable confidence within
a single season. Still others could be evaluated after several years'
testing, simulating the full cycle from statistical data.
24.       On-Farm Storage of Food Crops (c).  On-farm storage losses of
maize and beans are reported high in the project area. The technical
aspects of low-c -t methods of protection (e.g., FAO method of beans



ANNEX 4
Page 10
conservation) are well-known. The methods must be tried on farms to
evaluate their attractiveness to users and to develop methods of diffusion.
25.       Companion Equipment for Animal Traction (b, c).  The project
would import a small number of simple animal-drawn tools as prototypes for
local testing. Tools would include those for soil preparation, sowing,
retaining furrows, clearing stumps during land-clearing, on-farm transport
and spraying with an animal-drawn sprayer. For example, simple equipment
to move the relatively large amounts of water used in crop spraying would be
farm-tested. Testing would include use of the principal animals (oxen,
donkeys, horses) singly and in teams.
26.       Early Planting (c).  An existing study (very limited, based on one
year only) indicates a marked yield increase in first-year cotton resulting
from planting in early February rather than mid-March. Early planting is
greatly facilitated by use of an animal-drawn sowing machine permitting a
farmer to plant 4 ha in the 2 days following a rain (20 mm or more), compared
to 0.5 ha if done manually (following which he must wait for the next rain).
Finalizing the early planting recommendation could be greatly facilitated by
rainfall probability studies based on the 50-60 year rainfall records from
a dense network of stations. Within several years this practice could
probably be widely recommended.
27.       Plant Protection (b).  The project would carry out farm trials
to develop recommendations on type, quantity and frequency of application
of pesticides. Timeliness of pesticide application is important in con-
trolling insect parasites. Consequently, as a parallel activity the
project would support actions to carry out a simple insect parasite
early-warning system. Two man-months of the funds provided for con-
sultants would be expected to be used to study the practical steps that
could be taken within existing institutions operating in the State to
provide a simple early warning system for the major insect parasites.
28.       Improved Weeding.  Presence of weeds markedly affects yields.
However, weeding from the second year of cotton onwards is very time-
consuming because the cotton "trees" have grown to the stage where use
of animal-drawn cultivator becomes difficult. Two promising improvements
should be worked on:
-    Use of Herbicides (b).  Initial studies by the Federal
University of Ceara indicate favorable results from
using herbicides. However, further farm-level studies
are needed to determine economic dosage and spraying
method.
-    Cotton Trimming (a, b).  This involves trimming the
second-year cotton plants to enable the animal-drawn
cultivator to operate between the rows. The yield
effects and labor requirements need to be studied further.



ANNEX 4
Page 11
29.       Traditional Food Crops (a, b, c).  Low yields of corn and beans
(200 to 400 kg per ha), the most widespread food crops of the project area,
are mainly due to: (a) low grade varieties; (b) rainfall variability; and
(c) poo! soils. In addition, being generally interplanted with first year
cotton, food crops density per ha is necessarily limited. Largely ignored
by research and experimentation, especially in their role in association with
cotton, these crops should be the subject of intensive research efforts as
the potential for improvement appears large. New varieties more adapted to
the area should be tested. For instance, the Pitiuaba beans variety, with
a cycle of 70 days, would show a yield of 10% to 40% higher than local
varieties (experiments conducted in Ceara). For beans, the main problem
seems to be the treatment against parasite attack at the blooming period
and at the conservation stage (para 24). In other selected areas, cassava
may have a relatively large importance.
30.       Alternate Food Crops (a, b, c).  Sorghum is more drought-resistant
than maize and probably more suitable for the project area. Preliminary
trials indicate a much higher yield from a sorghum/beans combination than
from maize/beans. An important problem is the central role of maize in
the traditional diet of the area. However, consumption by animals usually
exceeds that of the typical family so that an initial 50% of the foodcrop
area for sorghum for animal feed could be contemplated. Sorghum could
probably be recommended for general diffusion within one or two seasons.
In the meantime further farm trials to pinpoint the yield differences
between sorghum and maize in different years and to test farmers' reactions
should be conducted. Trials should also be conducted on different sorghum
varieties and cultivation practices. A sorghum "technical package" might
include soil preparation with animal traction, use of manure, early sowing
and weeding with animal traction.
31.       Fertilization (a, b).  Most of the soils devoted to cotton and
foodcrops are poor. Limited testing of inorganic and organic fertilizers
indicate profitable possibilities from small dosages in specific circum-
stances in "normal" years. The use of chemical fertilizers will depend on
the climate and soils of a specific micro-region, together with farmers'
willingness to risk the loss of investment in fertilizers in a drought year.
The agricultural college at Mossoro, near the project area, is equipped to
analyze soil samples. Fertilizer trials have been carried out at the Cruzeta
research station and for two years by INFAOL (a semi-public Northeast trials
and seed-producing body) on selected farms producing seed under contract.
Preliminary results indicate that a light dressing (13-17-20) which minimizes
investment in fertilizer may be attractive in normal years. However, the
results are highly sensitive to changes in the cotton/fertilizer price
relatives. These tests need to be continued, to give a picture of results
in a variety of years, and modified according to soils of a particular micro-
region. Within several seasons, some limited diffusion (stage c) should be
attempted to test farmrrs' attitude to investing in chemical fertilizer
under the (then) protection of the PROAGRO crop insurance being introduced
parallel to the project.



ANNEX 4
Page 12
32.       Organic fertilization (b, c) also shows promising preliminary
results. From a technical point of view the use of organic fertilizer can
be immediately recommended for the project area. However, additional re-
finements are necessary to develop the best way to prepare the manure so
as to maximize its nutrient value and to estimate the cost and refine the
methods of transporting it (e.g., animal-drawn cart).
33.       Crop rotations and association merit further work at the research
station level. Several associations of crops with cotton should be tested,
e.g., fodder crops, groundnuts, beans, sorghum and corn. Emphasis should
be given to the study of crop associations including legumes for the supply
of nitrogen.
34.       Crop production on receding shorelines.  Part of the area, par-
ticularly the southern "Serido", contains several thousand small dams that
are used for animals and "subsiding" crops (subsistence crops, including
a wider range, e.g., bananas than under dryland conditions) along the
receding water's edge as the water level drops during the dry period or
during drought. No previous work has been carried out on devising better
practices (varieties, fertilizer, sequence, etc.) for these activities.
The project would support farm-level trials on these activities.
35.       Annual Cotton.  Where technically feasible (in the wetter part
of the project area, generally around Umarizal and Santa Cruz)o annual
cotton provides much higher yields than perennial (by two to three times
in a normal year). Farmers' incomes are higher than with perennial cotton
in that the greater quantity of cotton more than compensates for the lower
quality and price of annual cotton. For much of the project area the
rainfall conditions clearly favor perennial cotton. However, based on
rainfall averages and probabilities, both perennial and annual cotton
could be recommended for certain micro-regions. For annual cotton, addi-
tional farm trials are necessary for improved seeds and for early sowing
and other practices. Great care must be taken to avoid genetic crossing
of the two seeds (which would be detrimental to both) and at least 200 m
must be maintained between fields of the two.
C.   Organization - Project Implementation
36.       The complexity and urgency of the problems surrounding the tech-
nical aspects and related proposed interventions under the project require
an elaborate and comprehensive organizational arrangement which would
ensure: (a) promptness in the design and implementation of an experiment-
ation program; (b) timely transmission of results to farmers in practical
terms; (c) flexibility for speedy and continuous refinement of technologies
and dissemination methods.
Organization (Annex 12)
37.       The overall responsibility for the technical package generation
and diffusion program would be placed in a Technical Committee to be created



ANNEX 4
Page 13
to coordinate planning, implementation and review of activities and to
develop technical recommendations under the project. This Committee would
make recommendations to the Project Unit through its chairman, the Technical
Adviser of the Project Unit. Besides the Adviser, the Committee would be
made up of various experts on Northeast Brazil, including representatives of
EMBRAPA, the extension service (ANCAR-RN), INFAOL, the Federal University of
Ceara, the IPA 1/ of Pernambuco, Banco do Brazil and Banco do Nordeste.
Besides its role in coordination of project-related research and experimenta-
tion activities, the Committee would be expected to provide imaginative
leadership in the field. As a larger forum for permanent communication
between all parties involved, the Committee would also be expected to ensure
consistency of the program with other research programs in the State and
Northeast Brazil.
38.       The initial program implementation would pivot around five insti-
tutional bodies with well-defined responsibilities:
(a)  The Project Unit, through its Technical Adviser besides its
leading role within the Technical Committee, would be
responsible for: (a) coordinating the detailed planning
of research activities, the monitoring and control of
expenditures; (b) the exploitation of results provided
by the project Monitoring Group; (c) the modification and
elaboration of the technical packages according to research
results and farmer's reaction; (d) the coordination between
research activities (package generation) and the extension
service (diffusion); and (d) participation in the training
of extensionists.
(b) INFAOL would be responsible for the implementation of the
trials program at the farm level (para 43).
(c) The University of Ceara, through its Department of Agri-
cultural Economics, would be employed as a consultant to
provide: (a) the conceptualization of the actual imple-
mentation of the program, including the methodology to
be used for sample design, statistical review and analysis;
(b) participation in the training of INFAOL personnel; (c)
consulting expertise during implementation in the field;
and (d) first analysis of results.
(d) The Project Monitoring and Evaluation Group would further
analyze results transmitted by the University of Ceara
and translate them into recommendations to the Project
Unit for speody corrective actions.
1/   Instituto de Pesquisa Agropecuaria.



ANNEX 4
Page 14
(e) The Extension Service (ANCAR-RN) would be responsible
for the general diffusion of the techniques which are
deemed ready for generalized use by farmers in the
project area.
39.       The above institutional arrangements would be expected to be
followed throughout the project but would be reviewed annually (Annex 12)
during the annual plan and subject to change as necessary.
Nature of Research and Analysis
40.       The main emphasis under the project would be placed on experi-
mentation at the farm level. Two types of investigation would be under-
taken:
(a) Analysis of plots: the objective would be to determine
the physical impact of selected improved techniques and/or
technical packages. Although mainly technical (i.e.,
effect of techniques on yields), this analysis should
also provide for essential economic and financial informa-
tion (cost-benefit) on each selected technique. The
methodology used should be simple (e.g., variance anal-
ysis, regression, factorial analysis). Registering docu-
ments and simple computerized programs for analysis should
be elaborated by the University of Ceara (the former for
use by INFAOL) along with the statistical tools to be
used. 1/
(b) Analysis of farms and farm systems; the objective would
be to analyze the economic impact of improved technolo-
gies and selected socio-economic variables (e.g., agricul-
tural credit, land tenure) on farmers' income. Besides
simple analytical tools, more sophisticated methods
(linear programming) could also be utilized. 1/ Overall,
the emphasis would be on simplicity and speed of results
rather than absolute accuracy, so that practical results
can be made available as soon as possible.
41.       These farm-level analyses should in due time be complemented by
a socio-economic analysis of various significant variables related to farmer
organizations such as small farmers groups or cooperatives, their relationships
with local administration, etc. This activity may in part be dissociated
from the farm level investigation and attached to the monitoring and eval-
uation unit effort.
1/   Appendix A of this Annex provides for a tentative identification of
selected variables which could be included in this analysis.



ANNEX 4
Page 15
Research at the Experimentation Station Level
42.       Several of the techniques which appear to have a promising poten-
tial require further testing and experimentation at the experimentation
station level. EMBRAPA would be directly responsible for carrying out the
basic and/or applied research required, establishing its program at Cruzeta
in accordance with the overall program established by the Technical Committee
and the detailed planning from the Project Unit. Because of its current
status of reorganization at the Federal level, EMBRAPA would not be expected
to fully contribute its share of the research effort until several months
after the project initiation date. However, it could be expected that the
Cruzeta station facilities would be rapidly put into use for the project
benefit as part of EMBRAPA's Northeast program, for which the Government
has also requested Bank financing. In addition, close relationships would
be expected between EMBRAPA and INFAOL, the former providing the latter
with consultant specialists when and if necessary.
Farm Trials and Experimentation
43.       The research program at the farm level would be centered on a
large number of experimentation sites located throughout the project area.
Because of the nature of the research (para 40), plots of different sizes
(for the testing of selected techniques) as well as whole farms (for the
analysis of farming systems) would be required. The project would thus
cover about 400 sites distributed in the project area, with gradual
phasing over the five-year disbursement period:
 ----------Year----------- -
1      2      3      4      5         Total
Number of Sites      40     80      80    100    100         400
The exact distribution among sub-areas and between plots and whole farms
would be established by the Project Unit, the University of Ceara and
INFAOL, following criteria of statistical representation. 1/ Besides
the experimentation activities, all sites would be used for the collec-
tion of generally relevant data (rainfall, temperature, etc.) and as sites
for meetings of extensionists and farmer groups.
44.       INFAOL would be responsible for carrying out the bulk of the
trials conducted on each site. INFAOL, a semi-public institution located
in the Northeast, has developed considerable experience in on-farm trials
1/   For purposes ot cost calculation, each site was assumed to be about
3 ha.  Var--tions from this average can be expected for project implemen-
tation.



ANNEX 4
Page 16
work in connection with seed production for cotton and in demonstration
plots of modern technology use. Besides this experience with the North-
east Brazil agricultural environment, INFAOL benefits from its experience
in dealing with small farmers and evidences the flexibility necessary to
adjust to the expected changing needs in the research program. Furthermore,
it displays a sufficient technical and financial absorptive capacity to
undertake a large scale experimentation effort over and above its present
activities.
45.       The actual implementation would be done by INFAOL through con-
tracts with individual farmers. Under the terms of the contracts, the farmer
would essentially provide for his own labor under supervision from INFAOL
technicians. All other inputs would be provided by INFAOL, together with the
necessary investment requirements in equipment, animals or other items (e.g.,
fencing). The farmer would keep all the returns from the sales of output
produced under the particular trial. In addition, he would be ensured a
minimum guaranteed return (in case of failure of the trial) equivalent to the
returns he would have obtained on the same area without the trial. Further
details of contract arrangements should be prepared by the Technical Committee.
46.       Total manpower requirements from INFAOL after five years would
be: 1 general coordinator, 4 regional coordinators, 8 research specialists
and 20 field technicians (Annex 10, Table 3a). One man-year of consulting
service (at least initially from the University of Ceara) per year over
five years would be added to provide assistance in the program elaboration,
analysis of results and field implementation (para 38). The total cost of
the program would be approximately Cr$ 19.7 million (US$2.7 million) over
5 years. Project costs would cover salaries and training of the additional
manpower required, equipment (especially vehicles), on-farm investments
and operating costs (including labor).
Special Cases: Small Equipment - Small Dams
47.       The testing of equipment for animal traction can be separated in
two parts: (a) the estimation of the effect of cultivation practices with
various kinds of small equipment on crop yields (this can be part of the
testing at the farm level); (b) the testing of equipment per se (handiness,
necessary adaptation, adjusting, time studies, etc.) which can be performed
in any field (Ministry of Agriculture farms, INFAOL traditional fields).
Testing should be made on several types of equipment imported from Southern
Brazil and/or other countries with similar natural conditions as RN, e.g.,
European countries (Italy, Spain), African countries (Algeria, Senegal)
or others. Depending upon the first results of testing, the local opportu-
nities for manufacturing adapted sets of equipment may be investigated.
48.       Small dams are very important in the farming system (para 34) in
terms of production and security, especially during drought years. Besides
experimentation on selected crops on receding shorelines, small dams could



ANNEX 4
Page 17
be the subject of a sample survey designed to evaluate and analyze their
general technical and economic impact on: (a) ecological conditions, (b)
cattle production; (c) cultivated area around the reservoir (subsiding
and/or irrigated); and (d) area under irrigation below the catchment. In
addition, the selection of a few (two or three) dams could be useful to
test and analyze possible improvements in water usage, including water
consumption (human and animal), irrigation by small pumps and fish breeding.
49.       The eventual programs referring to these two special cases would
be defined and elaborated by the Project Unit Technical Adviser for approval
by the Technical Committee for implementation by subsequently selected agencies
(consultants, DNOCS, etc.).
Tentative First-Year Program
50.       The first year program should be conceived as a phase of elabora-
tion and consolidation. It should especially focus on:
(a) Finalizing the institutional and organizational arrange-
ments, through the establishment of "convenios" among
the different parties involved e.g. Project Unit,
INFAOL, University of Ceara, ANCAR/RN.  Considerable
progress has already been achieved in this direction;
(b) Building the capabilities for implementation and
analysis through recruitment and training of per-
sonnel, establishment of the field organization
network, identification of the variables to be
tested, and preparation of recording documents;
(c) Establishing the exact methodologies to be used,
especially for farm sample selection and analysis
of results; 1/ and
(d) Initiating implementation in selected areas, with
the selection of sites (farms and plots), establish-
ment of contracts with farmers, elaboration of the
farmer's work plan, supply of necessary inputs,
supervision of work and data collection.
51.    . The timing of this program should take into account the cycle of
cotton and food crops (both rainfed and on receding shorelines). The first
set of results relating to yields would be available in July (beans),
September (corn and sorghum), October - November (cotton), and January -
February (crops on receding shorelines). The work program for each crop
1/   An inventory  E existing research data should be conducted in the early
stages of this preparation.

should be established accordingly. In addition, several of the variables
tested are highly dependent on the rainfall pattern during the test period.
The identification of variables should therefore clearly separate variables
according to their sensitivity to rainfall probability. Finally, a full
investigation and data collection refers to variables ahich can be analyzed
independently of the crop cycle and the rainfall pattern (equipment, socio-
logical studies, etc.) and should be dealt with accordingly. Appendix 2
provides for a tentative time schedule of the various activities to be under-
