NORTHEAST BRAZIL
RIO GRANDE DO NORTE RURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Health
A. Introduction
1.        The health component of rural development projects not only contri-
butes directly to social welfare as the final aim of development, but is also
highly complementary to the directly productive investments. Better health
will increase labor availability and productivity, improve learning ability
and avoid treatment costs. Moreover, basic health services and environmental
sanitation are rated highly by rural communities and participation fostered
through community involvement in the construction and operation of such facil-
ities may be the first step in the acceptance of directly productive components
of the rural development package.
2.        However, the indirectly productive impact of health services and
environmental sanitation is difficult to measure. This adds to the importance
of making sure that health components of rural development projects are cost-
effective and potentially replicable over broader areas. This in turn implies
emphasis on low-cost, environmental and preventive services at the community
level, assuring wide coverage of the rural population and relying heavily on
personnel who live and work in the community.
3.        This Annex deals with, (a) health status and disease pattern in the
project area, (b) the organization and resources of the rural health sector
in Rio Grande do Norte, (c) effectiveness and limitations of the rural health
care system in the project area and the major issues for rural health policy,
(d) the proposed expansion and improvement of the rural health delivery system
in a selected sub-area of the project, and (e) the potential second phase ex-
pansion to the balance of the project area.
B. Health Status
(a) Basic Demographic Indicators
4.        Compared to Brazilian national averages, the Northeast of Brazil
has a much lower per capita income and reports markedly lower health status
indicators: life expectancy at birth is about 49 years (versus 61 for all
of Brazil), infant mortality 137 per 1,000 live births (versus 75) and the
crude death rate of 18 (versus 10). The 1960-70 crude birth rate of at
least 44 per 1,000 was considerably higher than the Brazilian average of
38.3.



AKNEX 5
Page 2
5.        In Rio Grande do Norte (RP), the 1960-70 population growth rate was
almost 3.0% per year, with urban and rural populations expanding at 5.3% and
1.2%, respectively. Available data for Natal indicate an infant mortality
rate of 161 per 1,000 live births in 1968-73, with rural rates estimated to
be even higher. 1/
(b) Disease Pattern
6.        Communicable diseases remain the principal health problem in the
rural Northeast, particularly among children under five years of age. Although
data are of only limited reliability because of under-reporting, primarily in
rural areas, and mis-reporting due to confounding underlying and associated
causes, they convey a picture of leading causes of mortality and morbidity.
Enteritis and other diarrheal diseases, influenza and pneumonia are the most
prevalent diseases for all age groups. 2/ Tuberculosis, measles, hepatitis,
whooping cough, diphtheria, Chagas Disease, and meningitis are still major
illnesses. 3/ Schistosomiasis is prevalent in the humid coastal belt of RNT.
7.        Malnutrition is a major underlying or associated cause of mortality
in children under five years of age. 4/ For example, an investigation of the
nutritional status of children in rural RN (Varzea) revealed that in 1973
41% of children of ages 0-5 suffered from nutritional deficiency of Grade I
(according to the Gomez scale), and 16% and 6% of Grades II and III, re-
spectively. 5/ Protein malnutrition and vitamin A deficiency were the most
prevalent.
8.        Most of the diseases in rural RN are amenable to simple, low-cost
interventions in the fields of preventive medicine and environmental sanita-
tion (paras. 28 ff.).
1/   Foundation SESP data for the mid-sixties indicate an infant mortality
rate of 224 for Natal and 409 for the interior of RN. However, these
figures probably reflect some under-reporting of births, primarily in
rural areas, i.e., parents register a child's birth only concomitantly
with its death.
2/   According to a 1969 PAHO investigation in Recife, diarrheal diseases
accounted for 31.2% of deaths in the 0-5 age bracket, measles for 16.1%
and pneumonia for 10.6%.
3/   According to reported cases of communicable diseases of both State
Secretariat of Health and Foundation SESP, measles shows highest inci-
dence, followed by tuberculosis, infectious hepatitis, whooping cough
and diphtheria.
4/   According to PAHO, nutritional deficiency was an underlying or associated
cause in 46.2% of deaths in the 0-5 age bracket in Recife in 1969.
5/   In the Gomez scale, Grade I corresponds to least severe malnutrition,
Grade II to more serious and Grade III to most serious malnutrition.



ANNEX 5
Page 3
C. Organization and Resources of the Rural Health Sector
9.        The effectiveness of RN's rural health delivery system, including
both medical services and environmental sanitation, is seriously affected by
duplication and inefficiency of the organizational set-up as well as by
limited availability and misallocation of financial, physical and human re-
sources. The following examines organization and resources of the rural
health sector.
(a) Institutional Framework
10.       The health sector in Brazil is characterized by a large number of
private organizations and of government and semi-autonomous entities at
federal, state and municipal levels, which in some cases even address the
same target groups, operate in the same areas and perform the same functions.
Their uncoordinated, parallel activities result in duplication of efforts, in-
effective operations and waste of resources. Basic needs of the rural popula-
tion remain unattended. The recent upgrading of the social insurance system
with the creation of a Ministry for Social Security is not expected to signi-
ficantly improve health sector administration. 1/
11.       The main public agencies involved in both development and operation
of health services in rural RN are the State Department for Public Health
(Health Department) 2/, the Foundation for Public Health Services (Foundation
SESP), 3/ the Extension Service (ANCAR/RN), and the Rural University Center for
Training and Community Development (CRUTAC). 4/ The Fund for Assistance of
Rural Workers (FUNRURAL) 5/ finances and provides health services to the rural
population through agreements and contracts with other government agencies and
the private sector. Foreign donor agencies, mainly CARITAS and UNICEF, provide
material and financial assistance. Under a 1973 agreement with the State Gov-
ernment, the private Brazilian Society for Family Welfare (BEMIFAMI) 6/ is devel-
oping a state-wide family planning program.
12.       The Health Department is responsible for planning, coordinating and
executing health programs at the state level. With the assistance of SUDENE,
planning capacity has been improved by the establishment of Planning Unit
(ASPLAN) 7/ and the division of the State into six Health Regions 8/ in order
to decentralize and increase effectiveness of health services administration.
Regions include between 16 and 35 municipios and will be centered around a
regional hospital.
1/   The Ministry of Health is primarily responsible for health sector policy
formulation and coordination, development and control of norms and stand-
ards, and special programs. The Ministry of Education is responsible for
health sector research.
2/   Secretaria i.stadual de Saude Publica, or SESP.
_/   Fundacao S- vicos de Saude Publica, or F.SESP.
4/   Centro Rural Universitario de Treinamento e Acao Comunitaria.
5/   Fundo de Assistencia ao Trabalhador Rural.
6/   Sociedade Civil de Bem-Estar Familiar no Brasil.
7/   Assesoria de Planejamento/Secretaria de Saude.
8/   Regioes de Saude.

Chart 1 gives the Health Regions by municipios and relates them to the Project
area, which falls into several such regions. Health Region 3, however, coin-
cides with the Serido sub-area of the Project.
13.       Foundation SESP is a semi-autonomous federal agency which comes under
the Ministry of Health. Its objectives are to provide health care and environ-
mental sanitation in remote and in new areas of the country (e.g., along the
Transamazon Highway), to train technical and auxiliary health personnel and
to conduct related research and surveys. Despite recent heavy involvement in
the Amazon, the bulk of activities are being developed in the Northeast.
Since its establishment in 1942, 1/ Foundation SESP has won the reputation
of being an efficiently managed agency with successful, comprehensive programs,
including curative and preventive primary care as well as environmental
sanitation. Its status as a Foundation by-and- Large prevents political staff
appointments and ensures continuity of leadership. On the other hand, the
structure of its organization is very centralized with little flexibility
at state and field levels. In RN, Foundation SESP is represented by a Regional
Office in Natal, which supervises and coordinates a network of health
centers, a training center for auxiliary personnel, sanitation programs, and
joint (disease-specific) programs with the State government.
14.       The organizational structure of ANCAR/RN - as one of the main
agencies involved in the Rural Development Project - is described elsewhere.
Through its social extension unit, ANCAR/RN develops and operates a health
minipost network in close collaboration with Foundation SESP and with some
coordination with the Health Department.
15.       Established in 1966, CRUTAC is an "interiorization" program of the
Federal University of RN, providing graduate students with compulsory training
and practical experience in the rural environment, and providing communities
of three selected regions, comprising between 8 and 11 municipios each, with
services in the fields of education, health services, environmental sanitation,
home economics, legal counseling, administration and community development.
The program is being directed by a Central Office at the University in Natal
and Regional Offices with participation of local representatives. In health,
there are regional hospitals and community health posts, with emphasis on
outreach, preventive services and collaboration with the Health Department
and FUNRURAL. Five municipios of the Santa Cruz Region are within the Project
area.
16.       FUNRURAL is a semi-:-utonomous entity which comes under the recently
created Ministry for Social Security and Assistance. 2/ Having undergone sev-
eral changes since being established in 1963, it now extends the provision of
1/   Based on an agreement betweer Lne Brazilian and the U.S. Government
which envisaged the delivery of curative and preventive (anti-malaria)
health services for the workers employed in the strategic rubber pro-
duction in the Amazon.
2/   Ministerio de Previdencia e Assistencia Social, or MPAS.



ANNEX 5
both pension benefits and health services to rural workers and smallholders.
The target group does not contribute to the financing of the program. Funds
are being collected through a 2% sales tax on agricultural products and a
2. 4% payroll tax for all--primarily urban--corporate employees.  In RN,
FUNRURAL has a Regional Office in Natal and 15 local o'fices of which the ones
in Santa Cruz, Currais Novos, Jardim do Serido, Caico and Pau de Ferros cover
the Project area. There are about 40,000 beneficiaries among the rural popu-
lation. In health, FUNRURAL finances and provides mainly curative services
through agreements with public and private hospitals and ambulatory care
centers.
(b) Health Expenditures of Public Sector
17.       As in other Latin American countries, health sector expenditures
show a marked imbalance between the relatively large amounts spent by the
Social Security system on medical care for the insured segments of the urban
population and the resources available for general health services for the
urban and rural poor. According to available data for 1971, national health
expenditure of the public sector was about US$10.50 per capita, of which the
Federal Government spent 12% 1/, state governments (primarily Sao Paulo and
Guanabara) 27%, municipios 5% and Social Security 54%.
18.       Data on rural health expenditures in RN are of only limited avail-
ability and reliability. The Health Department had total 1974 revenues 2/ of
US$3.45 million or about US$2.20 per capita. Most of these resources are being
spent in the major urban centers, primarily Natal. In 1974, FUNRURAL spent
about US$1 million on health services for the families of its 40,000 rural
beneficiaries, which is about US$4 per beneficiary family member of US$1.30
per capita of total rural population. Foundation SESP expenditures on sanita-
tion and health care are estimated to be approximately US$900 thousand of .
which more than 50% are being spent on urban population in the interior of RN.
19.       Municipios in RN, finally, spent between 2.1% and 28.8% of 1973
revenues on health care, with poorer municipalities allocating a much higher
proportion of resources to health than relatively richer ones (e.g., in
Jucurutu, 28.8%, compared to Natal, 2.2% and Caico 6.5%). In per capita
terms, health care expenditures of municipios range from US$0.15 to US$2,
with rural per capita expenditure estimated to be much lower. Considering
all different sources, it is estimated that total health expenditures of
the public sector in rural areas of RN are roughly US$2 per capita. Ex-
cluding FUNRURAL beneficiaries, this average is much lower.
1/   But the Ministry of Health spent only 5.3% of total public health
expenditures.
2/   Including Federal Ministry of Health transfers and Social Security
contracts.

Page 7
(c) Health Facilities and Water Supply Systems
20.       Table 1 gives the distribution of hospital beds, health centers and
posts, small community health posts and water supply systems by administrative
health regions (see Chart 1).  With the exception of Region VI (Pau dos Ferros,
Umarizal), it shows a strong positive correlation between proportion of rural
population and population per hospital bed. There is a relatively large supply
of hospital beds. 1/ However, health centers and posts provide services to be-
tween 10,000 and more than 70,000 people; community health posts, designed to
serve about 1,000 persons, are virtually lacking in most rural areas. Con-
sidering the fact that the averages for Regions I (Natal) and II (Mossoro)
are distorted by the inclusion of major urban centers, the rural population
of Region III (Serido), which corresponds to Project sub-area II, seems to
have the best supply of health facilities. However, this does not necessarily
imply superior quality of services or even better health status.
21.       Nearly 75% of hospitals but only 52% of bed capacity belong to the
private sector. In the public sector, health facilities are operated by
federal, state and municipal agencies. Foundation SESP has ten health
centers in the interior, of which the ones in Angicos, Santa Cruz, Currais
Novos and Serra Negra do Norte fall into the Project area. ANCAR/RN operates
52 health miniposts. Of these 28 are in the Project area. The university
program CRUTAC maintains 3 regional hospitals and about 30 simple satellite
outposts. The Santa Cruz hospital is in the Project area. FUNRURAL has
contracts with 70 hospitals and 15 outpatient facilities, buying services
for its beneficiaries.
22.       In Table 1, the water supply systems with house connections are
all urban and include only 16% of municipios. Only Natal has a sewerage'
system. The other municipios usually have tubewells for their urban popula-
tion. But there is virtual lack of safe rural water supply and households
have to rely on treating their drinking water. As shown, prevalence of water-
borne diseases is high.
(d) Health Manpower
23.       In 1971, Brazilian averages were 1 physician per 1,330 population,
1 nurse per 11,100, and 1 auxiliary per 1,040. While the population/manpower
ratios for state capitals were estimated to be 530 for physicians, 3,450 for
nurses and 540 for auxiliaries, the corresponding ratios for the interior
(including urban areas) were 2,270 people per physician, 33,330 per nurse
and 1,390 per auxiliary. However, these figures still mask considerable
differences between urban and rural areas of the Brazilian interior.
24.       In primerily rural areas of RN, the population/physician ratio is
estimated to be abcat 16,700, compared to 710 in Natal. Table 2 gives the dis-
tribution of health personnel, employed by the Health Department, between the
1/   However, most hospitals are of very poor standards.

25.       In contrast, Foundation SESP pays attractive salaries for interior-
based personnel and its ten health centers are well staffed with physicians,
nurses and auxiliaries. ANCAR/RN has 52 community health workers staffing
miniposts. CRUTAC has a small permanent staff at regional hospitals and works
primarily through its students doing ruraL service. Since mid-1973, BEMFAM
has trained more than 500 volunteers as educators and distributors for its
family planning program, of which 45% were teachers, 17% nurses and community
health workers, 8% midwives and 30% other community leaders.
D. Rural Health Delivery in the Project Area
26.       Despite innovative approaches at the grass roots level, availability,
accessibility and quality of basic health services in the Project area are
still very poor. The major causes are:
(a) The virtual lack of minimum standard water supply and sewage
disposal facilities;
(b) The very limited coverage by the lowest tier of the rural
health system, i.e., the community health posts with emphasis
on outreach preventive services, including promotion of envi-
ronmental sanitaticn atd Improved nutrition;



MNNEX 5
Page 10
(c) The lack of integration of the community health level with
higher tiers of the system; and
(d) The ineffective operation of the higher levels of health care
in primarily rural areas, i.e., health centers a-c1 small hospitals.
27.       In the following, emphasis is placed on the analysis of services
provided to small rural communities, i.e., ANCAR/RN's health minipost approach,
rather than on the assessment of health care in the urban seats of project area
municipalities. However, health centers located in urban areas are analyzed
as referral, supervision and manpower training points for the community-based
health system.
(a) Basic Health Services at the Community Level
28.       Based on its experience in working with rural populations in the
fields of agricultural and social extension, ANCAR/RN in 1968 started a rural
community health program in close collaboration with Foundation SESP. The pro-
gram aimed at (i) achieving active community involvement in health promotion,
(ii) providing low-cost services with emphasis on prevention, (iii) increasing
coverage of rural population through community-based health miniposts and out-
reach services, (iv) training persons from and selected by the local community
as health workers, and (v) providing back-up support through health centers,
operated by Foundation SESP and the Department of Health.
29.       This innovative approach proved to be highly successful in terms of
social acceptability, motivation of health workers and apparently also cost-
effectiveness. It has meanwhile been adopted by ANCAR agencies in other
Northeastern states and has recently drawn the attention of the Federal gov-
ernment, which shows increasing commitment to rural health policy. However,
while Foundation SESP has been able to contribute considerable expertise on
the predominantly medical issues (e.g., the activity-mix of health workers),
lack of experience and difficulties in financing, operating, managing and
integrating the system still pose serious constraints on program effective-
ness.
30.       Chart 2 gives the location of the 28 existing ANCAR/RN health mini-
posts and of their supporting Department of Health and Foundation SESP health
centers.
(i) Community Participation
31.       The establishment of a health minipost requires (i) a small rural
agglomeration which serves as a center for a rural community, (ii) the per-
ceived and expressed need of the population for community-based, simple health
care, (iii) an accessible health center, and (iv) the collaboration of the
local municipio administration (Prefeitura). Usually the social extensionists

Page 12
of ANCAR/RN start and stimulate the process, selecting communities, promoting
community awareness of health needs and assisting in the formation of a
Health Society. 1/
32.       Central points of such rural communities are (i) minor settlements
of smallholders, sharecroppers and laborers on former or actual, larger 2/ or
smaller 3/ farms, usually providing a marketplace, school and church; (ii) small
villages which in some cases are sub-districts of larger municipios, or (iii)
seats of small municipios. Category I communities typically comprise some 80
to 150 families within a radius of a few kilometers. Among the community
health schemes visited, examples are Povoado Cruz (Mfunicipio Currais Novos)
and Juazeiro (Parelhas) with about 500 and 640 people, respectively. In
contrast, examples for Category III are the small municipios around Umarizal:
Lucrecia with some 900 urban and 2,400 rural population, Vicosa with 390 urban
and 580 rural people, Riacho da Cruz with 970 and 1,500 and, finally, Olho
d'Agua dos Borges with some 900 urban and 2,800 rural population.
33.       Relative to other countries, the social acceptability of the
community health approach appears to pose no serious problems. After support
of local opinion-leaders in this field (mainly traditional midwives and
primary school teachers but also larger farmers, heads of municipalities and
physicians) is ensured, objectives, activities and requirements are discussed
with the community. Experience to date suggests that the need for MCH,
immunizations nutrition education and supervision of environmental sanitation
as well as the advantages of the community approach are being easily perceived
by the target group. In most cases, the majority of the population living in
the immediate vicinity of the post (village) join the Health Society immediately.
Membership of the scattered segments of the community, living in a distance of
more than about 1-1.5 kilometers, varies widely but is usually low. Notwith-
standing, they seek and receive the services offered.
34.       Among the Umarizal health societies (see para 31), for example,
Lucrecia had a membership of 80 families, Vicosa 56 "urban" and only 8 "rural"
families, Riacho da Cruz 46 and Olho d'Agua dos Borges a high membership of
nearly 450. In a sample of 18 health societies, the average number of members
was 124 families, ranging from 40 to 450.
35.       All Health Societies function as pre-paid cooperatives.  The majority
are also registered, non-profit corporations, with by-laws regulating activ-
ities. The four-member management 4/ is elected annually and is usually com-
posed of teachers, heads of municipalities and influential farmers. Monthly
membership contributions vary between societies, and in 1974 ranged from Cr $1
1/   Sociedade de Saude.
2/   Fazendas.
3/   Sitios.
4/   President, Vice-President, Secretary and Treasurer.



ANNEX 5
Page 13
to 2 (US cents 15-30). Members select a person from the community to be trained
as a local, minipost-based health worker. Meetings are held monthly and are
also attended by non-members as discussions include non-health conmmunity pro-
blems. In some cases, activities include fund-raising festivities and the
Societies resemble more general, community development foci.
(ii) The Health Minipost
36.       In most cases, the health minipost is located in one or two annex
rooms to schools, churches, storage buildings or homes. Frequently simple
mud buildings are renovated and adapted by the community at very low cost.
The size of such miniposts varies from about 20m2 to 50m2. They are owned
or rented by the Health Society. In some cases, municipalities pay for the
construction of larger, brick-built posts, comprising 3-4 rooms. In the
case of renovating existing structures, ANCAR/RN estimated the 1974 average
unit cost to be about Cr$1,370 (US$190).
37.       The minipost has only the modest elementary equipment, i.e., simple
furniture, syringes, tweezers, containers and materials for routine treatments.
The 1974 cost of equipment was about Cr$2,230 (US$310). Most of the equipment
of the existing miniposts was donated by UNICEF (para 11). Despite primitive
surroundings and conditions, the buildings and the equipment of all ten health
miniposts visited were well maintained and clean.
(iii) The Community Health Worker
38.       The Community Health Worker 1/ is required to (i) have completed a
primary education and be 18 to 35 years old; (ii) be elected by the members
of the Health Society; (iii) be trained for three months by Foundation SESP;
and (iv) be paid by the municipio administration. With the exception of a
few recent cases, all health workers are females. Most are selected at the
age of 17 to 20.
39.       Selection.  According to interviews with about 30 health workers--
10 selected from 1968-1973 and 20 in 1974--virtually all come from families
of smallholders, tenants or landless laborers. Despite very low pay, the job
appears to confer considerable status and to receive high preference among
village youth. Many health workers planning to become primary teachers or
nurses attend evening secondary school. Usually one to four candidates are
nominated for election. Participation in elections seems to be high.
40.    . Training.  Community health workers are trained in providing primary
and secondary care, curative and preventive services for three months in groups
of about 20 at the comprehensive and integrated care center (Unidade Mista) of
Foundation SESP in Acu (Central RN), which has 24 beds 2/ and 4 physicians.
Every year, they attend 15-day refresher courses at health centers of
1/   Orientador(a) de Saude.
2/   Of the 24 beds, 8 are maternity, 12 pediatrics and 4 general.



ANNEX 5
Page 14
Foundation SESP or the Department of Health in their region and once a month,
they gather at their supporting health center and receive instructions. Com-
pared to the initial training, however, refresher and in-service training
courses seem to be poorly structured and held rather irregularly.
41.       The three-month training is divided into didactic and clinical
classes. Foundation SESP developed the curriculum and a well-structured
200-paged textbook with simple but precise language. Didactic classes deal
with:
(a) Terminology and concepts.of epidemiology (7 pages);
(b) Communicable diseases, the major 21 grouped by modes of
transmission: infectious agent, reservoir, source of in-
fection, mode of transmission, incubation period, period
of communicability, symptoms and diagnosis, resistance and
susceptibility, occurrence and mortality rate, methods of
control by prevention and by control of patient and his
immediate environment (40 pages);
(c) Immunization; types and techniques (2 pages);
(d) Nursing techniques and application of pharmaceuticals (16 pages);
(e) Vital signs (6 pages);
(f) Laboratory tests (3 pages);
(g) MCH; including supervision of traditional midwives and home
visits (11 pages);
(h)  Environmental sanitation and personal hygiene, including water
supply and waste disposal (7 pages);
(i) Public health: concepts, activities, manpower and attitudes
(5 pages);
(j) Social psychology (20 pages);
(k) Use of audio-visual materials (7 pages);
(1) Nutrition: concepts, nutrition for infants, under-fives and
during pregnancy; taboos; food preparation (26 pages):
(m)  Use of short scripts for giving health and. nutrition education
classes (20 pages); and
(n) Monthly recocding system (10 pages).



ANTNEX 5
Page 15
42.       Clinical classes give the health workers in-service training in the
different sections of the health center, particularly in MCII, ambulatory care,
laboratory and archives, as well as in outreach services by assisting auxiliary
nurses of the health center in home visiting programs. The--e are weekly tests.
43.       The three month training cost of Cr$2,625 (US$365) per worker (see
para. 79) including transportation and boarding, is covered by the federal
PIPMO 1/ training program, by some municipio administrations and by community
Health Societies, which in 1974 contributed Cr$325 (US$45) per selected health
worker.
44.       Aiming at integration of traditional and official sectors, ANCAR/Rl's
community health approach also includes brief training of traditional sector
midwives for three-six days at health centers of Foundation SESP and SSP. They
then receive back-up support from the community health workers. So far only
a relatively small number of traditional midwives has been trained.
45.       Since both ANCAR/RN and Foundation SESP come under federal ministries,
community health workers do not receive training in family planning. However,
being paid by municipios, they subsequently are asked by heads of municipalities
and the Department of Health to participate in BEMFAM training programs. 2/ A
large number of health workers received BEMFA,M's three days training in Natal.
(iv) Community Health Activities
46.       As indicated by the curriculum, the emphasis of health services pro-
vided at the community level is being placed upon MCH, immunizations, nutri-
tion, health education and environmental sanitation. A Manual of Instructions,
prepared by ANCAR/RN, Foundation SESP and the Health Department, lists specific
activities, which are summarized in Table 3.
47.       In MCH, the formation of "Mothers' Clubs" and the annual "Minipost
Baby Contest" 3/ proved to be effective in promoting community participation.
In what has become a popular program, contests take place at local, municipal,
regional and finally, state levels and the winning infant or child is deter-
mined according to history and criteria of nutrition, immunization, hygiene,
education and behavior. Prizes given by both public and commercial sectors,
are relatively valuable. In MCH and nutrition, miniposts administer the food
supplementation efforts of the Federal Ministry of Health 4/ and CARITAS. Most
health workers give advice on birth control and distribute contraceptive pills,
supplied by BEMFAM, although these services are not contained in the Manual.
1/   Programa Intensivo de Preparacao de Mao-de-Obra, which comes under the
Ministry of Education (MEC).
2/   BEMFAM has a formal agreement only with the state government of RN
(para 11), but there is no federal population policy.
3/   Concurso Bebe Miniposto.
4/   These efforts have not yet become a regular, larger-scale program.

Page 17
48.       Health workers usually attend people at the minipost during the
morning and visit homes in the afternoon. In many cases, however, home
visits seem to be performed on a somewhat irregular basis (see para. 50).
Health workers typically give one health education class per week in schools.
Once a month they visit the health center (para. 61), in order to receive
supplies (vaccines) and instructions, and to buy minor sanitary material.
(v) Coverage and Utilization
49.       Interviews and records indicate that the spatial coverage of com-
munity health schemes varies more between miniposts than according to type
of service provided. This seems to iiiply that both preventive and curative
care are rated highly by the rural people but that there are significant
differences in program effectiveness between communities. In the case of a
typical minipost, practically all patients seem to come from within a radius
of 6-8 km and the vast majority from up to 3-4 km away. On the other hand,
outreach activities of the health worker typically cover a radius of only 1-2
km. The lack of transportation, e.g. a bicycle, was named as the major con-
straint.
50.       In the cases of the miniposts visited, the average utilization
rate was estimated to be more than ten cases per day, excluding outreach
activities. Records of 31 health miniposts for 1973, 1/ however, give an
average of 5.8 visits per day, of which 6% were cases of pregnant women,
23% visits of under five year-olds, and 71% cases of school children and
adults. The average number of outreach activities, including health education
classes and home visits, was only about 3.0 per week (Table 4).
51.       Table 4 presents utilization and outcome figures by different cate-
gories of services delivered for an average minipost, and for miniposts in the
Serido and Umarizal sub-areas (Juazeiro and Lucrecia). Delivery of services
shows the heavy emphasis which is being placed on prevention and considerable
efforts in the fields of health and nutrition education and environmental
sanitation. They also show, however, that performance still needs to be sub-
stantially improved. Low vaccination rates, primarily in the cases of BCG and
measles, are due to the limited supply of vaccines; low laboratory test rates
reflect deficient integration of miniposts with the health center level (para
58).
(vi) Financing of Recurrent Costs
52.       The inadequate financing of the operating costs of community health
posts constitutes one of the major constraints on program effectiveness.
Operating costs are supposed to be paid for by (i) members of the Health Society
through the prepayment scheme; (ii) non-member patients on a fee-for-service
1/   January through November 1973.  Calculations based on six working days
per week.

basis; (iii) local municipio administration, paying the salary of the com-
munity health worker; and (iv) federal and state governments by providing
supplies, supervision and back-up support.
53.       In 1974, operating cost of the community health program was about
Cr$12,600 (US$1,750) per health minipost or, assuming an average coverage of
about 750 people, US$2.35 per capita. Nearly 80% of cost were for personnel.
ANCAR/RN paid for 57% of operating cost, Foundation SESP for 6.5%, Department
of Health for 13%, the municipios for 10%, the communities for 11% and "others"
for 2.5%. However, these figures are somewhat misleading inasmuch as the
bulk of these costs were incurred for the overall administration and super-
vision of the program at higher levels (e.g., ANCAR/RN's social extensionists)
rather than at the community level. Excluding costs of supervision and back-
up support, health minipost activities are basically being financed by the
community, the municipio and the federal Central Supplier of Medicaments
(CEME). 1/
54.       CEME is a relatively recent addition to national health policy and
comes under the Ministry of Health. It distributes basic drugs free of charge
to rural health centers and posts. In most cases, supplies seemed to be ade-
quate and distribution effective. However, drug prescription rules (para. 65)
prevent health miniposts from keeping a complete, basic drug inventory, unless
they have a visiting physician.
55.       Most municipalities pay extremely low salaries to the community
health workers, ranging in 1974 from Cr$50-160 (US$7-22) a month and in some
cases corresponding to less than 25% of the legal minimum wage. 2/ Payment is
usually delayed by one to three months. In some cases, salaries are not being
paid at all. A large number of health workers depend on their families or the
Health Society for support.
56.       Based on monthly membership quotas ranging from Cr$1 to 2, Health
Societies typically collect between Cr$50 and 220 (US$7.50 to 30) per month.
According to interviews, these funds only cover purchase of basic sanitary
materials and of some drugs. In some cases, income supplements of about
Cr$30 (US$4.20) per month are being paid to health workers.
57.       In virtually all miniposts visited, non-members of the Health Society
received services free. 3/ According to health workers, this led in some cases
to a reduction in membership rates. It seems to be difficult for health
workers to enforce fee-for-service payment by non-members and to prove
1/   Central de Medicamentos.
2/ According to ANCAR/RN, the average 1974 wage of a community health worker
was Cr$80 in the Umarizal sub-area and Cr$100 in the Serido sub-area.
3/   An exception was Juazeiro (Parelhas) where non-members, for example, have
to pay Cr$1.00 for an intravenous injection if administered at the health
post, and Cr$1,50 if administered at home.



ANNEX 5
Page 20
patients' capacity to pay. Primarily the more scattered segments of rural
populations, whose income is less well known, appear to take advantage of
this situation.
(b) The Health Center Level: Supervision and Referral
58.       Probably the most serious obstacle to efficiency and effectiveness
of community-based health services is posed by the virtual lack of medical
supervision by higher tiers of the official health system, i.e., health centers
of Foundation SESP and the Department of Health. Moreover, referral is poorly
organized with people depending largely on self-referral to urban hospitals.
However, non-medical back-up support is provided by ANCAR/RN in the fields of
community development and administration.
(i) Foundation SESP
59.       Although back-up support is poor in both cases, there is still a
substantial difference between quality of services provided to health mini-
posts by the under-staffed and under-financed facilities of the Health Depart-
ment (para 24) and the effectively operated health centers of Foundation SESP
(paras 13 and 25). The latter's four basic health centers 1/ in the Project
area (Chart 2) comprise outpatient and two-three bed inpatient facilities.
The relatively modern brick-building comprises several spacious rooms, which
contain all necessary equipment. The staff usually includes (i) a full-time
physician, (ii) two Auxiliary Nurses for Health Promotion (Visitadoras) 2/
with six months training, (iv) a laboratory auxiliary with seven months
training, and (v) a sanitary auxiliary with about four months training.
Motivation appears to be high.
60.       Activities heavily emphasize preventive, outreach and curative,
outpatient services for predominantly urban population, i.e., on MCH, train-
ing and supervision of midwives, immunizations, environmental sanitation,
health education (through classes in schools and home visits) and screening
of patients. Spatial coverage seems to be about 30 km, with most patients
coming from the urban vicinity. Utilization rates range from 20 to 40 cases
per day. In the case of the health center in Acu--which has large inpatient
and outpatient facilities and serves as training center for the community
health workers (para 40)--coverage is estimated to be 50-100 km and
utilization 50-60 cases per day.
61.       Because of the heavy outpatient caseload, only residual time is
allotted to supervising and supporting rural health miniposts. Visitadoras
are supposed to visit frequently community health workers but on average
visit miniposts only once a month, and then somewhat irregularly (e.g., Acu
and Angicos). In many cases, (e.g., Santa Cruz), monthly visits of community
1/ Unidade Basica de Saude/Unidade Sanitaria.
2/ Visitadora de Saneamento.



ANNEX 5
Page 21
health workers to the urban health center substitute for even these rare visits.
There they discuss their problems, attend refresher courses and receive in-
structions and materials. Experience shows that this results in extremely
poor supervision. Community health workers feel isolated with their problems,
seriously affecting motivation.
62.       In contrast, Foundation SESP's support of supply, training and re-
fresher courses, and referral seems more effective. More importantly, the
basic philosophy of Foundation SESP's health center activities and of
ANCAR/RN's community-based health services is the same, facilitating colla-
boration.
(ii) The Health Department
63.       The Health Department operates 11 health centers 1/ in the project
area (Chart 2), which serve as supervision and referral points for health
mlniposts. Buildings comprise three-five rooms and equipment is poor.
Maintenance standards are low. A physician is usually employed on a part-time
basis. In Parelhas, for example, a physican was contracted for three days
of five hours a week and paid Cr$1,550 (US$215) per month. Other staff
,includes a part-time dentist, and two auxiliary nurses 2/ per month who have
three months training and receive the minimum wage (about US$35). Grossly in-
adequate supplies of drugs, vaccines and sanitary materials were named as
another serious constraint on delivery of services. Administrative workload
appears to be excessive and poorly organized.
64.       In contrast to Foundation SESP, much emphasis is being placed by
Health Department personnel on episodic, curative services rather than on
outreach, preventive activities. These activities are basically confined to
immunization campaigns. However, services include family planning in colla-
boration with BEMFAM. Utilization rates seem to be relatively high. In
Parelhas, there were more than 50 cases per day.
65.       In most instances, support of health miniposts is limited to supply
of available materials and vaccines. In Umarizal, however, the physician
visits miniposts every two weeks for four hours, attending patients directly
rather than only serious cases screened by the health worker; this pattern
seems to be common in the sub-area. In Olho d'Agua do Borges, for example,
two physicians attend about 30 not previously attended patients every Sunday
at the health minipost. The physicians are contracted by the municipio and
paid Cr$800 (US$110) per month. This system reflects the prevailing attitude
among Hlealth Department physicians as opposed to the community health approach.
The physician remains the point of first contact and is reluctant to delegate
1/   Including the state maternity hospital in Umarizal, which is also
supervising health miniposts.
2/   Atendentes.



Page 22
responsibilities.  Obviously, there are serious detrimental effects on com-
munity health activities. People prefer to wait and to be attended by the
physician. 1/ Curative services are rated highly and prevention is neglected.
Community participation in health promotion is lacking.
(iii) ANCAR/RN
66.       The only effective support and supervision is provided by ANCAR/RN's
domestic extension service. Besides its role in selecting communities and
promoting community participation, it coordinates activities of all agencies
involved and supervises day-to-day operations at the community level. Its
support is necessarily non-medical in content. There are eight social ex-
tensionists in the Project area, i.e., in Currais Novos, Parelhas, Caico
(two), Santa Cruz, Santana dos Matos (two) and Umarizal. They all appear
highly motivated, and dedicated to community health work, and collaborate
effectively with the community health workers. Usually they visit health
miniposts once a week. The lack of transportation is the most serious
constraint on their effectiveness. Most depend on their male extensionist
counterparts for a vehicle. However, although learning from experience and
compensating for much of the lack of supervision by the official health
system, they have not gained the medical skills required for adequate
administration of the community-based health system.
D. The Project
67.       As shown by the preceding analysis, ANCAR/RN's community health
post program constitutes an innovative, basically successful approach to
low-cost health services delivery at the grass roots level. However, impact
on health status in the project area is as yet constrained by (a) the limited
number of health miniposts, giving coverage to perhaps some 7% of rural pop-
ulation; 2/ (b) the poor integration with mostly ineffectively operated higher
levels of the official health services, i.e, the lack of supervison and back-
up support; (c) the insufficient financing of operating costs at both mini-
post and health center levels; and (d) the poor logistics of supply.
68.       Most of these are operational, managerial and financial issues rather
than predominantly medical problems. Measures to improve health care delivery
would thus be naturally integrated into overall project implementation. Fol-
lowing the general project approach and considering limited implementing cap-
abilities, a two-phase development of the health component is proposed. Under
1/   According to the community health worker in Olho d'Agua do Borges, almost
all cases seen by the physician could be attended by her. There are
people who retu:-l every Sunday with the same minor illness to see the
doctor.
2/   Rural population in the Project area is calculated to be 280,000 in
1974, and average coverage of the 28 miniposts is estimated to be
about 700 rural people.



ANNEX 5
Page 23
the first Phase, the community health system would be improved and expanded
in selected municipios of the project area, with the objective of testing
interventions and developing criteria for larger-scale implementation. Phase
2 would implement the health component to the balance of the project area.
(a) The First Phase
(i) Selection of Municipios
69.       In selecting municipios in the Serido and Umarizal sub-areas of the
Project area, consideration was given to (a) intrinsic characteristics of the
municipio, such as area, population and land use, and (b) existence or ab-
sence of delivery systems, such as extension service, cotton gins, bank
branches, cooperatives and health miniposts. Three types of municipios were
selected in each sub-area, 1/ of which one already benefits from the existence
of most or all of these variables, and the third represents an "intermediate"
situation. Table 5 characterizes the 11 selected municipios as to their avail-
ability of health services (see also Chart 2). The existence of hospitals in
municipio centers was not taken into account in establishing selection cri-
teria. With their emphasis on curative medicine, they do not provide back-
up support to health miniposts and do not contribute (and may even be detri-
mental) to the community health approach.
1/   In the case of the municipio of Umarizal, four very small,
"satellite" municipios were included as well.

70.       The four municipios of the Serido sub-area fall into Administrative
Health Region III and all selected municipios of the Umarizal sub-area into
Region VI (see para 12 and Chart 1). Administrative centers of these Health
Regions are Caico and Pau dos Ferros, respectively.
(ii) Selection of Communities
71.       In determining the location of health miniposts in the selected
municipios, ANCAR/RN's community development approach would be followed and
improved (paras 31-35), making sure that health services are not "imposed"
upon the community by the Project. Because of the limited availability of
data, a survey would probably first have to be conducted, in order to iden-
tify suitable rural agglomerations which serve as centers for rural communi-
ties of some 500 to 2,000 people.  S  ±Lal extensionists would then promote
awareness of health needs and active community involvement, by discussing
health status, available resources, possible interventions, and the community's
role under the reformed system with local people. This would result in the
formation of a Health Society and a request for assistance in setting-up a
community-based health scheme.



ANNEX 5
Page 25
72.       Ideally, a rural "health community" would comprise about 1,000
people or 185 families. 1/ To arrive at this figure, the following average
utilization rates have been considered. 2/  Assuming a well established,
efficient community health system and excluding outreach services, there
would be an average of about two contacts of the adult segments of the
population with the system per year, four contacts per school child per year,
six annual contacts of under five-year olds and, finally, five contacts of
pregnant women per year. Given the age distribution in the rural Northeast, 3/
this implies about 3,400 annual cases per 1,000 population 4/ or (assuming
300 workdays per year ) 11.3 cases per day. 5/ If an average of three patients
are attended per hour at the health post, this caseload would require about
half the workday of a community health worker. The other four hours would
be available for more time-consuming outreach activities, i.e. health and
nutrition education and promotion of environmental sanitation through classes
in schools and home visits. Probably an average of 2 families could be
visited per day or every household in a community of 1,000 once or twice
a year. However, considering the vastly higher outpatient workload during
an initial period, home visits would only be started later.
73.       In the case of effective supervision and support, the utilization
of a health minipost after an initial period could probably be increased to
about 14 daily visits and the coverage of 1,250 people. 6/ It has further
been taken into account that perhaps some 20% of the population reside in
sparsely populated rural areas which cannot be economically be served by a
community health scheme. In some cases, finally, population and community
size distribution patterns would require miniposts to cover less than 1,000
people. Given a rural population of about 67,000 in the selected municipios
(Table 5), it is assumed that about 55 community health schemes would be
improved or developed under the Project, covering an average of 1,000 people
each.

Every health center would have one or two vehicles for supervision of mini-
posts at a cost of Cr$30,000 (US$4,170) each.

and successful pattern developed by ANCAR/RN and Foundation SESP (paras 38-45).

79.       The training of health workers in excess of the number of Phase 1

(US$365) per worker and includes transportation, room and board.
80.       Auxiliary Nurses.  Community health workers would be supervised by
health center-based auxiliary nurses with about nine months training, which would
correspond to Foundation SESP's present Visitadoras de Saneamento (paras 59
and 61). They would supervise an average of five health miniposts each and
visit communities, depending on distance and location, once a week for a
full day or preferably twice a week for a few hours. Since health centers
would be located at the seats of the municipios, there would be additional
auxiliary nurses for outreach health promotion among urban and semi-urban
populations (Table 7).
81.       If one allows for a 20% drop-out rate, the Project would train about
20 auxiliary nurses as Foundation SESP facilities. The 1974 cost of the 9
months training is estimated to be approximately Cr$7,500 (US$1,040) per
enrollee.



ANNEX 5
Page 29
82.       Social Extensionists.  ANCAR/RN's social extensionists and assistant
social exten-onists would continue promoting community participation in health
services and providing non-medical, opcrational support and supervision to
community health workers (para. 66).  However,  given effective technical super-
vision of miniposts by the health center-based auxiliary nurses, the extension-
ists' involvement in day-to-day minipost operations would be somewhat reduced
and their other community development responsibilities increased. They would
visit health miniposts about once a week and on average supervise four-five
community health workers each. Transport facilities would be improved. Table
7 gives the distribution of social extensionists by municipios.
83.       The Project's health component would provide for the 60 days pre-
service training of 16 social extensionists. 1/ Unlike at present, this
training would need to include a two week-course in the medical aspects of
community health. The curriculum for this course has already been developed
by ANCAR/RN and Foundation SESP; training cost is about Cr$6,600 (US$920) per
extensionist.
(v) Operating and Financing Community Health Schemes
84.       Since Phase 1 would constitute a learning-by-doing process, the
Project would provide for payment of (a) the minimum wage of about US$35 per
month to community health workers; (b) salaries of Cr$1,313 (US$182) to super-
vising auxiliary nurses; and (c) salaries of about Cr$2,100 (US$292) to super-
vising social extensionists. The Project would thus finance most of the
rural system's recurrent costs over the first years. The Department of Health
and Foundation SESP need to support the project to (a) adequately finance
other operating costs of the eight supporting health centers; (b) fully
staff these centers; and (c) ensure sufficient supply and improved distribution
of drugs (CEME), vaccines and sanitary materials.
85.       At the community level, after further assessment of both capacity
and willingness to pay, an increase of membership subscriptions to the
Health Society would be promoted, raising monthly contributions in 1974
prices from about Cr$1-2 (para 35) to Cr$3 (US 45 cents) per family. Non-
members would be required to contribute equally on a fee-for-service basis,
with a small premium added (to encourage membership).
(b) The Second Phase
86.       Under Phase 2, larger-scale implementation would follow after two-
three years, drawing on the results of evaluation of the cost-effectiveness
of Phase 1 interventions. Therefore, at this stage, the Phase 2 health
component can be quantified only in general terms.
1/   Allowing for the fact that there are presently eight social extensionists
in the project area and two in the first-phase municipios (Parelhas and
Umarizal)(para 66), that additional social extensionists are needed for
other health-related community development tasks and that there would
be one supervisor.

87.       The rural population of the Project area is estimated to be approx-
imately 280,000 people in 1974 and about 290,000 in 1976. It has earlier been
assumed (para 73) that perhaps 80% of this population or 230,000 rural people
can be reached by community health schemes, of which 55,000 would be included
under Phase 1 and 175,000 under Phase 2. In the Phase 2 Project area, there
are presently 20 miniposts and 12 health centers, of which 3 are being operated
by Foundation SESP and 9 by the Health Department (Chart 2).
88.       Based on the above facilities and manpower ratios (paras 73-83),
the second phase of the Project would thus provide for (i) the adaptation,
construction and equipment of about 155 health miniposts; (ii) the construc-
tion and equipment (including vehicles) of 10 health centers, (iii) the
training of 175 community health workers; (iv) the training of 50 auxiliary
nurses, 1/ and (v) the training of 32 social extensionists 2/ (see Annex 10,
Tables 5 and 5a). Project Phase 2 does not pay for salaries of health
personnel. Since the existing health centers are either being operated by
Foundation SESP or are expected to be gradually improved under the State
government's regionalization program, Project Phase 2 does not provide for
renovation of existing facilities.
(c) Project Cost Estimates
89.       Tables 5 and 5a of Annex 10 present cost estimates for Phases 1
and 2 of the project. The cost of adapting, renovating, construction and
equipment facilities would be about US$216,500 for Phase 1 and US$630,000
for Phase 2; the manpower training cost would be US$59,000 and US$145,000,
respectively. The cost of Phase 1 salaries, provided under the Project,
would be about US$740,000. At the community level, total cost (Phases
1 and 2) of community health facilities, training health workers and Phase
1 salaries would be US$656,000, whereas total cost of facilities and manpower
at the second tier amounts to US$1,156,000. Thus total project cost of
the Rural Development Project's basic health services component would be
approximately US$1,749,000.
(d) Organization and Management
90.       The general Project Unit and the Monitoring Group, which are to be
located in CEPA/RN, would, respectively, be responsible for coordinating and
controlling and for monitoring and evaluating the health services component.
The staff of the Project Unit would include a human resources specialist, who
would deal with education, communication and health components and with gen-
eral community development aspects of the Project (see Annex 12 on Management
and Organization).

(i) Implementation
91.       The health component would be implemented by the Department of
Health, Foundation SESP and ANCAR/RN, which would form a task force. The
Department of Health would be the coordinating agency. The health facili-
ties module would be implemented by the Department of Health and Foundation
SESP. Foundation SESP would also develop the training programs and provide
technical assistance to the other agencies, and ANCAR/RN would promote and
develop the community health schemes. The task force would closely collabo-
rate with Health Societies and municipio administrations. It would report
to the Project Unit which would have disbursing authority.
(ii) Monitoring and Evaluation
92.       The Monitoring Group would essentially have a supervisory role and
would draw heavily on Foundation SESP's and ANCAR/RN's expertise and existing
recording systems. These agencies together with the Department of Health
would be responsible for data collection and day-to-day monitoring of opera-
tions, and would report to the Monitoring Group. Evaluative research on com-
munity health system's performance in terms of costs, resource utilization,
services delivered and impact on health status would be conducted by the RN
University program CRUTAC (para 15) and other university institutes (e.g.
Brasilia) on a continuing basis. Under the Bank's new health policy, Bank
staff would participate in evaluations of the cost-effectiveness of Phase 1
interventions and in the development of criteria for the second phase of the
Project (see also Annex 8 on Monitoring and Evaluation).

NORTHEAST BRAZIL
RIO GRANDE DO NORTE RURAL DEVELOPMENT PROEJCT
Infrastructure, Marketing, Cooperatives
A. Infrastructure - Physical Distribution of Delivery Systems
Infrastructure
1.        The road network in the project area is not a major bottleneck
especially since movements of harvested crops and inputs are generally during
the dry season and because of the largely rocky nature of the terrain.
Two major paved arteries (in the north and center-south of the area) ensure
easy communication with Natal. No intervention with respect to the road
infrastructure would be undertaken within the project. However, the State
and municipalities would be expected to provide for the normal maintenance
and eventual upgrading of existing facilities through their normal operations.
2.        The present supply of essential production-related services is
characterized by inadequacies in the total amount and distribution of agri-
cultural extension, banking services, cooperatives, physical input delivery,
and cotton processing. Crop transport is dominated by private truckers.
Agricultural Extension
3.        A full treatment of the present situation is in Annex 2.  Probably
less than 60% of the project area is actually covered by the existing extension
network of ANCAR. Effective extension assistance is essentially directed to
larger farmers; hence, probably no more than 10% of the total number of farmers
in the project area receive assistance. The major bottlenecks to an efficient
extension coverage are the limited manpower resources devoted to field opera-
tions and the misallocation of the extensionist's efforts, currently con-
centrated on the processing of agricultural credit to large farmers.
4.        There are wide differences in the degree of extension coverage among
the sub-areas. Overall, Serido receives less coverage than others, such as
sub-area 1 (Pedro Avelino). Among the municipios actually receiving effective
coverage, sub-areas 3 and 4 (Umarizal and Santa Cruz) appear relatively better
off.
Gins
5.        There are 13 gins located within 10 municipios of the project area.
Some of the cotton produced within the area probably also is processed in a
gin outside the project area, in Joao Camara, close to sub-area 1 (Pedro



ANNEX 6
Page 2
Avelino) and possibly in another gin in Teniente Ananias, close to sub-area 3
(Umarizal). Most of the gins are located in the southern and eastern parts
of the project area (south of Serido and Santa Cruz area).
6.        There are 28 gins in the entire State, equipped with 7,650 saws and
113 rollers, providing a total ginning capacity of close to 195,000 tons of
seed cotton. The 13 gins of the project area represent 46% of the saws and
55% of the rollers, providing a ginning capacity of 92,000 tons, or a little
less than 50% of the State total. This could probably be raised to a capacity
of 115,000 tons, using longer working days and the equipment for part of
January and February. In contrast, actual production of the project area in
the 1973-74 season was about .48,000 tons.
7.        The gins are distributed unevenly among the four sub-areas;  only
one gin is located in sub-area 3 (Umarizal) compared to seven in Serido.
However, this unbalanced distribution of gins does not reflect cotton produc-
tion distribution which is almost equally divided among the four sub-areas
(about 25% each, Table 1). The ginning capacity in the center of the project
area (north of Serido, east of Umarizal and south of Pedro Avelino) is espe-
cially low in relation to cotton production.
Bank Branches
8.        The project area is covered by agencies of Banco do Brasil (BB) and
Banco do Nordeste do Brasil (BNB). BB coverage is uneven: 3 agencies (Santa
Cruz, Currais Novas and Caico) operate in the project area, providing services
to 32 municipios (the whole Serido and Santa Cruz sub-regions). Hiowever, the
21 other municipios depending on five agencies located outside the project
area: sub-area 1 (Pedro Avelino) depends on three, sub-area 3 (Umarizal) also
on three, one of which (Catole do Rocha) is located in the neighboring state
of Paraiba. Some municipios, especially in sub-area 3, may be located as far
as 200 km from the BB branch they depend on. The situation in sub-area 3 would
improve with the opening of a BB agency in Umarizal by the end of 1975.
9.        BNB coverage is somewhat more even since 3 agencies are located
within the project area (Santa Cruz, Angicos and Jardin de Serido), providing
services to the 39 municipios of sub-areas 1, 2 and 4. The 14 municipios of
sub-area 3 (Umarizal) depend on 3 agencies located outside the project area,
one of which is outside the State (Catole do Rocha).
10.       Hence, sub-areas 2 and 4 (Serido and Santa Cruz) appear to be better
off in bank coverage than areas 1 and 3. Sub-area 3 is poorly served, being
divided among 3 different brancnes of both BB and BNB. The difference in
focus between BB an-d BNB operations, the latter concentrating its efforts
through cooperative:.s, further suggests that wide areas are poorly serviced
by bank agencies.



ANNEX 6
Page 3
Cooperatives
11.       There are presently six cooperatives in the project area:  two are
located in sub-area 3 (Umarizal), two are in sub-area 4 (Santa Cruz) and one
each in sub-areas 1 (Pedro Avelino) and 2 (Serido).. In addition, there are
three other cooperatives: Acu (close to sub-area 1) and Apodi and Martins
(close to sub-area 3), all located outside the project area but whose zones
of influence include small parts of the project area. With two cooperatives
in Agreste (San Antonio and San Jose do Campestre), these constitute 11
cooperatives which are forming a Central Cooperative in Natal. These coop-
eratives vary widely in numbers of members, functions and success. Three
cooperatives (Umarizal, Santa Cruz and Sao Tome) are engaged in seed cotton
processing. Table 2 illustrates the distribution in membership, physical
assets and seed cotton marketing, pointing to the unequal weight and impor-
tance of cooperative coverage among sub-areas. An important feature is the
relative absence in sub-areas 1 and 2, Pedro Avelino and Florania, with each
having only one cooperative, limited membership and no ginning facilities.
12.       The most successful cooperative is Umarizal which has been growing
rapidly, capturing potential and actual membership of the neighboring coop-
eratives of Martins and Alexandria. As of November 1974 total membership
was 1,260. Its geographical zone of intense influence extends over 15
municipios, with members coming from 25 different municipios. BNCC lends
money for infrastructure to cooperatives. Although BNCC does not yet operate
in the project area, the Government hopes to expand BNUC's operations and
scope. Parallel to the project, BNCC will evaluate cooperatives' infrastruc-
ture needs; the Government may decide to finance some of these infrastructure
investments as complements to the project.
Physical Delivery of Inputs
13.       CIDA (Integrated Agricultural Development Company) is a newly created
State-owned input supply and development company established partially to re-
place COFAN/RN, the Agricultural Development Corporation, which hitherto
had principal responsibility for the official distribution of agricultural
inputs throughout the State. 1/ There are 30 COFAN outlets in the State, 20
of which are located in the "Casas do Agricultor" (literally the "Houses of
Farmers", which may house extension services and other services to farmers);
the remaining 10 are simple "postos" (selling points). Total COFAN sales are
limited, and, although doubled in real terms since 1970, probably did not
1/   CIDA will also include the former CIMPARN (Companhia de Implantacao de
Projectos Agrarios do Rio Grande do Norte), mainly responsible for the
settlement project of Vilas Rurais on the northern coast of the State.
It will finally be responsible for dam construction, a responsibility
previously assigned to the Secretariat of Agriculture. COFAN was prev-
iously operated through "convenios" with the Secretariat of Agriculture,
INFAOL and SUDENE, while receiving loans from several banks (BB, BNB).
The financial and organization structure of CIDA has not yet been estab-
lished definitively.

exceEd Cr$5.5 million for 1974 for the whole State 1/. COFAN purchases
include agricultural equipment (35% to 45% of all purchases), fertilizers
(5% to 10%), insecticides (10%), seeds (5% to 10%) and veterinary inputs (15%).
COFAN operations were plagued by managerial inefficiency, as witnessed by
excessive stocks throughout the year.
14.       Fifteen of the total 30 State outlets are located in the project area.
Sales in these outlets barely represent 25% of the State total, or approximately
Cr$1.35 million; more than 50% of this amount is transacted in the Serido sub-

15.       Numerous small private outlets are scattered throughout the State
and the project area. Their volume of sales is not known; however, they are
of great importance in view of the inadequacies of the COFAN network, despite
generally higher prices they are mainly supplied by firms from the large
cities of the Northeast: Recife, Fortaleza, Natal, Campina Grande and Joao
Pessoa.
16.       Current State-level plans are for CIDA to perform substantially
better than its predecessor, COFAN. CIDA would be expected to provide for
timely distribution of inputs in all its outlets located in the project area.
New outlets, both within and outside the project area, should be contemplated
by CIDA in its regular planning program.
