B. 'Marketing
Minimum Price System
17.       A minimum price scheme was introduced by decree in 1966.  It is
currently operated by Banco do Brasil and the Production Financing Commission
(CFP) of the Ministry of Agriculture. cEP determines the minimum price level
for a number of agricultural cormodities in each major region. This price
determination takes place several monthis beforc the crop season to which
prices will apply (e.g. prices set in December 1974 for the 1975/76 crop
season). Prices are fixed for a "base-state" (Ceara in the Northeast, for
cotton) and adjustments are made for the neighboring states, and their
eventual geographical sub-divisions. In recent years, minimum prices have
represented from 60 to 70% of the actual market price.
18.       The minimum price system offers three possibilities for farmers
at the end of the crop season: (a) 180 day-term loan (representing 70
to 80% of the value of output estimated at the minimum price) without neces-
sary sale to CFP; (b) 90 to 300 day-tern  loan (for 100% of the value of
output) with possibility of sale to CFP, in which case the farmer is free to
sell his product to third parties at a higher market price at any time within
the loan term, the sale to CFP otherwise becoming automatic at the end of the
term; and (c) immediate sale to CFP at minimum price. Under the last two
options official grading of the product is required, as well as storage in
an officially acceptable place.
19.       Because there is a rather large difference between the minimum price
and the actual market price, the minimum price mechanism is becoming increas-
ingly inoperative. In effect, sales to CFP at the minimum price (especially
cotton) often concern low quality products which could not be sold elsewhere.
Such a process may act as a disincentive for improving quality of production.
20.       In most instances, especially for cotton, the Government may well
want to fix the minimum price considerably closer to the actual market
price. Alternatively, it might be possible (at least for cotton) to arrange
a "start of season" minimum price followed by a ;'mid--season" minimum
price which would be retroactive to prior sales.
Food Crops Marketing
21.       Beans and corn are the main subsistence crops in the project area
and are likely to continue having considerable importance for low-income
producers, larger farmers generally not being interested in producing them



ANNEX 6
Page 6
(Annex 4). 1/ However, a "subsistence mentality" as opposed to an "exchange
mentality" does not necessarily describe producers of food crops. Many small
producers live so close to the margin of subsistence that they cannot afford
to stock their own production for consumption throughout the year. They must
sell their beans and corn immediately after harvest, and "buy them back" on
credit later on (this is further aggravated by the need for cash to hire
labor for cotton harvesting); in this way, they sell their produce when prices
are down and buy it back when they are up. The vast majority of food crops
entering the market are sold at the local "ferias" (market fairs).
22.       Marketing of beans and corn is also subject to problems of consump-
tion habits (taste and categorization by class). The Northeast upper classes
for instance, do not buy the type of beans produced and consumed by the North-
east peasant, feiiao macassar (or Feijao de corda), preferring another more
expensive variety, feijao mulatinho, imported in large part from the southern
part of the country. However, as Center-South agriculture becomes increasingly
export oriented, and the opportunity cost of growing crops for local consumption
on these lands increases the Northeast might take over the production of domes-
tically consumed food products like corn and beans.
23.       The project would attempt to increase the production and product-
ivity of food crops, possibly to a level of marketable surplus. Through the
provision of agricultural credit for cotton production, especially at harvest
time, the need to sell food crops immediately after harvest would be greatly
reduced. Concurrently the project would aim to promote on-farm conservation
of beans and corn (Annex 4) to permit regular supply for on-farm consumption
and to delay sales of surplus production to benefit from higher prices during
the off-season. The project, through the experimentation interventions (Annex
4), would further encourage research on new improved varieties taking into
account consumers' tastes and habits. It would finally attempt to identify
new food crops (e.g., sorghum) which could complement existing ones.
Seed Cotton Marketing
24.       Picking of perennial cotton (Moco) commences in July and usually
continues into November and occasionally, as in the 1974 season, into December.
1/   The prevalence of bean and corn production reflects to a certain extent
the severe institutional barriers to planting anything else. In general,
the short cycle of a crop is a landowner's hedge against a tenant putting
down roots in his land. The sharecropper, tenant farmer and occupant are
traditionally prohibited from planting perennial crops because they are
considered fixed real proDerty, and if the tenant is expelled, he has
the legal right to idemnification for his property. In the case of perennial
cotton, this explains the relatively high turnover rate of sharecroppers
on large properties. For a full treatment, see "'Rural Development Issues
and Options in Northeast Brazil' IBRD Report No. 665a BR, June 23, 1975



ANNEX 6
Page 7
The ginners normally start buying in July, closing down the gins in December
or January, although, with additional seed cotton supplies, climate conditions
would allow satisfactory ginning through to the end of February.
Producer Delivery and Selling Methods
25.       Officially, ginners may only purchase from landowners who have
registered with the gin through a process in which, at the landowner's
request, the local "land registry" of the applicable Municipio forwards a
registration card to the gin; except for very rare exceptions, almost all
sharecropper production is sold through the landowner's account. Ginners
are officially obliged to maintain separate accounts against each landowner
registration and render a return to the Municipio at the end of the season
stating quantity of deliveries made (in theory the information is used in the
process of re-assessing land values and land taxes). Ginners do purchase
from "unregistered" producer-landowners but then ofticially render a notifica-
tion of such deliveries to the tax authorities.
26.       It follows, therefore, that there are no middlemen buyers officially
authorized, only middlemen "transporters" of cotton. In practice, however,
the "transporter" very often becomes a middleman buyer through a series of
unofficial mechanisms which are largely associated with the producer's need
for "cash flow" credit during the latter part of the flowering season and
through the picking season.  In brief, the "transporter" makes monetary ad-
vances to the smaller growers (these advances he may have obtained from the
ginner initially) which have the effect of purchasing the crop in advance--
at levels often below real values. The project would attempt, via improved
credit distribution to farmers (Annex 3), to limit the gin's use of credit to
exert a semi-monopoly position over farmers. Informal and precooperative
market groupings of small farmers, organized with the assistance of extension-
ists, may allow small farmers to deal directly with the gins, which are often
willing to collect the cotton from the farms if quantities are sufficient.
Improved grading procedures may also contribute substantially (para 31).
With regard to sales from unregistered properties, both large and small,
complete land registration would be encouraged.
27.       Actual transport from farm to gin is relatively simple.  Smaller
producers deposit job lots in small roadside shelters and arrange collections
dates with the transporter during the weekly market day at the Municipio's
central "township". Delivery distances vary considerably and 80 km is not
uncommon. (However, transporter charges are consistently quoted at Cr$ 0.05
per kg irrespective of distance.)   In addition the transporter may receive
an end of season bonus from the ginner if agreed delivery targets are met.
Seed Cotton Grading/Lint Cotton Classification
28.       Seed cotton is graded as it enters the gin from delivery trucks in
sacks and classified after ginning in 180 kilo bales. The grading is of
prime concern to the producer, affecting the price he receives, and also



ANNEX 6
Page 8
serving as a sorting mechanism for the ginner in producing an even quality
batch flow of cotton then classified by type (fibre quality) and by length,
for sale to the spinning industry. There should be a close correlation be-
tween top seed cotton grades at the input side of the gin and top lint classi-
fications at the output side. However, this is not necessarily so at many
gins and complaints regarding the grades applied are very prevalent among
producers, particularly the smaller producers.
29.       Officially all gins should have a government classifier positioned
at each gin; in practice the coverage is incomplete. Even where official
classifiers are operative there appears to be no "audit" of their work stand-
ards (e.g. attempts to correlate input grades with output classification)
and there are thus widely different standards applied by different gins.
However, available information from selected gins in the area indicates that
the Moco cotton of the State is in fact relatively uniform. Generally, only
very short cotton (possibly mixed with some annual cotton), very dirty or
damaged cotton is rejected. Where gins are using a large number of buying
grades, such grades might be either fictitious or necessary only to compensate
for the gin's ongoing processing inefficiency.
30.       The belief by many growers that cotton is unnecessarily downgraded
at the gins requires attention and steps need to be taken to restore producer
confidence. This possibly implies several steps:
(a) Advice at field level as to what grades might be expected
coupled with advice on how to separate production into
grades at farm level (an extension service task);
(b) Tighter control of classifiers at gins, possibly coupled with
a check system and definite Moco and non-Moco grades at those
gins which accept both perennial and annual cotton. An
assurance would be sought at negotiations that the Government
of RN would effectively implement in the project area its
cotton grading regulations; and
(c) A producer sack stamp and identitication system, allowing
the source of job lot collection by transporters to be
specifically identified as a further control on transporters.
31.       Such actions would bear implications for the whole perennial cotton
production area of the Northeast. The project, however, could provide the
testing grounds for initiating these steps. Gins located in the project area
and supplied exclusively by project area cotton could thus be selected and
submitted to various alternative measures designed to improve grading and
classitication for the producers' benefit. Such actions should be combined
with interventions of the agricultural extension service. The Project Unit
would work with the State Sec-.starint of Agriculture in developing the appro-
priate steps to be taken and the n.ecessary organizational (institutional)
arrangements to carry them out.



ANNEX 6
Page 9
Producer Prices
32.       With the exception of the Federal Government's official minimum
price (para 17), which in recent seasons has approximated two-thirds of ac-
tual final price statements of the more reputable gins, the ginners operate
in a free market. A variety of practices seem to op_rate, but a number of
gins operate by offering a base price early in the season, which they calcu-
late to give themselves a margin for error related to current world market
trends in cotton. This offer becomes a minimum subject to upward revision
at a "price fixing date"--usually mid-October--by which time forward sales
or market trends of lint cotton give a clearer indication against which to
"fix" the seed cotton price.
33.       Arising from the historical patterns, many ginners finance producers
through the picking season by a series of cash advances against anticipated
deliveries. Thus very few producer accounts at the gins pass into credit
balances until after the "fixing date" and only at the end of the delivery
season will the ginner completely balance accounts by making a final clearance
payment. This system appears to operate satisfactorily in regard to the
larger growers but as the final payment is usually drawn from the ginners'
headquarters offices, perhaps situated in Natal, the distance is a deterrent
to the small producer who often authorizes a third party to collect for him--
the third party being more often than not the middleman "transporter" which
further strengthens his position and makes it possible for him to in effect
operate all the "at gin" accounts of his suppliers.
C. Cooperatives
34.       Cooperatives in the project area should be placed in proper per-
spective. With less than 5,000 total membership in the six cooperatives cur-
rently functioning, cooperative members probably represent less than 20% of
the total number of farmers in the area. Cotton marketed by cooperatives
amount to about 18% of seed cotton production of the area. In addition,
large differences in the individual performances of each cooperative indicate
that the cooperative movement still has limited impact.
35.       The performance of cooperatives in the project area reflects the
history of the movement in the Northeast. The success has been spotty in
spite of official vocal support (the "cooperative solution" is an approach to
small-farmer problems having widespread vocal support within the Brazilian
Government without significant political or financial commitment). Despite
formal support for cooperatives by Banco do Brasil and Banco do Nordeste
do Brasil, only 7% of the rural credit of the Northeast banking system was
destined to them in 1972.



ANNEX 6
Page 10
36.       Obstacles to cooperative development are numerous.  First, several
aspects of tax and administrative regulations put cooperatives at some dis-
advantage. 1/ Secondly, although cooperatives can sometimes obtain financing
before the first rains and in time for planting, they are reluctant to pass
such financing to their members until the arrival of the first rains which
are normally some assurance that the year will not be drought-ridden. Since
the first rains can start several weeks after the beginning of the planting
season, the member is forced to take non-institutional credit at high rates
of interest until his cooperative financing is available. Third, the
cooperative-associated small farmer is often doubly disadvantaged in getting
institutional credit since he has to face the risk-averting disbursement
procedures of two organizations to get his credit, rather than just one.
Fourth, marketing cooperatives, in contrast to other intermediaries, cannot
pay their members immediately for the production they acquire, because of the
legal definition of the cooperative's acquisition of member produce as a
"cooperative act" and not a purchase and sale in the market. The cooperative
therefore, cannot get credit for payment in advance of resale and as a result,
it can only reimburse the farmer fully when his production is resold (in the
case of cotton, the product is handed over by the farmer from August to
November and he is fully repaid only in April). As a consequence, many small
farmers find it necessary to sell their product to the marketing intermediary
at a lower price for immediate reimbursement. Fifth, management is probably
one of the most serious constraints to the rapid development of the cooperative
movement in the project area. The somewhat unique success of the Umarizal
Cooperative is to a large extent attributable to competent and imaginative
management combined with local knowledge and respect.
37.       The industrial structure dictated by cotton and the decentralization
of ginning in the Northeast made it natural for producers to form marketing
rather than production cooperatives. Cooperatives that have focused on the
marketing process have in general been less prone to failure than production-
oriented ones. All the cooperatives of the project area are involved in
cotton. Arrangements have begun to be made between cooperatives for the
joint processing of seed cotton, even in cases where none of the participating
cooperatives owned a processing unit. In 1974, the Santa Cruz cooperative
arranged the acquisition of its own processing unit and undertook contract
processing for the cooperative at Santo Antonio. In 1974 also, the Umarizal
cooperative undertook contract processing for the Acu cooperative, and the
Florania cooperative obtained private contract processing services for its
members' production. Another significant development at the next higher
level in the cotton marketing system is the recent formation of a central
cooperative in Natal which would be responsible for collecting cotton lint
production from member cooperatives and reselling it in bulk, as well as
for input supply.
1/   See "Rural Development Issues and Options in Northeast Brazil," IBRD
Report No. 665a-BR, June 1975.



ANNEX 6
Page 11
38.       The production of cotton encompasses a wide variety of producers
ranging from large holdings using sharecroppers to medium and small producers
which use few or no sharecroppers. While it is medium and some small owners
who avail themselves of the cooperative marketing program, even some larger
landowners producing through sharecroppers participate in the cooperative
availing themselves of the credit onlent through the cooperative. Coopera-
tive development probably brought no oignificant improvement in the conditions
of cotton sharecroppers. In addition, small farmers participate very little
in the decision process and general management of the cooperative.
39.       Cooperatives can play an important role in the development of the
area, as evidenced by the successful experience of the Umarizal cooperative.
However, in view of the situation discussed above, the approach to cooperative
development in the project area should be a cautious one; under present cir-
cumstances, a rapid expansion of the cooperative coverage cannot be anticipated.
Cooperatives in the area appear to have a potential comparative advantage in
marketing, input supply, extension service delivery and social mobilization.
Although they can further contribute to credit distribution, concentration of
cooperatives' efforts on marketing aspects should be encouraged, not only for
cotton which nevertheless would remain the predominant commodity to be dealt
with, but also for food crops (corn, beans). The supply of inputs is especially
important in view of the current inadequacy of the official input delivery
system,(para 13).
40.       Cooperatives in the project area already receive substantial assist-
ance from various sources. ANCAR/RN has several technicians seconded to most
cooperatives, providing technical assistance and agricultural extension serv-
ices. Training facilities are provided through a convenio between ANCAR and
SUDENE (Annex 7). BNB provides direct management support through seconded
personnel, mainly in the areas of general management and credit procedures.
The State Secretariat of Agriculture provides for management support in
accounting.
41.       BNB is especially committed to the development of "full service"
cooperatives, including the service of delivering credit in the less-than-50-
times-reference value (Annex 3). A potentially promising recent development
concerns BNB's suggestion of promoting the creation of "entrepostos" or store-
houses, as a means of enhancing the development and coverage of cotton coopera-
tives in the project area. These storehouses would be attached to, but operate
at a distance from, their respective parent cooperatives. Their functions
would be the same as the cooperative's (extension, input supply, credit), with
a special emphasis on concentration of produce for bulk transportation to the
cooperative. However, the "entrepostos" idea has not yet been tried in
practice nor its financial feasibility been studied in depth.
42.       All the actions proposed under the project would apply to coopera-
tive members as well as others. In specific instances (agricultural credit,
extension), selected actions would be tested and refined for the specific



ANNEX 6
Page 12
context of the cooperative movement. The Project Unit would include an
Adviser to deal especially with cooperatives and marketing aspects, who would
work closely with agencies already working with cooperatives.

NORTHEAST BRAZIL
RIO GRANDE DO NORTE RURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Training
I. Existing Situation
1.        In Rio Grande do Norte and elsewhere in the Northeast, infrastruc-
ture for training is largely available. However, the scope and nature of
training related to agriculture and rural development is limited. Training
is generally provided on a piecemeal basis and except for training of higher
staff in several official institutions (para 13), little or no interagency
cooperation takes place. Each agency which would participate in the project
administers its own training program suited to its own immediate objectives.
Farmers' training, the last and eventually most relevant level of training
because of its direct impact on target groups, is extremely inadequate.
Several groups of farmers (small landowners, sharecroppers) are largely
unattended, especially in productive fields (e.g. delivery of technical
packages). Training in social- and welfare-related fields has improved in
recent years, but still has limited impact.
Formal Education
2.        In formal education, the Northeast in general has a low level of
investment in human resources. There are about 4,000 primary schools in
RN with approximately 240,000 students in grades 1-4 and 22,000 in grades
5-8 (1973 data). Curricula are the same for urban and rural schools. The
estimated percentage of untrained school teachers in rural areas of the State
is 90, compared to 50 for the whole State and 37 for Brazil. Many school
teachers have not even completed fourth grade. The municipal authorities hire
non-qualified teachers because they cannot pay the higher salaries which
qualified teachers can demand by law. Lack of physical facilities results
in two or three school shifts per day and short daily sessions. The problems
are further compounded by many overaged children (40%) in the first four
grades, for whom no special vocational training is available.
3.        There are a few vocational high schools (e.g. agricultural schools)
in the State. In 1973 there were 220 graduates from the agricultural high
schools; 500 are expected in 1980. Higher education can be pursued at the
Federal University of Rio Grande do Norte or at the agricultural college in
Mossoro.



ANNEX 7
Page 2
Job Training
4.        Agricultural Extension staff of ANCAR-RN presently receive a pre-
service training dealing with credit procedures and administrative duties.
Little or no technical training in agricultural matters or extension method-
ology is given, since an ANCAR agent is mainly occupied with preparing credit
proposals for large farms. Social extensionists of ANCAR attend a pre-service
training course in Bahia or Ceara for extension staff from all Northeast
states. Although their work mainly consists of supervising community health
systems, they lack training in the medical aspects of administrating such a
system. However, they receive pre-service and regular in-service training
on health and nutrition education, extension methodology and planning methods.
5.        INFAOL staff frequently has had previous occupational experience
with ANCAR and only needs to acquire knowledge on the technicalities of seed
production, the main activity of INFAOL. Field staff is generally trained
on the job, while higher staff has been trained abroad.
6.        Cooperatives.  Assistance is provided by ANCAR/RN for technical
matters by seconding extension agents to cooperatives, and by the Secretariat
of Agriculture for administrative and accounting training offered to coopera-
tive management. Technicians of the Secretariat of Agriculture are trained
in a course given by SUDENE which emphasizes economics, accounting, marketing,
management and sociology. Managers and accountants receive a two-week course
in cooperative administration under the auspices of the Secretariat of Agri-
culture. The course is given four to five times a year and organized and run
by PIPMO 1/, a national organization specialized in training. ANCAR/RN trains
its own cooperative technicians. All these activities are carried out on a
limited scale as few cooperatives are presently functioning in the State (6
in the project area, see Annex 6).
7.        Banking personnel receive in-service training within their insti-
tutions (Banco do Brasil and Banco do Nordeste do Brasil); however, this
training is geared to attitudes and procedures for dealing with large and
medium-scale farmers, the traditional borrowers.
8.        Community health workers currently receive a training of three
months in Acu; the sessions are organized by Foundation SESP and partly
financed by the municipality. Municipal authorities also pay for a three-day
training in family planning which is organized by BENFAM (a private family-
planning organization). Refresher courses are periodically given at the
health centers.
1/   "Programa Intensivo de Preparacao de Mao de Obra"



ANNEX 7
Page 3
9.        Auxiliary nurses (Qvisitadoras"), also receive training by Founda-
tion SESP for approximately six months. They are trained in medical aspects
rather than in supervision.
10.       Teachersfrom rural schools receive in-service training, in health
and nutrition education, and in community action under the Integrated Health
Education Project (Annex 2). The State Secretariat of Education and the
local authorities pay for this training. Upgrading of school teachers is
accomplished through various programs. To complete grade 4 adults can
follow "Project Minerva," a national program administered by the Ministry
of Education, under which courses are offered by radio followed by discussions
led by a monitor. Under "Project SACI" 1/, television receivers have been
installed in selected schools; programs are provided for the teachers first
and later repeated for use during school hours.
11.       Farmers and their families have access to training on an informal
basis, mainly provided for by ANCAR together with MOBRAL (the literacy move-
ment). Short courses are given to groups, e.g., on leadership or technical
subjects. 2/ Over 700 farmers were trained during 1974 in courses featuring
planting techniques (cotton, cassava and cocoa), horticulture, livestock and
small animal husbandry, and others. Permanent training is a focus of several
of the groups organized by ANCAR (mother's clubs, youth artisan and agricul-
tural clubs). The Syndicate of Rural Workers (FUNRURAL) is also active in
giving training to its members in leadership and organization, syndication,
cooperativism, etc. Instructors for these short courses are drawn from
qualified staff belonging to agencies working in the area. In places where
cooperatives exist or are in the process of developing, "cooperative weeks"
are held in the community. (Annex 6)
12.       Media.  Besides the educational radio and television programs to.
provide primary education up to fourth grade (Minerva, SACI) the network of
television receivers will be used in a new "Project SACI." Short television
films are shown on subjects of interest to farmers and their families, e.g.,
technical crop husbandry lessons at the appropriate time in the crop cycle.
Each film is followed by a discussion led by the monitor of a "teleclub"
organized around viewers. Each film is shown three times a week. Television
is used almost entirely for training programs.
13.       Most of the larger agencies (e.g., ANCAR, Secretariat of Health,
Banco do Brazil) use PIPMO to set up their training courses. PIPMO operates
1/   National program sponsored by the National Space Research Institute,
recently started in the Northeast.
2/   In 1974 ANCAR-RN opened a new training center (CETRERN) in Eduardo
Gomes, 21 km from Natal. This center has 21 ha of land which will be
converted into demonstration and training plots. Presently, courses
are frequently given in the weekends or evenings using school facilities.



ANNEX 7
Page 4
by training teachers and using the equipment available on the job to imple-
ment a training program tailored to the needs of the trainees. Active
participation of the trainees is sought. PIPMO usually develops, executes
and adjusts a training program for trainers within an organization. No
systematic development of new training techniques, nor any systematic col-
lection of data to evaluate the techniques used, is being done by PIPMO.
14.       Information is an important prerequisite and a necessary follow-up
of training. Agencies have their own newsletters and in-service training to
keep staff informed. Farmers and the people in rural communities receive
information through formal and informal channels. Informal sources include
local priests, the agents of the rural assistance service (SAR) and the
Syndicate of Rural Workers, and traders. Studies indicate that information
on the price of cotton is usually obtained from traders at the weekly market
days which are attended by some 60% of farmers. Formal channels supplement
the information thus obtained. Three radio stations ("Radio Rural") broadcast
farm prices and crop husbandry information at the proper time in the crop
cycle. These radio stations are largely financed by the Catholic Church and
managed by an independent board of directors; two out of the three operate
with a small profit through advertising. The four daily Natal newspapers
contain useful agricultural information, but circulation is limited in the
countryside. The television network is only used for training (not for farm
prices and other agricultural information).
II. Training Actions Under the Project
Introduction
15.       Training is an important element of the project because:  (a) new
target groups of small farmers, hitherto largely unattended, are to be
reached at the cost of considerable additional manpower; (b) existing and
newly recruited staff would need a reorientation toward the socio-economic
position of small farmers and ways to improve their situations; and (c)
largely experimental aspects of the project would require permanent adjust-
ments in staff work and continuous in-service training.
16.       The project would provide training for all categories of people
involved in the project. Recipients of the proposed training actions would
be: (a) the higher level staff of the various organizations responsible
for project implementation, administration and supervision, who would be
largely responsible for the training of the next lower echelon; (b) the field
staff who are in direct contact with the target groups; and (c) the target
groups, rural families who would benefit from the project actions.
17.       A general objective of training would be to familiarize all those
involved with the project nature, scope and objectives. Staff training
would be directed towards creating a body of motivated and dedicated men



ANNEX 7
Page 5
and women working together, each having the necessary technical skills to
fulfill their respective roles. The project would place heavy emphasis on
assuring consistency among the various training programs administered by
different agencies. Coordination of these programs would be developed under
the responsibility of the Project Unit. In addition, the training component
of the project would be subject to periodic review and evaluation, to adjust
rapidly to changing conditions brought about by the project development.
18.       Consultants (probably PIPMO) would be retained to assist the Pro-
ject Unit and participating agencies in developing new training approaches,
focusing on the training of the field staff and also higher staff responsible
for planning and coordination with other agencies. In this way, permanent
training would become a regular feature.
Specific Training Proposals for Each Agency
19.       The Project Unit and Monitoring Group.  Provision would be made under
the project for members to obtain informal training through seminars, observa-
tion tours, etc. organized by themselves or by consultants. Travel funds
would be provided to study specific technical questions (e.g. equipment for
animal traction) or implementation of similar projects in other parts of
Brazil or other countries. Their training should start as soon as possible.
20.       Higher planning and coordinating staff of the participating agencies.
These would participate in seminars organized by the Project Unit and, on an
individual basis, they would accompany members of the Project Unit on study
tours (e.g. the animal-drawn equipment to be tested by INFAOL). Consultants
(probably PIPMO) would train higher echelon personnel on how to train their
field staff.
21.       Field Staff
(a) ANCAR's Technical Advisers, the Agricultural Extensionists
and INFAOL field staff all would receive thirty day pre-
service training focusing on:
(i) the Project, its objectives and their role in it;
(ii) the area, its agronomic risks and potentials;
(iii) the initial technical package, practical training,
and technical solutions which still require more
research and experimentation;
(iv) the socio-economic and political structure and
characteristics of the target group;
(v) the survey and interview training; and



ANNEX 7
Page 6
(vi) their own attitudes towards the target group and
implications thereof.
After the initial training ANCAR/RN agricultural advisers
would conduct a field survey in their own area, to build
up working files on farms under their supervision. The
INFAOL staff would receive a one-month field training,
in existing practices, selection of sites and farmers for
conducting field trials (Annex 4).
(b) Technical Advisers 1/ would receive 20 more days of training
after completion of their survey to distill from the survey
results the most pressing problems in their area and to build
an extension plan based on the farmers' felt needs. Planning
methodology and extension methods would be emphasized and
each adviser would develop his own work plan for the first
few months with his Regional Coordinator's assistance.
(C) Agricultural Extensionists 1/ would receive the same basic
training. In addition they would be trained in the pro-
cessing of traditional credit proposals, problems of larger
farms and possible alternatives for their eventual joint
actions with Technical Advisers, Community Development
Officers, Village Assistants or Farmers' Representatives.
In-service training courses for the agricultural advisers
would be held regularly, at least three times a year, in
sessions lasting a minimum of two weeks. Topics covered
would include new technical methods and information,
management aspects and extension methodology. Perhaps
the most important training of agricultural advisers and
other field staff would be provided through the frequent
(often weekly) meetings between the field staff worker(s)
and the regional coordinator. These meetings would be
"two-way" sessions at which the previous results would be
reviewed and the field worker's subsequent program (for a
week or two) planned.
(d) INFAOL staff would receive further pre-service training in
the experimental system used, the research design and

data gathering, the role of the consultant on the trials
program (probably the University of Ceara), their own
specific role in supervising the farmers. INFAOL staff
would also receive regular in-service sessions lasting from
a few days to two weeks to keep them up to Jate in the newest
techniques.
22.       Branch and Mobile Bank Managers would receive training in modified
banking procedures and relationships with the target group. This would be
followed by a short (perhaps two-day) training together with the agricultural
advisers, in which the extension file, the participating bank's credit
proposals and a plan or timetable for bi-weekly meetings in the community
would be discussed.
23.       The existing programs for training cooperative managers or coopera-
tive advisors are expected to be continued.
24.       Community Health Workers would receive the existing training course
in Acu conducted by Foundation SESP. The family planning course by BENFAM
would be continued.
25.       Auxiliary Nurses would receive a training of nine months, instead
of six as at present, to teach them the supervision skills which are called
for under the Project in addition to the existing focus on prevention and
curative medical actions. Foundation SESP would train all auxiliary nurses
("visitadoras") both for its own health centers and those of the Secretariat
of Health.
26.       Social Extensionists would receive 60 days of pre-service training,
which includes the medical knowledge necessary for their role in the health
program. Emphasis would be on community actions. A three-day training
session in the use of demonstration units for raising animals and vegetable
growing would be given during the first year of the project.
27.       One hundred rural school teachers for the project Phase I area
would receive a one-week training under the existing integrated school com- -
munity education program. They would be trained in health and nutrition
education, and community action by a multi-disciplinary team of health and
extension professionals with use of the demonstration units.
28.       Sixty caretakers for the demonstration units for small animal
raising and vegetable growing would be trained for one week at an already
existing unit or in an agricultural school, by experts from the agricultural
school and the extension service.
29.       Community Development Officers  (Annex 2) would be brought together
in small groups for special training from the extension service.



ANNEX 7
Page 8
30.       Over 500 teleclub monitors would be trained under the project to
lead the discussions for the teleclubs (Project SACI). The monitors would be
trained in use of a T.V. set and in techniques of group discussion.
Training of Target Population
31.       Training of farrers and other project beneficiaries would form the
critical link in the chain of training. Only if all the training efforts
are combined to create an integrated approach at the community level, can
project success be expected. Most of this burden of integration would fall
upon staff of the extension service which would have to integrate the train-
ing in technical components (for an estimated 15,000 farmers) with other
training. The agricultural advisers would be responsible for organizing,
with the collaboration of all community level workers and other agencies,
training in farmer's organization and leadership. The messages to farmers
and their families via the teleclubs would have to be followed up in
regular extension activities and meetings with the farmers. The viewership
of the teleclub is wider than the clientele of the agricultural adviser.
The subjects dealt with at the teleclub would not be restricted to agri-
cultural practices; films on health and nutrition education and topics of
general interest would also be shown. Social extension workers, school-
teachers and community health workers would follow up on these activities.
The activities of the community health workers, schoolteachers and social
extension workers would have to be integrated with the activities of other
agencies to give the full impact of the proposed training of the whole
community in health and nutrition education, sanitation and self-help
activities. Youth clubs, mothers' groups, and parent groups all would
receive training which would reinforce the approach and the messages of
the project actions.
Monitoring and Evaluation
32.       Monitoring would involve collection of data on numbers of people
trained, number of hours of training received and the subject of the training--
a quantitative collection of data. Qualitative information, e.g. the extent
to which training program worked towards project goals, would also be collected.
33.       Evaluation of each training program would be done by each agency
employing formal evaluation tools both at the end of the course and later,
in the field. The Monitoring Group would also try to determine the elements
in training which influence the project success (e.g., previous attitudes
and attitude changes, levels of technical and practical know-how.) Success
would be judged from the results at the community level.
34.       Permanent training is in itself a feedback mechanism, a monitoring
of adjustments judged necessary to carry out the project actions success-
fully. The lIonitoring Group would compare the system of regular in-service
training among the different agencies.



ANNEX 7
Page 9
Organization
35.       W4ithin the Project Unit, the Human Resource Adviser would be
responsible for formulating training policies under the project with the
participating agencies and consultants (Annex 12). He would be responsible
for harmonizing the training efforts of all participating agencies in project-
related matters, and adjusting the training policies, objectives and programs
according to the changes in the execution of the project. Within the Project
Unit the Human Resources Adviser would coordinate his actions with actions
of the advisers responsible for a particular field or agency (e.g., developing
extension training with the Advisor and ANCAR/RN). A committee would be formed
consisting of all training advisers of the participating agencies, chaired by
the Human Resources Adviser.
Cost Estimates
36.       Total costs for training are estimated at Cr$6.5 million (Annex 10
Table 8).

NORTHEAST BRAZIL
RIO GRANDE DO NORTE RURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Monitoring and Evaluation Group
1.        Background.  An important element of the project would be the
detailed planning of subsequent steps based on actual results of earlier
actions. For Phase I in particular, which is intended largely to introduce
and refine technical, credit and extension delivery systems, this would be
especially important. Accordingly, a careful monitoring of project actions,
providing rapid feedback to the Project Unit and participating agencies,
would be crucial to project success. The project monitoring would provide
a continuing focus on the extent to which project actions were contributing
to the project's overall objectives, especially concerning the target group
of small farmers.
2.        A separate Monitoring and Evaluation Group would be created within
the Project Unit to evaluate the impact of the project by collecting and
analyzing information from the project area. The Monitoring Group would not
be directly involved in the implementation; however, a close relationship
with the Project Unit would be maintained to enable the Monitoring Group to
keep abreast of the project actions, to generate new data and feed back all
results rapidly and freely to the Project Unit and participating agencies
thus enabling the project to respond quickly to new knowledge (Scheme 1).
3.        Functions.  During and after familiarizing itself fully with the
project, the Monitoring Group would decide on the information necessary to
monitor and evaluate. The information to be obtained can be divided into:
(a) general information about underlying trends involving the target groups
and the project area due to the project or other causes; and (b) specific
information related to the project actions.
4.        As information collection has to be kept within limits, both
with regards to time and costs, priorities have to be established and a
work program drawn up. Possible priorities are:
(a) Monitoring of farm results, both for project and non-project
farms, implying an efficient farm data collection system,
which records yields, cropping and production patterns,
economic data for farm budget analysis, labor use and
requirements and participation of the farmer in the
project. Farms would be chosen on a sample basis from
different types and categories;
(b) Monitoring of technical package generation, development
and distribution, implying monitoring of adoption rates
and/or adaptations; and



ANNEX 8
Page 2
(c)  Monitoring of the different delivery systems proposed under
the project with special emphasis on credit, technology,
extension and health, and the extent to which they reach the
target groups of small farmers (mainly 0-50 ha. and 50-200 ha
farms) and others including the landless; and measuring the
cost-effectiveness of the different systems.
5.        To carry out the information collection task the Monitoring Group
would establish an information system, with the assistance of a consultant
to determine the appropriate methods of data-gathering, e.g., sample sizes.
Information would be collected through:
(a) small on-the-spot surveys;
(b) examination of the records of each participating agency;
(c) record-keeping on project and control farms on yields,
prices, household budgets, market information, etc.,
(d) market structure survey; and
(e) group meetings.
This process should be made as simple as possible in order to minimize time
and costs. Flexibility would also be important to monitor the project
actions as they are modified. Much of the work of the Monitoring Group
would be a continuing, simple and practical inquiry (or management information
system) into the impact of the project actions. Often this can be done by
Monitoring Group staff interviewing and observation on a small, simple and
rapid feedback basis.
6.        The Monitoring Group would analyse its data and regularly prepare
a short report with the highlights for immediate and widespread dissemination
and discussion. The Monitoring Group would translate data into a readable
form and compare them with stated goals. The Project Unit and the participat-
ing agencies would thus be able to make quick decisions in order to adjust
strategies. The management information function of the Monitoring Group
requires a flexible interpretation, covering progress on the above-mentioned
reports and information obtained from outside sources. A set of review
questions would be a useful tool to guide discussions between the Monitoring
Group and the Project Unit and participating agencies.
Phase I - II Evaluation
7.        The Monitoring Group would also support the evaluation and review
of Phase I to permit a decision on whether or not to proceed with Phase II,
and the timing and detailed design of Phase II. Important criteria of that
review would be:



ANNEX 8
Page 3
(a) extent to which farmers have accepted and benefited
from the project's technical recommendations, as an
indication of the project's overall success in refining
and delivering technical packages profitable to target
farmers;
(b) extent to which effective credit procedures and
delivery systems have been developed and accepted by the
various size-groups of target farmers;
(c) effectiveness of an agricultural advisory body focussing
on the problems of the small farmer, and sufficiently
trained in the new delivery systems, extent to which more
cost-effective methods (e.g., farmers' representatives,
small group formation) have been developed;
(d) effectiveness of community development efforts: promotion
of community health societies and other community
activities such as produce-raising units, with
adequately trained supervisory personnel in both
the medical supervision channels as in the social
extension service;
(e) cost-effectiveness of delivery systems, particularly
extension, expected to be the largest item of recurrent
cost under the project; and
(f) adequacy of administrative procedures and institutional
coordination in meeting the above objectives.
The evaluation would necessarily examine whether the conditions during
Phase I were sufficiently "normal" to warrant drawing particular inferences.
The above mentioned criteria are linked to the response of the target
groups, the single most important criterion in the overall evaluation of
the project. Phase II would not be undertaken if the evidence from Phase I
does not provide adequate assurance that the target group would receive the
proposed benefits.
8.        Project actions under Phase II could be initiated at different
times for different actions. For example, the health and community develop-
ment and/or the farmer's education components could precede the credit-
extension components. Similarly farm level research should have expanded
into Phase II areas before the credit-extension components.
Staffing
9.        The M4onitoring Group would be staffed by at least two full-time
professionals, although their actual recruitment could be phased to match
the Monitoring Group's workload and staff availability. They should have



ANNEX 8
Page 4
field research experience on economic and social aspects of rural areas.
They would be backed by support staff, including at least four interviewers/
tabulators and administrative staff. The Monitoring Group would be assisted
by a consultant (firm, person, university or other) experienced in monitoring
or similar activities involving research design, survey work, field work,
data processing, analysis and interpretation of data. Data processing faci-
lities would be rented. The consultant service would require three to six
months per year. Part of the actual evaluation of Phase I would also probably
be done by consulting institutions.
Costs
10.       Total costs for monitoring and evaluation are estimated at
Cr$ 5,250,000 (US$700,000) for the five-year period (Annex 10, Table 7).

NORTHEAST BRAZIL
RIO GRANDE DO NORTE RURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Project Preparation Studies
Background
1.        The project area roughly corresponds to the area under perennial
cotton in RN State (Annex 1). The project would finance studies to determine
the prospects for further rural development efforts in other parts of the
State. Two areas would be considered: (a) the Serra de Santana, a small
(5,000 ha, 10,000 inhabitants) homogeneous area located within the project
area, well differentiated from the latter (higher altitude of about 600 m,
better rainfall averaging 500 to 800 mm annually, and better soils); and
(b) the Agreste-Coastal area, bordering the project area to the east and
extending to the coast, a large area of approximately 8,100 sq km with a
population of 725,000 (1970).
2.        The selection of these areas is based on:  (a) the location of
Serra de Santana within the project area, to which it could be rapidly
integrated after brief studies are conducted; (b) the importance of the
Agreste-Coastal area in terms of population, area and potential agronomic
use as well as the seemingly serious bottlenecks to development in this
area.
A. Serra de Santana Area
3.        The Serra de Santana is characterized by relatively good climate
and soil conditions. Combined with a favorable topography of elevated flat
tablelands, these characteristics allow for a diversified agriculture. Land
use data indicate the predominance of annual crops (46% of total farm land)
over perennial cultivation (9%). Food crops are important, especially corn,
bananas and manioc, while groundnuts appear to have some potential. Sisal
(on middle and large units) and fruit (on small size family farms) are also
grown. Beef and dairy cattle, feeding on native grass and crops residues,
are raised extensively and semi-extensively, although water availability
is a limiting factor. The land tenure situation appears similar to that of
the project area, with a seemingly relatively larger number of sharecroppers.
4.        The main issues concerning the development of this area may be
similar to those of the project area, despite the absence of perennial
cotton. No highly attractive technical packages are currently available
for the crops proiuced in the area. In view of the relative smallness of
the area, a major study is not warranted. Rather, the basic approach used



ANNEX 9
Page 2
for the project area could be fruitfully used in this area, i.e., a small
experimental phase to test and refine technical improvements adapted to the
specific conditions of the area. All other interventions under the project
would apply to this area (credit and agricultural extension delivery systems,
trials and experimentation, health services, etc.). The major prerequisite,
justifying the non-immediate inclusion of this area in the project area,
would be a study on the agronomical potential of the area for subsequent
selection of the relevant agricultural activities (food crops, fruit, live-
stock) to be experimented with.
B. Agreste Coastal Area
Brief Description
5.        The Agreste-Coastal area includes 52 municipios representing an
area of 8,100 sq km (15% of the State total area) with a total population
of 725,000 (47% of the State total) and a rural population of 330,000 (21%
of the State total and 41% of the State rural population). The area can
be divided in two major sub-zones: (a) the coastal area, a narrow strip
(20 to 30 km wide) along the eastern littoral, with good agroclimatic
characteristics (average annual rainfall of more than 1,000 mm), including
the main population center of Natal (300,000); and (b) the Agreste, a transi-
tion zone between the coastal area and the semi-arid Sertao (project area)
with average annual rainfall between 700 mm and 1,000 mm. The whole area
is marked by large diversity in soils (reflecting the division between the
crystalline formations in the South and sedimentary basin in the North) and
climate conditions. The agronomic potential is significantly larger than
in the project area (main crops are sugarcane, bananas, cassava, beans,
corn and herbaceous cotton, and livestock is generally important). About
37% of total farmland is in annual crops, the remaining 63% being largely
in permanent pastures (and permanent crops to a lesser extent). Land tenure
is varied, with 46% of farms under direct ownership (representing 90% of
farmland), 21% in renting and 26% under squatting. The area is also seem-
ingly characterized by serious underemployment, especially in areas close
to Natal.
Development Issues and Problems
6.        For the Agreste, the agronomic potential appears to be large and
varied, the nearby Natal consumption market is promising, and risks attached
to agricultural production are substantially lower than in the project area
owing mainly to a better rainfall pattern. However, the land distribution
pattern, present tenure arrangements and underemployment problems are
constraints whi-h sould be accounted for in any development effort.  From a
technical viewpoint, water availability still remains a problem, although
there appear to be yet untapped resources in subterranean waters in the



ANNEX 9
Page 3
North which could possibly be used for irrigation. In addition, the development
of this area should be conceived in harmony with that of the neighboring semi-
arid Sertao (project area) so as to avoid excessive dichotomy between the two
areas.
7.        In summary, the Agreste-Coastal area, whose exact liimits still
remain to be defined, could lend itself to a rural development effort directed
at small farmers, mainly owner-operators and tenants, with a special focus on
employment generation in agriculture. This could be accomplished through
development of food crops, vegetables, fruits and/or dairy production.
Emphasis should probably be placed on fully utilizing the resources of the
large Natal consumption market. Farmer organizations, especially for market-
ing, could be contemplated.
8.        The whole study program related to this area should necessarily
be closely related to the general study of coastal areas (Zona de Mata and
Agreste) in different states of the Northeast, initiated under the
POLONORDESTE program.
Program of Studies
9.        The existing information on the area is substantial.  However, it
is incomplete and partly inadequate for the purposes of elaborating a
structured rural development effort. The project would thus finance studies
which would have the following nature and scope:
(a) Inventory of all existing information, with subsequent
detailed analysis. The collection of_existing data can
be conducted at three levels: (i) at the State level,
CEPA/RN (the agricultural planning commission) already
has its own library, relatively well supplied, but there
are several other State institutions whose resources should
be fully exploited (ANCAR/RN, Banks, State secretariats, etc);
(ii), at the level of other states, several studies have
been-made, or are being made, in similar ecological areas,
especially in the Northeast; and (iii) at the regional and
federal levels, information is also available and should be
collected and analyzed (more specifically, the POLONORDESTE
program has already initiated studies of selected zones of
the coastal area of the Northeast for possible rural development
interventions; close contacts with the team of consultants in
charge of these studies should be developed).
(b) Characterization of the area from agronomic and socio-
economic viewpoints with possible differentation in homo-
geneous sub-areas. This phase should be accompanied by a
clear diagnosis of the present situation with a precise
identification of target groups and of a series of projects
and sub-projects of potential interest.



ANNEX 9
Page 4
(c) Final selection of projects according to specific criteria
including: target groups, technical packages and their
economic impact, consistency with State and/or regional
planning, economic and financial viability.
(d) Preparation and appraisal of the projects selected.
10.       The complexity of the area warrants special care and emphasis to be
placed on the problems of interrelationships among the various sub-areas and
between the area and neighboring regions of the State (especially the pro-
ject area). These interrelationships concern most of the.important variables
at play in the area, e.g., man-power and labor (migration), technical aspects
(crop vs livestock, annual cotton vs perennial cotton, etc.), institutions
especially as they relate to services delivery systems such as credit and
agricultural extension), and others. In the context of Northeast Brazil,
particular attention should be given to the problems of unemployment/under-
employment.
Organization of the Program
11.       The inventory phase would be placed under the direct responsibility
of CEPA/RN. After collecting and analyzing all the existing information
(possibly with temporary assistance by consultants), CEPA/RN would prepare detailed
terms of reference for the specific studies to be undertaken under the second
phase (characterization/diagnosis/project identification). This phase could
be undertaken by CEPA/RN or by consultants (or by a combination of both). The
project selection phase would be the responsibility of CEPA/RN, but con-
sultants could be called upon for assisting in the methodological aspects.
The phase corresponding to the feasibility studies of the projects identified
and selected would be placed under the responsibility of consultants, with
supervision by CEPA/RN.
12.       The overall program of studies would not exceed three years.  The
time sequence of the four phases described above is somewhat theoretical.
A project (or sub-project) defined rapidly and with certainty in the second
phase could well be implemented before the end of other studies. The only
relative confidence in terms of timing, refers to the completion of the data
collection/analysis phase which would take about six months.
13.       Total costs of these studies (including the study referring to
the Serra de Santana Area) would amount to approximately Cr$2,000,000
(US$278,000), over three years. This is roughly equivalent to a total of
4.5 man-years of consulting. The exact distribution of tasks between CEPA/RN
and consultants and plan of action for study execution would be defined
precisely together with consultants' terms of reference (and subsequent
selection) before the end of the Aa&a collection phase, and presented to
the Bank for review and approval.

ANNEX 12

NORTHEAST BRAZIL
RIO GRANDE DO NORTE RURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Organization and Management
1.        The project would involve the detailed planning and implementation
of a number of actions to refine technical packages and delivery systems to
further the project's objectives. The initial phase would require several
developmental approaches which, based on careful monitoring of results,
would be modified as necessary. The project cannot therefore be defined
in advance with great precision and requires a flexible project organization.
2.        The project's detailed actions would be implemented by existing
agencies rather than by the setting up of a new institution. A Project
Unit would be established within CEPA/RN to conceive and stimulate actions
under the project, coordinate the work of the various participating agencies
(PA's), and control project finances. A Monitoring Group would monitor the
project and prepare for and participate in its evaluation. The Project Unit
would report periodically through a state-level agricultural policy coordinat-
ing body (Conselho Diretor) which includes representatives of the various
agencies involved in agriculture in the State to the special coordinating
group of POLONORDESTE, the Federal fund from which most of the Borrower's
contribution to the project would flow.
3.        The Project Unit would be expected to have a small staff which in
view of the complex nature of the project must be experienced and imagin-
ative. Much of the project's success would depend on the quality of the
staff of the Project Unit. The project should be able to offer attractive
salaries in order to recruit good people. Additionally, the project would
provide funds for further training (travel, courses) of the staff of the
Project Unit. Suggested terms of reference for the initial staff of the
Project Unit are in paras 22-29. These terms of reference would need to be
interpreted flexibly, depending on the project's evolution and on the parti-
cular experiences of the people the project recruits. In addition to func-
tional specialists, the Project Unit may require one or more field coordinators.
A general description of the role of the Project Unit follows.
Planning and Coordination
4.        The Project Unit would be the only body whose exclusive concern
would be the project. The various participating agencies (PA's) would have
an important role in project implementation, but for each the project would
be only one program if many. The Project Unit would thus be the principal
body for conceiving the on-going role of the project, stimulating the PA's
to carry out the detailed planning and implementation of each, revising plans
in light of observed results, and coordinating the work of the various PA's.



ANNEX 12
Page 2
Operating Procedures
5.        The basic building block of the project would be the annual plan.
This would be prepared by each PA jointly with the Project Unit; the summation
of the annual plans of the various components would form the annual plan of
the project. The annual plan would be reviewed by the Conselho Diretor and
POLONORDESTE and would be subject to Bank approval. The Regional Coordinator
of POLONORDESTE would review implementation of the project plan.
Generation of Technical Packages
6.        The project would finance field trials and on-farm demonstration
plots for cotton and related crops and work closely with basic research. A
variety of potential technical packages exist which are in varying degrees
of development (Annex 4). The distinct focus or orientation that the Project
Unit could provide is the profitability to users (hence "saleability") of
the eventual results, rather than technical performance per se. It would
focus on crops and activities of importance to farmers of the Northeast,
particularly farmers not currently reached by the formal extension and credit
channels. The work is thus analogous to the "product manager" of a commercial
enterprise.
7.        The Project Unit, in creating technical packages, must also develop
close links with similar work in neighboring states having similar agricultural
conditions, bringing to bear in RN the appropriate results of other states.
It should develop good files on, and attempt a catalytic role in bringing
about coordination of the various technical investigations proceeding in
various parts of the Northeast.
8.        A Technical Committee would be established to coordinate planning,
implementation and review of activities (at the research station and farm
levels) to develop technical recommendations under the project. This
Committee would make recommendations to the Project Unit through its chairman,
the Technical Adviser of the Project Unit. Besides the Adviser, the Committee
would be made up of various experts on Northeast Brazil, including representa-
tives of the Federal University of Ceara, the IPA of Pernambuco, EMBRAPA,
the extension service (ANCAR/RN), INFAOL, BB and BNB. The Committee would
meet at least twice a year.
Credit and Extension Delivery Systems
9.        The development and expansion of credit and extension delivery
systems would be a major project activity. As noted in Annexes 2 and 3,
the initial actions in 11 pilot municipios involving a total of 33 agricultural
advisers would require especially close management attention and liaison with
the project during the initial phase. Accordingly, ANCAR/RN would establish
a special unit to oversee this action during the first phase (about three



ANNEX 12
Page 3
years). Similarly, the Project Unit would have an important role in working
with the participating banks in evolving cost-effective procedures for
making sub-loans under the project.
10.       Credit procedures would be developed through testing, monitoring
and evaluating two or three different credit delivery systems in selected
municipios, under the guidance of a Credit Committee. The Credit Committee
would meet at least quarterly and would be made up of representatives from
the ProJect Unit (the Credit Adviser serving as chairman), the participating
banks, ANCAR/RN and possibly SUDENE and the Central Bank (the latter two
probably participating less frequently).
Financial Control
11.       The Project Unit would be responsible for coordinating financial
arrangements under the project. Accordingly, it would coordinate and approve
the annual project plans and approve the PA requests for disbursements under
the project. It would be responsible for reporting on the project's progress
to Government and the Bank.
Fall-Back Implementation Capability
12.       Both the Conselho Diretor and the Coordinating Group of POLONORDESTE,
to whom the Project Unit would report, are composed of representatives of
agencies (e.g., Ministry of Agriculture) senior to the PA's which can be
expected to exert a positive influence. Nevertheless, performance of all PA's
may not be uniformly high. Accordingly, the Project Unit, representing the
interests of the project as a whole, must have some power to vary, at the time
of the annual project plan, the precise role of each PA. The extent of such
modifications is clearly limited, particularly in the short run.
13.       The Project Unit must also have a fall-back capability to actually
implement itself certain parts of the project (in addition to refusing to
approve disbursements of a particular PA) if a particular PA does not perform
satisfactorily. This provision would be expected to be used infrequently.
Mini-Project Preparation
14.       The Project Unit in its work would encounter situations where
small investments (e.g., small dams, storage facilities) would be necessary,
economically justified and in consonance with project objectives but for
which finrances would not have been provided under the project. A variety
of programs providing engineering and financing for such mini-projects
currently exist in the northeast. One of the roles of the Project Unit
would be to become intimately familiar with such programs so that it could
perform a "brokerage" role in bringing together specific mini-projects and
programs.



ANNEX 12
Page 4
Cooperatives, Input Supply and Marketing
15.       The Cooperatives and Marketing Adviser (para. 26) would work
closely with other agencies in these fields to relate existing programs
to project objectives and work on specific problems (e.g., cotton grading
improvement).
Planning and Administering Studies
16.       The project would finance project preparation activities in areas
of RN outside the immediate project area. The Project Unit would be respon-
sible for finalizing terms of reference, recruiting, selecting and contracting
consultants, and supervising implementation of such activities.
Training
17.       The Project Unit's training activities are outlined in Annex 7.
Health
18.       The Project Unit's role in health is outlined in Annex 5.
Monitoring and Evaluation
19.       A separate Monitoring Group (MG) would be created within the Project
Unit to collect and analyze information on the project area and on the impact
of the project (Annex 8). MG would be staffed by at least two full-time
professionals (plus necessary support staff) experienced in research in
the social or economic fields, preferably of rural areas. MG would be
assisted by periodic consulting assistance (para 29). MG would not be
involved in actual project implementation; however, it would maintain close
liaision with the Project Unit in order to be familiar with the project's
actions and be able to report quickly and freely on results.
Suggested Staffing of Project Unit
20.       The task of the project unit is to further the objectives of the
project according to its prescribed method of operation, in conjunction with
other existing agencies. The magnitude and direction of particular year-
to-year actions under the project would be tempered by results; accordingly
a flexible project organization and staffing is required. The following
suggested terms of reference for Project Unit staff and staff numbers would
need to be interpreted flexibly in practice depending on the particular
experience of the staff the project recruits. It is possible that certain
jobs, or parts of the jobs, could be combined to result in a full-time staff
of fewer than seven persons.  'This c-re staff may need to be supplemented
from time to time by others.



ANNEX 12
Page 5
21. Project Leader/Coordinator
Duties: Overall direction and coordination of work of project
unit, liaison with other agencies, and reporting.
Experience: Leader/coordinator is the key job and the incumbent
should have a proven record of successfully leading a similar unit engaged
in projects, studies or similar activities. Experience in rural activities
in Northeast is essential.
22. Technical Adviser (the "Product Manager")
Duties:
(a) Coordinating the development, within the project and
elsewhere, of technical packages attractive to the target
group of farmers;
(b) Defining the project's priorities in developing, refining
and extending technical packages at the levels of the research
station, farm trials and widespread distribution to farmers
by use of farm budgets and similar tools in analyzing
attractiveness to farmers;
(c) Accumulating an extensive data bank on research and farm-
level trial results of technical work throughout the Northeast;
(d) Maintaining continuing effective liaison with relevant
research and extension workers throughout similar agro-
climatic areas throughout the Northeast;
(e) Liaising closely with other participating agencies
involved in research, trials, extension and credit;
(f) Working closely with the Extension Adviser in supervising,
directly or through regional coordinators, the initial
technical interventions of the project;
(g) Developing, with other members of the Project Unit and
in cooperation with other relevant PA's, the details of
project actions in particular micro-regions; and,
(h) Working with consulting assistance in achieving these tasks.
Experience. This is a key job and the incumbent should have a university
degree and/or adequate relevant experience in research and testing of technical
ideas for distribution to farmers. Experience in extension and agricultural
credit would be essential. He should be familiar with conditions in the rural



ANNEX 12
Page 6
Northeast. He should be experienced in the use of tools such as farm budgets
for analyzing the attractiveness to fanmers of various technical packages.
23. Extension Adviser
Duties:
(a) Working with other team members in planning details of
project actions in particular areas;
(b) Coordinating recruitment and training activities of
extension personnel under the project;
(c) Developing the appropriate overall mix of direct personal
representation (i.e., through extension workers) and of
other means of disseminating technical knowledge to
farmers (e.g., radio, mail, printed matter, etc.);
(d) Planning the appropriate messages, aids and methods of
distribution of particular "campaigns" in disseminating
technical ideas;
(e) Working closely with ANCAR/RN in coordinating the supervision
of actual extension actions under the project (including
supervising project field extension staff in the pilot
municipios during the first phase); and initially selecting
and training agricultural advisers and regional coordinators
under the project;
(f) Liaising closely with project Monitoring Group, consultants,
rural sociologist, participating agencies, etc., in devising,
implementing and revising the necessary training activities
under the project; and
(g) Continuously reviewing, in close cooperation with others
under the project, the costs of delivering credit and extension
to the target group. In this respect he should initiate

advice on overall project implementation. The consultant
desirably should be familiar with Northeast Brazil but this
is less critical than breadth of experience in rural develop-
ment projects, especially the development of technical packages
attractive to small farmers and their systems of delivery.
He would visit the project several times each year for a total
assignment of perhaps two to three man-months per year. He would

reviewing previous results and in detailed planning.

NORTHEAST BRAZIL
RIO GRANDE DO NORTE: RURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Economic Analysis
Introduction
1.        The basis for the methodology used in the economic analysis of
the project and in the evaluation of its financial implications rests on
the general objectives to be reached and the means used to attain these
objectives. These are largely determined by the total investment under
the project, especially in terms of manpower, and its immediate relation-
ship with the beneficiaries or target group. Thus the major inputs having
direct impact on the productive level of the target populations are: (a)
agricultural credit (Annex 3); (b) the agricultural extension service
(Annex 2); and (c) field testing and experimentation effort to be under-
taken within the project (Annex 4).  An indirect impact also expected to
result from the health and training components and the various efforts of
the project administration and the monitoring and evaluation group (Annex 8).
2.        The first objective of the analysis was to determine the number
of agricultural advisers from the extension service which would be required
to reach a large number of farmers, especially small ones, while at the same
time considering the absorptive capacity of the existing structure and the
potential for development. The new extension requirements (see Annex 2)
(both quantitatively and qualitatively), called for the differentiation
between two types of agricultural advisors: (a) the agricultural exten-
sionist proper, a well trained individual with the equivalent of "engen-
heiro agronomo" background, a comparative advantage to deal with rela-
tively large farms (at least more than 50 ha); and (b) the technical
adviser, probably a younger individual with a degree from an agricul-
tural technical school, who would be specially trained to deal with small
farmers' problems. The objective of the project would be to increase the
total number of agricultural advisers in the whole project area to 136
over 5 years, from the present level of 38 (from 12 to 36 for agricul-
tural extensionists and from 26 to 100 for technical advisers).
3.        On the other side, the principal target group was identified as
farms of less than 200 ha, further divided into classes: (a) farms between
1 and 50 ha which would constitute mainly the "market" for the technical
advisers and (b) farms between 50 and 200 ha, constituting the market for
the agricultural extensionists. Because agricultural extensionists would
deal with all farmers with more than 50 ha, the second target class iden-
tified above (in the 50 to 200 ha group) would only represent about 70%
of their total "market".



ANNEX 13
Page 2
4.        The overall "market" for each agricultural adviser would be about
120 farm units, but the degree of job efficiency and farmers' acceptance
and response would be uneven. During the first year of the project, coverage
by each adviser would not be expected to be more than 50% of his future
coverage.
5.        Under these assumptions, the total coverage of the target group
by the agricultural advisers of the extension service would be approximately
15,000 farms after 5 years, 12,000 of which would be less than 50 ha and
3,000 would be between 50 and 200 ha. The phasing would be as follows:
