Statistical Data - Sources of Information
6.        The major source of information providing comprehensive statistical
data on the various target groups has been the results of the survey undertaken
by SUDENE-IBRD/DRC in 1973. This survey covered the whole of Northeast Brazil
through a sample of some 8,000 farms. Four municipios only from the project
area were covered by the survey, giving rather small sub-sample populations
not necessarily representative or usable because of excessively large variation
coefficients. However, extrapolation could be made from the semi-arid zone



ANNEX 13
Page 3
C sub-sample population which covered a vast area largely representative of
the project area. In addition, when warranted, extrdpolation was also made
from the results of the whole state of Rio Grande do Norte.
7.        Complementary sources of Tfiormation, especially with respect to
the natural environment (soils, climate) and the technical aspects, included
several studies and reports originating from the University of Ceara, SUDENE,
and the State as well as consultants' reports (both from the Bank and else-
where), particularly with respect to the analysis of the drought factor and
technical problems. Good data on the rainfall patterns was available (45
years from several state recording stations, see Annex 1, para 3). Data
relating to the agricultural credit situation was provided mainly by the two
major banks operating in the area, Banco do Brasil (BB) and Banco do Nordeste
do Brasil (BNB).
8.        The various censuses of 1970 (Agricultural, Population) and the
Cadastral Survey of 1972 provided useful data in spite of some inconsis-
tencies and gaps. Finally, expert advice was available from several Bank
missions to the Northeast of Brazil.
Target Group Classification
9.        Since the major inputs under the project would be agricultural
credit and technical advice through the agricultural extension service,
farms were classified according to their present and expected response to
the delivery of credit and agricultural extension. There are thus eight
groups of farms:
1.   Farms from 0 to 50 ha - (12,000):
Group A (9% of total or 1,080 farms). These farms presently
have access to institutional credit. They are assumed
to continue using credit and to benefit intensively from
technical advice provided by the extension service.
Group B-I (25% of remaining, or 2,730 farms). These farms
do not presently receive credit but they would under
the project and would also be reached intensively by the
extension service. They would not be expected to expand
the area under cotton cultivation (para 19).
Group B-II (2,730 farms). The only difference with Group B-I
is that these farms are expected to expand the area
under cultivation, eventually reaching the level of
Group A (para 19).



ANNEX 13
Page 4
Group C (2,730 farms). These farms do not presently receive
institutional credit and would not do so under the
project. They will, however, receive strong, direct
benefits from the technical assistance of the extension
service (para 16).
Group D (2,730 farms). Like the preceding group, these
farms would not receive credit under the project.
In addition, although they are part of the "market"
of the technical advisers, they are expected to
derive less intensive benefits from the extension
service (para 16).
2.   Farms from 50 to 200 ha (3,000):
Group M (17% of total, or 500 farms). These farms are
equivalent to Group A of the previous class, i.e.
they currently receive credit, would continue to do
so under the project and would benefit from the
extension service technical assistance.
Group N (50% of remaining, or 1,250 farms). These farms
currently do not receive credit. They would do so
under the project, would benefit intensively from
extension, and would expand the area under cotton
cultivation. Although some individual farms may
expand area to the level of Group M, the group on
the average would remain slightly under Group M
level (para 21).
Group 0 (1,250 farms). These farms currently do not
receive credit and are not expected to under the
project. They would receive intensive benefits
from technical assistance.
10.       The phasing of reaching these groups over five years is assumed
to be uneven, i.e., Groups A and M are assumed to respond more rapidly to
the availability of credit and extension. Groups B and N, undertaking some
structural changes, would probably be somewhat slower. Groups C, D and 0
are expected to respond most slowly. Phase II of the project, i.e., expan-
sion to the whole project area, is assumed to begin (slowly) in year 3.
Phasing of the various groups would be as follows:

Technical Assumptions
12.       The following hypotheses refer exclusively to aggregate situations,
i.e., yield figures do not correspond to individual farms, but rather to an
average for a relatively large group of farms. This is so because of the
large number of possible individual situations with e.tremely wide variations
in the existing circumstances at the farm level.
13.       Yields of cotton are expressed in yearly averages of the five-year
production cycle, in kg per ha. These yields represent average expected
values taking in consideration the drought probability coefficient (Annex 1,
para 3). The relationships between cotton yields and the technology used
are summarized as follows:
150 kg.   Base-line situation where little or no animal traction
is being used, rudimentary weeding is done manually,
traditional seeds are being used and there is little
or no spraying of insecticides.
175 kg.   Same as above except that weeding is more frequent,
early sowing used.
200 kg.   Corresponds to adequate spraying, without much
use of animal traction for cultivation, or use of
animal traction only with rudimentary weeding and
spraying.
250 kg.   Combination of spraying and animal traction use for
cultivation practices.
300 kg.   Optimal situation with existing technology.
350 kg and upwards. New technology available from research,
especially new improved seeds. 1/
14.       Additional important factors known to have a direct impact on
yields include: early sowing, land preparation including stump removal by
animal traction, various cultivation practices such as "bosque denso" (four
rows of cotton and for rows of food crops, see Annex 4), etc. Fertilizer
use is explicitly excluded in the above set of technologiws. As explained
in Annex 4, fertilizer use (chemical or organic) may be recommended under
specific circumstances where complete data on soils and other variables
are available.
1/   The 350 kg figure was used in the calculations, throughout the
life of the project.



ANNEX 13
Page 7
Phasing of Technical Improvements
15.       The starting situation (i.e., "without project") is assumed to
be: (a) 200 kg/ha for groups A and M (already receiving credit); and (b)
150 kg/ha for all other groups. (Note: On the basis of production costs
and income figures, these hypotheses yield results for the "without" situation
which are consistent with the data provided by the SUDENE-IBRD/DRC Survey,
for farms receiving and not receiving credit.)
16.       The assumptions on the phasing of cotton yields are summarized as
follows:
(a) Farmers receiving credit (A, B's, M and N) will achieve higher
yields more quickly than other farmers, taking into account
the greater access to modern inputs (seeds, insecticide,
equipment) and their possibilities of rapidly contracting hired
labor at harvest time;
(b) Since there is phasing of the number of farmers participating
in the project over a five-year period, farmers entering in
later years will reach their highest yield level relatively
more rapidly, mainly because of better coverage and improved
efficiency of the agricultural extension service. Eventually,
all farmers of the same group will reach the same higher yield
level at year 9 of the project. Thus the phasing is different
for each sub-group entering the project in a different year.
This accounts for the benefit of experience and results acquired
during the previous years.
(c) The residual groups of farmers not receiving credit (C, D and
0) but still under the influence of the technical assistance
from extension services would be expected to reach lower
yield levels than farmers from the other groups.
17.       More specifically, the phasing of technical improvements for
the various groups can be presented in summary form as in Table 1. This
table shows in what year of the project a sub-group entering the project
in year 1 and/or 5, would reach certain yield levels. All other inter-
mediate values for the other years and other sub-groups have been ex-
trapolated from these basic figures. 1/
1/   It should be clear that these yields do not necessarily represent target
levels for a specific group in a particular year. They merely represent-
the best available estimates of expected improvements at the time of
appraisal. In addition, being "expected" values including the drought
probability coefficient, they sihould be taken as averages of at least
a 6-7 year rainfall cycle. Daring project implementation, considerable
variation in yield recording can be expected (upward as well as downward)
for any given year, depending upon the rainfall registered in that year.



ANNEX 13
Page 8
Investment
18.       Group A, assumed to have already had access to credit is assumed
to be relatively more efficient than other groups in the 0 to 50 ha farm
class, partly because of better on-farm investment situation.  More specifically,
it is assumed to be equipped for animal traction. It therefore does not
require specific additional investment. (It should be recalled that only
the aggregate situation of farms is considered, i.e., an undefined percentage
of farms in Group A may well find it necessary to invest for on-farm im-
provements under the project.)
19.       Groups B-I and B-II are treated similarly.  The main difference is
that B-II farms will expand area under cultivation from 4.5 ha to 8 ha
(that is, to the level of Group A). This expansion is done in 2 years, with
an intermediate step to 6 ha (e.g. investment of 1.5 ha in year 1 and 2.0 ha
in year 2 of participating in the project). Investment requirements for
these two groups are: (a) 50% of the farms will require one work animal
with the proper equipment for cultivation (at least 1 cultivator and 1
sprayer); (b) 50% of the farms will require investment in fences for their
area already under cultivation; (c) all farms of group B-II (expanding
area) will require fencing for all new area. The assumption of fencing
investment includes the possibility for some farms to invest in small
storage capacity in lieu of, or in addition to fencing.
20.       Group M (50 to 200 ha) already receiving credit, is generally
treated in the same way as Group A. However, provision is made for 75% of
the farms to invest in storage facilities, on the basis of .8 m2 of storage
per ha under cotton.
21.       Group N is generally treated in the same way as Group B-II.  Farms.
in this group will expand area under cultivation from 16 to 20 ha, in the
first year of participating in the project. Investment requirements for
this group are: (a) 50% of the farms will require equipment for animal traction,
but no draft animals since most farms already have work animals (para 11),
even though they may not be used for cultivation purposes; (b) 25% of the
farms will require investment in fences for their area already under culti-
vation; (c) all farms will require fencing for all new area; and (d) 25% of
the farms will require investment in storage facilities.
22.       The life of the investments is:  (a) 4 years for equipment; (b)
6 years for animals (bought at 3 years); (c) 10 years for fences (replaced_
by 50% every 5 years); and (d) 20 years for storage facilities. Groups C,
D and 0 would not receive institutional credit under the project. They
would however, still have access to the non-institutional sources of credit
(landlords, intermediaries, etc.).
Operating Costs (Cuqteio)
23.       Operating costs are calculated on a per ha basis, at various
:Levels of cotton yields. The yield which is considered is the annual average
of cotton yields from year 2 to 5 of the cotton cycle. First year yield is



ANNEX 13
Page 9
considered only for cotton establishment (para 24). For each five-year
average yield corresponds a four-year average yield for custeio calculations.
The main components of operating costs are: labor (from 80 to 90% of custeio
per ha at various yield levels), insecticides, and transport costs. 1/

First-Year Cotton Establishment Cost
24.       For each five-year average yield, there is a corresponding first-
year average yield for calculation of the cost of establishing new cotton
areas. This is done on a per ha basis. Main components of cotton establish-
ment costs are: soil preparation, cultivation (including planting), harvesting,
seeds and insecticides, and transport. Labor costs at various yield levels
average from 85 to 90% of total establishment cost. From the first year of
participating in the project, first-year cotton represents 1/5 of the cotton
area (which itself represents 4/5 of the cultivated area). Although valid
at the aggregate level of numerous farmers, this assumption may not be
representative of the "without project" situation for each individual. How-
ever, it represents the objective to be reached by each farmer. As such, it
has important implications for the investment pattern (especially for plant-
ing of new areas) of each individual.
Subsistence Crops and Cattle
25.       The project would attach great importance to improved output of
foodcrops, mainly through the trials and extension components.  However,
because of the virtual absence of previous efforts on fooderops there is
insufficient data available to define expected potential yield increa&es;
hence foodcrops were excluded from the economic analysis. Subsistencc
crops generally occupy an area equivalent to the first year cotton area
since they are interplanted with cotton. Their labor requirement was taken
into account to estimate hired labor requirements for cotton.
1/ The apparently low figure for insecticide application is explained by
the fact that it represents an average application over a four-year
period. Applications in years 2 and 3 of the cotton cycle are in fact
relatively more important and are quite limited in year 5 when yields
would normally be low (main report, para 7.03). Transportation costs
are calculated on the basis of 0.05 Cr$/kg. (Delivery distances vary
considerably and 50 to 80 km are not uncommon--however, transporter
charges are consistently quoted at Cr$0.05/kg irrespective of distance.)



ANNEX 13
Page 10
26.       The sale of work animals purchased under the project was included
in the total benefit stream. At the aggregate level, 50% of farms in groups
B-I aad B-II (0 to 50 ha) display the absorptive capacity to support the
investment in work animals. Inevitably there may be individual limitations,
especially in the 0 to 10 ha range.
Methodology
27.       For each group, a "model" was prepared, not for an individual
farm, but for the group as a whole, without taking the phasing of farms
over the five-year project investment period into consideration. This
model was then disaggregated to show the hypothetical situation of an
individual farm which was used subsequently for phasing the various farm
sub-groups over the five-year period. Clearly the individual farm model
has little practical relevance in view of the numerous possible individual
situations which can only be accounted for on the average at the aggregate
level. 1/ The results (phased) for each group were then aggregated to pre-
sent the economic situation of the total 0 to 50 ha farms (12,000) on one
side, and the 50 to 200 ha farms (3,000) on the other. These results are
presented in Tables 2 and 3.
28.       In the financial and economic analyses, no differentiation was
made in the elaboration of the various models to take into account the
differences between sub-areas of the project area. These differences are
quite large (Annex 1) and would necessarily have to be accounted for during
project implementation. For the economic analysis however, it was not
possible to make further differentation because: (a) the number of relevant
farm models would have become unduly large, resulting in their being un-
manageable; (b) the exact allocation of project beneficiaries between sub-
areas would be premature before the results of phase I of the project are
known; and (c) the quantitative aspects of these differences is not con-
clusively documented at present. The assumptions presented above refer more
directly to the Serido area (sub-area 2), which is certainly the more
difficult and less favored sub-area of the project area. All the estimates
presented in this analysis would therefore represent relatively conservative
estimates for the other sub-areas.
Financial Analysis
29.       Because of the great diversity among farms, no financial analysis
of typical farms was undertaken on a systematic basis. This diversity is
illustrated by: (a) the land tenure system (sharecropping, renting, owner-
operating, squatting) under which dozens of critical assumptions would have
1/   However, several models of incividual farms under specific conditions
of investment (and not necessa,ily representative of all the groups)
were established for purposes of consistency and to give some ideas
of possible cash-flow and financial situations (para. 30).



ANNEX 13
Page 11
to be made for each farm class and each tenure arrangement; (b) the land
utilization system whereby critical differences arise from the possible
starting distribution pattern of the land cultivated under cotton (cotton
of first year, second year, etc.), 5ith serious implications for the invest-
ment pattern; and (c) the individual situations with rP3pect to the com-
petition between cattle and cash crops (cotton), where the information is
not sufficient to adopt a standard model, especially for farms of more than
50 ha.
30.       Financial analysis conducted on selected farm models under various
assumptions of investment pattern indicates that financial resuits of farmers
would be acceptable. As an example, an individual farm of group B-I (receiving
credit under the project but not expanding cotton area), with investments in
fencing for the whole cotton area, 1 work animal and corresponding equipment
(all investment in year 1) would have a financial rate of return of 13%.
With one-half the equivalent of this investment (fencing for part of the
area only), the rate of return would be increased to 21%. The net cash flow
position of the farmer in this instance would be acceptable, incremental net
cash income being about US$230 per year after 11 years (compared to present
level of about US$180). Under somewhat unfavorable assumptions concerning
credit obtainment and repayment, 1/ financial charges can be supported by
the farm. In fact, in most instances, farmers will be able to spread their
investment (especially fencing) over several years. Investment replacement
may also be spread over a few years. Hence, the situation analyzed in
this example can be considered rather unfavorable for the farmer. Most
farmers would be expected to be in relatively more favorable positions during
proj ect implementation.
31.       A general observation is that cash investments and operating costs
are kept low for each sub-group while the family labor component is relatively
large. Investment on smaller farms would seldom exceed Cr$7,000, or US$975,
and should not exceed the limit set under the project (50 times the reference
value, or about Cr$18,800) on farms from 50 to 200 ha.
Economic Analysis
32.       The economic analysis takes as the direct benefits of the project
only the incremental output of cotton, net of farming expenses. Incremental
output is derived from: (a) expansion in the area cultivated under cotton;
and (b) increases in yield of cotton per ha. Relatively small benefits are
also included from the sale of draft animals at regular intervals. Parts
of the costs of productive supports financed by the project are charged
against the benefit stream. Specific assumptions were made as follows:
1/   Seasonal credit every year, at 13% average interest rate:  cotton
establishment credit every year at 13% interest repayable in five
years; investment credit in year 1, repaid in 8 years including 3
years grace at average 7% interest.



ANNEX 13
Page 12
(a) Physical input prices. The prices of all variable inputs
were assumed equal to the local market prices. Special
atze-ntion was given to draft animals, seeds and chemicals
(pesticides and insecticides).
(b)  Labor.  All labor (family and hired labor) was priced at the
minimum salary level. Careful scrutiny led to the conclusion
that although the cost of hired labor was substantially above
the minim,=m off-farm wage level during harvest time (three
months) -Ad ccs:ewhat below for the rest of the year for on-
farm employment it would average at the minimum wage level
throughout the year. The same assumption would hold true
for family labori.  Thq,minimum salary level would be repre-
sentative of the oppor,unity wage level for off-farm employ-
ment.
(c)  Investment cost.  The casts of investment items are those
in effect at December 1974.,
(d)  Productive support cost.  The full cost of development of
the agricultural extensioi-'service was charged against the
benefit stream. Part of the costs of research and experi-
mentation (30%), project administration (60%) and monitor-
ing and evaluation (60%) weae also charged against the
benefit.stream, under the a$sumption that:  (a) large
benefits from research are expected to accrue to farmers
outside of the project area (in other parts of the State
and the Northeast); and (b) pioject administration and
control extends beyond the strictly productive aspects
of on-farm development. Finally, the expected cost of
operating the PROAGRO crop insurance scheme was added
to the investment cost for purposes of economic analysis.
(e)  Output prices.  The incremental output of cotton (equivalent
to 2% to 3% of Brazilian production of long-staple cotton),
was assumed not to affect the overall price relationship.
Price of cotton at the production level was assumed constant
throughout the development period, in accordance with inter-
national price projections. The price used already reflects
the substantial decrease in the international price for cotton
which took place at the end of 1974. The premium for long
staple cotton from the State (typical of the pro.ject area)
was kept unchanged. No shadow rate for foreign exchange was
used. The amount of the ICM (value added tax) has been added
to the producer price in calculating benefits.

35.       A sensitivity analysis,was c'arried out w7ieh- indicated that the
economic rate of return would be mainly affected by"- ('a) changes in the
total investment cost; (b) the rate 'or'iparticipation of' farmers; (c) the
price of cotton; and (d) the expected cotton yields. 'The latter is especially
important since.with increases of,25% in cotton yields, the rate of return
would increase to 26%.  Rather conserv,ative assumptions.of ethe yield
1fi ures (taken as expected values includi'ng the drought probability co-
',Mfiifdiit<;'i^0the Area,.and x onserv,tivqly restricting the potential impact
of 'research at a fixed yield levei.tthr,u h ' ahe 'developmpnt "period) lead
to somewhat optimistic expectations that the basic rate of return could
in fact be substantially improved.  Conversely, a decrease of 10% in the
price of cotton would reduce the economic,rate of return to 9%, and so
would a general increase of 10% of all costs charged to the project. An
increase of 10% in cotton prices would raise the rate of return to 20%.
An increase of the total investment costs (excluding on-farm investments)
by 10% would slightly decrease the rate-of return'to 14%. Finally, a 10%
increase of all farm costs (i.e., on-farm investment cotton) establishment
and production costs) would decrease the rate of return to 12%.
